Siman 603



Siman 604

<b>“The order of the Eve</b><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">All Jewish days, holidays, and festivals begin and conclude at sundown. The “Eve” of a holiday is considered any day which precedes the sundown marking the beginning of that holiday.</i> <b>of Yom Kippur</b><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Yom Kippur, or the Day of Atonement, falls on the tenth day of what, during our time, is the first month of the Hebrew calendar, the month of <i>Tishrei</i>. During the biblical period it was referred to as the seventh month. Yom Kippur follows by ten days the actual Jewish New Year, or Rosh HaShanah, which falls on the first day of the month of <i>Tishrei</i>. Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur are called the Days of Awe, <i>Yamim ha-Noraim</i>, because of their demand upon a religious Jew for special piety and self-reflection during this period of time devoted to repentance and self-improvement. These days which usually occur in the Fall of the year (September-October) and are referred to as the High Holydays. Yom Kippur is considered the most sacred day of the entire Jewish year and its commemoration involves a special, unique set of laws which are enumerated in the body of this thesis. For the biblical ordination of this holiday see: Leviticus 16 (all, but especially 16:29-34) and Numbers 29:7-11.</i> <b>(the Day of Atonement)” - Containing two paragraphs.</b><br>It is a commandment to eat on the Eve of Yom Kippur and to increase (one’s eating) at the meal.<br><i>Hagah</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Hagah, <span dir="rtl">הגה</span>, introduces the notes added to the text of Joseph Caro by Rabbi Moses b. Israel Isserles. Isserles is also known as “the Rema”, an acronym for <i>R</i>abbi <i>M</i>oses <i>I</i>sserles. He was a Polish rabbi, codifier and halakhic authority, who lived from 1525 or 1530 until 1572. He was born in Cracow under the name of Isserel-Lazarus which was later shortened to Isserles. He studied in Lublin at the yeshivah of Sholom Shachna. Isserles obtained such a fine reputation that he became known as the “Maimonides of Polish Jewry”. Isserles was in the middle of writing a code himself following the pattern of the four <i>Turim</i> by Jacob b. Asher which he called <i>Darkhei Moshe</i> which was to assemble the halakhic material of his time in a short, synoptic form so that a <i>dayyan</i>, a decision maker, could more easily find the material he needed to formulate a ruling on a particular issue. In the middle of his writing of the <i>Darkhei Moshe</i> he received a copy of the <i>Beit Yosef</i> of Joseph Caro which in essence had already accomplished this goal. But Isserles decided to complete his work operating a bit differently than did Caro. Isserles did not always agree with Caro’s selection of the “three pillars of halakhic decisions”, Alfasi, Maimonides, and Asher b. Jehiel to decide issues. He followed the principle which stated that laws should be decided according to later scholars. He also often agreed with Asher b. Jehiel and his son Jacob b. Asher even when they were in the minority, unlike Caro. Also Isserles realized that Caro ignored in his work Ashkenazi practices that were very much a part of his Polish and European community but were not included in Caro’s work in the world of Sephardi Jewry.<br>Isserles wrote other halakhic works, such as <i>Torat Ḥattat</i> which focused on mainly Jewish dietary laws. Finally Isserles received the Shulḥan Arukh by Joseph Caro. Like the earlier and more extensive <i>Beit Yosef</i>, it lacked many halakhic contributions and customs, <i>minhagim</i>, of Ashkenazi Jewry and was therefore not an adequate code for this segment of the Jewish world. Isserles decided to add notes (<i>hagah</i> or <i>hagahot</i>) to the body of the Shulḥan Arukh which has also been referred to as the “<i>mappah</i>” or “tablecloth” over the “Shulḥan Arukh” or “prepared table”. In his glosses, Isserles added his conclusions which he drew in his work <i>Darkhei Moshe</i> to the Shulḥan Arukh. In many cases he disagreed with Caro and he stated his disagreement, or he would cite an Ashkenazi custom not found in Caro’s work. He maintained the brief style employed by Caro, and he provided the existence of differing points of view by later scholars and Ashkenazi Jews which he felt were needed for a <i>dayyan</i> to be able to arrive at a correct decision. He often modified the views of the <i>meḥabber</i>, the author, as he referred to Caro, he explained, contradicted, added to and refined the structure.<br>Isserles put much emphasis on the custom, the <i>minhag</i>. He often gave it the same force as the <i>halakhah</i>. If there was no halakhah in existence, or in some cases where a <i>minhag</i> and a <i>halakhah</i> conflicted with each other, he decided according to the <i>minhag</i>, the custom which the people actually followed in their daily lives. If Isserles disagreed with a particular custom he would state so and he would urge against following such a custom.<br>Isserles was very lenient in cases of stress or in cases which would involve considerable financial loss. His leniency, which was seldom found in the works of others, was the subject of criticism by many of his contemporaries particularly Ḥayyim b. Bezalel who studied with him under Sholom Shachnan in Lublin. Even in view of the extensive criticism Isserles received, his notes to the Shulḥan Arukh became accepted and his rulings and customs were binding on Ashkenazi Jewry. The <i>mappah</i> of Isserles made the Shulḥan Arukh of Caro acceptable to Ashkenazi Jews and the authoritative code that it remains to this day.<br>The Shulḥan Arukh which was first published in Venice in 1565, was first published with the <i>mappah</i> of Isserles in Cracow in 1569-71 and it has been a part of the accepted text ever since.<br>Louis Ginzberg in Menachem Elon, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., “Codification of Law” v. 5. pp. 628-56;<br>Simḥa Katz, “Isserles, Moses ben Israel”, v. 9, pp.1081-85.</i> It is forbidden to fast on it (the Eve of Yom Kippur), even a dream fast<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">A fast was recommended to be observed as the result of an ominous dream (or a nightmare), <i>ta’anit ḥalom</i>, <span dir="rtl">תענית חלום</span>, to avert the evil consequences dreamt. In talmudic times and later it was believed that bad dreams could have pernicious effects. The fast was regarded of such urgency that the rabbis permitted it even on the Sabbath, but one was to fast on a weekday as well, as a repentance for having dishonored the Sabbath Joy through fasting. A fast as the result of a bad dream, though, is not to be observed on Yom Kippur Eve.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 6, p. 1196<br>(What amends shall he make (for having fasted on the Sabbath)? - He should observe an additional fast.)<br>B. Ta’an. 12b. (Soncino p.55)<br>Raba b. Meḥasia also said in the name of R. Ḥama b. Goria in Rab’s name: Fasting is as potent against a dream as fire against a tow. (Dreams were believed portents forshadowing the future, though, as seen here, the evil they foretold might be averted; cf. Ber. 55-58. B. B. 10a; Yoma 87b <i>et passim</i>). Said R. Ḥisda: Providing it is on that very day. R. Joseph added: and even on the Sabbath.<br>Shabbat 11a (Soncino p. 40, Shabbat I)<br>R. Eleazer also said in the name of R. Jose b. Zimra: If one keeps a fast on Sabbath (to overt the omen of a dream), a decree of seventy years standing is annulled; yet all the same he is punished for neglecting to make the Sabbath a delight. What is his remedy? R. Naḥman b. Isaac said: Let him keep another fast to atone for this one.<br>Ber. 31b. (Soncino pp. 194-95)</i> (due to a nightmare), (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>, is an acronym for <i>M</i>orenu <i>h</i>a-<i>R</i>av <i>J</i>acob ha-<i>L</i>evi. His real name was Jacob ben Moses Moellin. He lived from around 1360 until 1427. He was born in Mainz and became the foremost talmudist of his generation and the head of the Jewish communities of Germany, Austria, and Bohemia. He studied under his father and later went to Austria where he was ordained after studying under Meir ha-Levi and Sholom b. Isaac. After the death of his father, Maharil established a yeshivah from which came the greatest rabbis of Germany and Austria.<br>Moellin was famous and halakhic questions were asked of him throughout Europe. Many of his rulings became the foundation of the Jewish way of life for German Jewry. His decisions were characterized by the fact that he took into account the conditions of the time including the economic situation of a particular community. He would often decide to be strict in a case where a community had no rabbinic leadership. Moellin was concerned about leaders who did not possess the proper authority and the neglect of proper Torah and talmudic study that resulted from decisions arrived at using codified halakhic works instead of thorough original investigation. He placed a great deal of importance on charity and the honor of the poor.<br>Moellin was an accomplished <i>ḥazzan</i> and fought for the preservation of traditional melodies for the liturgy. His known works were two, <i>Minhagei Maharil</i>, (<i>Sefer Maharil</i>), which was first published in Sabionetta in 1556 and compiled by his student Zalman of Saint Goar, was a collection of halakhic statements, customs, and explanations which Zalman had heard from Moellin. It was these customs of Germany that Moses Isserles used so often in his glosses to the Shulḥan Arukh. The second work was a collection of responsa arranged by Eleazar b. Jacob and published in Venice in 1549.<br>Ephraim Kupfer, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 210-11.</i> And if one vows to fast on it, see above (in the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>), chapter  570, paragraph 2.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> chapter 570, Paragraph 2: “One who vows to fast on the Sabbath, on a festival, or on the Eve of Yom Kippur or on Ḥanukkah or Purim, the laws are the same as for one who vows to fast on so and so many days and these (holy) days happen to occur on them. If he expressed it using the word “vow”, the law is as if one has taken them upon himself, with the expression, a vow (it has the legal character of a vow). But if he expressed it merely with the (ordinary) expression of accepting a fast then the law is as if one has taken upon himself (the fast) with the expression of accepting a fast”. That is if one “vows” to fast on the Eve of Yom Kippur then he must if he uses the words “vow” and “fast”. If he only says I am going to fast, without using the word “vow”, it is not serious and he can postpone it. (Acceptance of a fast by a) vow is more serious than accepting a fast without it.”</i>
They do not “prostrate themselves” (i.e., they do not say the <i>taḥanun</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>taḥanun</i>, <span dir="ltr">תחנון</span>, prayer is the name of a prayer which is a confession of sins and a petition for grace. It is normally part of the daily Morning, <i>Shaḥarit</i> (see footnote 17), and Afternoon, <i>Minḥah</i> (see footnote 40), Services. It is recited after the reader’s repetition of the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). The <i>taḥanun</i> begins silently with a selection from II Samuel 24:14 which was uttered by King David after he was rebuked by the prophet Gad for sinning by numbering the people: “let us fall, I pray thee, into the hand of the Lord, for his mercies are many, but let me not fall into the hands of man.” The prayer is referred to literally as the “prostration prayer” because the Bible mentions the fact that one prostrates oneself during petitions (Deuteronomy 9:18; Joshua 7:6), and the prayer <i>taḥanun</i> was therefore customarily recited in the prostrate position. Today the prayer is recited while one is seated with one’s head bowed into the bend of one’s arm when a Torah Scroll is present to indicate the sanctity of the location. The Sephardi ritual begins the <i>taḥanun</i> with a silent confession of sins, <i>viddui</i> (see footnote 39) followed by the verse from II Samuel 24:14. The central part of the prayer for the Ashkenazim is Psalm 6 and for the Sephardim the penitential psalm, Psalm 25. In addition to this there are penitential prayers of <i>piyyutim</i>, or liturgical poems (see footnote 149). The <i>taḥanun</i> prayer is omitted on the Sabbath, festivals, semiholidays, New Moons, and from the <i>Minḥah</i> Service preceding these special days, during the month of <i>Nisan</i> and on the Ninth of <i>Av</i>. The <i>taḥanun</i> is also omitted at a circumcision in a synagogue, when a bridegroom attends the service during the first seven days following his wedding, and at the prayers held at the homes of mourners since the theme “I have sinned before thee” is deemed inappropriate.<br>Meir Ydit, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 15, p. 702.</i> prayers) on the Eve of Yom Kippur.<br><i>Hagah</i>: They also do not say “<span dir="ltr">למנצח</span><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="ltr">למנצח</span>, “For the Chief Musician, a Psalm of David” is Psalm 19, and it is recited normally during the <i>Shaḥarit</i>, Morning prayers on the Sabbath and festivals (see footnote 17). The theme of the prayer is the double revelation of God in nature, in religion and in Torah.<br>Dr. Joseph H. Hertz, <i>The Authorized Daily Prayer Book</i>, New York, Bloch Publishing Company, 1957, p. 60</i>” and “<span dir="ltr">מזמור לתודה</span><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><span dir="ltr">מזמור לתודה</span>, “A Psalm of Thanksgiving” is Psalm 100. The theme of the psalm is to let all the world join in the worship of God. The psalm is normally recited during the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Morning prayers on the weekday (see footnote 17). In addition to the day before Yom Kippur, it is also omitted on Sabbaths, festivals, the day before Passover, and on the intermediate days of Passover.</i>”, (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>, “customs” when used by Isserles denotes an anonymous collection of Ashkenazi customs in his glosses that were not part of the customs practiced by the Sephardi Jewish community. Additions such as these gave Ashkenazi Jewry the possibility of accepting the Shulḥan Arukh as a binding and authoritative code of Jewish law in that the additions of Isserles enabled the total Shulḥan Arukh to be a work common to all of world Jewry. There was no one book from which Isserles drew his <i>minhagim</i>, his customs, but rather he drew them from various <i>minhagim</i> books available to him and from customs he was familiar with in daily life. Many of the <i>minhagim</i> from which Isserles drew were contained in a book entitled <i>Minahage Maharil</i> or <i>Sefer Maharil</i> published in 1556 in Sabionetta which was compiled by Zalman of Saint Goar. It contained halakhic statements, explanations, and customs that Zalman heard from his great teacher the Maharil, Jacob ben Moses Moellin (see footnote 8).</i> Also they do not say before dawn many “<i>seliḥot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Seliḥot</i>, <span dir="ltr">סליחות</span>, means “prayers of forgiveness”. When this word is used in its singular form <i>seliḥah</i>, <span dir="ltr">סליחה</span>, it means “forgiveness” and it usually refers to a liturgical poem, <i>piyyut</i> (see footnote 149), who’s subject is a plea for forgiveness. When the term is used in the plural, <i>seliḥot</i>, it refers to a special order of service which consists of non-statuatory additional prayers which are recited on all fast days, on occasions of special intercession, and during the Penitential season which begins with a special <i>Seliḥot</i> Service usually held at midnight on the Saturday night immediately preceding Rosh HaShanah and concludes with Yom Kippur. The Mishna (Ta’an 2:1-4) gives the order of the service for public fasts which were often proclaimed during periods of drought and it provided for six additional blessings inserted into the daily <i>Amidah</i> after the sixth blessing which is a prayer for forgiveness of sins (see footnote 43).<br>The first mention of any kind of definite order of <i>Seliḥot</i> is found in <i>Tanna de-Vei</i>, Eliyahu Zuta (23 end). The order of <i>Sheliḥot</i> was not found until the ninth century in the <i>Seder</i> of R. Amram which included “May He Who answered” and the biblical verse “Thee Lord, the Lord God, merciful and gracious longsuffering and abundent in goodness and truth” (Exodus 34:6) along with others.<br>Over the centuries many more <i>piyyutim</i> with the theme of forgiveness have been added to the <i>Seliḥot</i> prayers. Because of the many liturgical poems added at various times, many Jewish communities have had their own distinct rites evolve. It became a Palestinian custom not to say the <i>Seliḥot</i> prayers during the <i>Amidah</i> but after it, and this became the custom generally accepted (Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 566:4).<br><i>Seliḥot</i> prayers were originally confined to fast days. God was just, and it was felt that if one confessed one’s sins and prayed for forgiveness, calamities which were the result of Israel’s sins, would be averted. In modern times the <i>Seliḥot</i> prayers were first recited in conjunction with the six fast days prior to Rosh HaShanah and then they were extended to include the ten days of Penitence including Yom Kippur but not Rosh HaShanah in the Ashkenazi ritual. Among Sephardi Jews it was a custom to recite <i>Seliḥot</i> for forty days from Rosh Ḥodesh Elul (the New Moon of the month of Elul, the last month of the Hebrew year preceding the New Year beginning with Rosh HaShanah on the New Moon of Tishrei) until Yom Kippur. The Ashkenazi custom was evolved in our day to recite <i>Seliḥot</i> from midnight on the Saturday night prior to Rosh HaShanah or the week before that should Rosh HaShanah fall on a Monday or a Tuesday. (Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 581 with the Isserles). Only on the first night is <i>Seliḥot</i> recited at midnight. On all other days it is recited in the Morning Service.<br>Present day customs also allow individuals to recite <i>Seliḥot</i> on semi-official voluntary fasts.<br>Louis Isaac Rabinowitz, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 1133-34.</i>” (prayers of forgiveness), but there are places where it is customary to increase <i>seliḥot</i>. All (this should be done) according to the (local) custom. But concerning the matter of the saying of “<span dir="ltr">אבינו מלכנו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avinu Malkhenu</i>, <span dir="rtl">אבינו מלכנו</span> “Our Father our King” is a prayer recited during the ten days of Penitence between Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur immediately after the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). The prayer is not said during Friday <i>Minḥah</i> Afternoon, on the Sabbath, or on the day before Yom Kippur. If the day before Yom Kippur is a Friday then the prayer is recited during the Morning, <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service (see footnote 17). Each of the forty-four invocations of the prayer begins “<i>Avinu Malkhenu</i>”, “Our Father our King”. This litancy has the elements of a confessional and petitionary prayer. The prayer is quite old and the Talmud attributes some of the lines to Rabbi Akiba when they were spoken on a fast day due to a drought. The prayer was expanded over the centuries to include prayers for life, pardon, and the needs and trials of human existence. Toward the end are references to the terrible massacres during the Black Death in the fourteenth century where much of German Jewry was annihilated.<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., pp. 161-67.</i>”, (Our Father, our King”), on the Eve of Yom Kippur, there is a disagreement among the <i>aḥronim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Aḥronim</i>, <span dir="ltr">אחרונים</span>, the later scholars or authorities. This term is used to designate the later rabbinic authorities as opposed to the <i>rishonim</i> or the earlier authorities. There is no clear line of demarkation separating the <i>aḥronim</i> from the <i>rishonim</i>. Some scholars date the <i>aḥronim</i> as early as the tosafists in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries while others start the period in the beginning of the fourteenth century where the appearance of the <i>Sha’arei Dura</i> of Isaac ben Meir Dueren. Most scholars agree that the period of the <i>rishonim</i> ends with the death of Israel Isserlein in 1460 (see footnote 96) and that the <i>aḥronim</i> begin with the Shulḥan Arukh including the glosses of Isserles (1525-30-1572). The later authorities are therefore thought of as the collection of all the predecessors of the Jewish world of sages in both the Sephardi and Ashkenazi communities included by both Caro and Isserles. When Isserles then referred to the <i>aḥronim</i>, he referred to his contemporaries and those authorities immediately preceding him.<br>Some of the greatest <i>aḥronim</i> were produced in Poland during the end of the sixteenth century where the study of the Torah and Talmud became quite intensive.<br><i>Aḥronim</i> is a term now used to refer to all rabbinic authorities after 1500 who decide <i>halakhah</i> even to this day.<br>Yehoshua Horowitz, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 1973 Year Book, pp.153-57.</i>, (later scholars). The custom in my city is not to say it unless Yom Kippur falls on Shabbat; since we do not say on the Shabbat “<span dir="rtl">אבינו מלכנו</span>”, therefore we say it in the <i>Shaḥarit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shaḥarit</i>, <span dir="ltr">שחרית</span> Morning Service, or actually the dawn prayer. The <i>Shaḥarit</i> prayers are the most elaborate of the three daily prayer services (the Shaḥarit, Morning; <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon; and <i>Aravit</i>, Evening). It has been traditionally attributed to Abraham. “And Abraham got up early in the morning to the place where he stood before the Lord,” (Genesis 19:27). After the destruction of the Temple the rabbis made the recitation of the <i>Shaḥarit</i> prayer obligatory to replace the daily morning sacrafice called the <i>Tamid</i> which had been performed in the Temple (Ber. 26b).<br>There are basically eight parts to the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service and they are the following: (1) The Morning Benedictions or <i>Birkhot ha-Shaḥar</i>, <span dir="ltr">ברכות השחר</span>, these are preliminaries to the Morning Service and they consist of hymns, blessings, and meditations, the themes of which are generally concerned with the change of night to day and of sleep to wakefulness. There are also readings from the Torah and rabbinical writings to get the soul ready for worship. Originally this part of the service was to be read at home before coming to the synagogue for communal prayer.<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., p. 4.<br>(2) The Psalms and Passages of Song or <i>Pesukai de-Zimra</i>, <span dir="ltr">פסוקי דזמרא</span>. This section of psalms and anthems is intended to serve as the transference from private worship in the first section to public prayer. The tradition says that pious men during the days of the Second Temple would completely read the entire Book of Psalms everyday. This was an ideal that men with necessary work could never emulate, thus it became the custom to read at least six psalms in the morning, Psalms 145-150. There have been additions to this nucleus. Prior to the above mentioned psalms, are recited other psalm-like selections, I Chronicles 16:8-36, a collection of Biblical verses, Psalm 100, and more Biblical verses. Psalms 145-150 are followed by responses of adoration (“doxologies”), the benediction of David, I Chronicles 29:10-13; the prayer of Nehemiah 9:6-11; and the Song of Moses, Exodus 14:30 - 15:18. Therefore this section contains no formal prayers but only psalm-like material. It was brought into the Morning Service by Rabbi Meir of Rothenburg (1230-1293).<br><i>ibid</i>., pp. 50-1.<br>(3) Reading of the <i>Shema</i>, <span dir="rtl">קריאת שמע</span>, and its benedictions. This is truely the central part of the Morning (and the Evening) Service. It is Israel’s confession of faith in the One God. The worshipper, by reciting it, proclaims his allegiance to the Kingdom of Heaven and his submission to God’s commandments. The <i>Shema</i> is preceded by two blessings; (1) The <i>Yotzer</i>, <span dir="ltr">יוצר</span>, Prayer which is a prayer of thanksgiving for the creation of physical life, for the actual light of day and for God’s renewal of creation which is demonstrated by the fact that the sun, the light, returns; and (2) The <i>Ahavah Rabbah</i>, <span dir="rtl">אהבה רבה</span>, a gracious prayer of thanksgiving, gives thanks to God for the light of Torah which he gave to Israel and its moral teachings.<br>The <i>Shema</i> in the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service is followed by two prayers; (1) the <i>Emet Veyaẓiv</i>, <span dir="ltr">אמת ויציב</span>, which means (True and Firm). The prayer confirms the faith in the declarations that were made in the <i>Shema</i>. (2) and the prayer <i>Go’el Israel</i>, <span dir="ltr">גואל ישראל</span>, the Redeemer of Israel which praises God.<br><i>ibid</i>., p. 108.<br>The <i>Shema</i> itself consists of three Torah sections, Deuteronomy 6:4-8; 11:13-22; and Numbers 15:37-42. It is a proclamation of God’s Unity and Oneness, Israel’s total loyalty to God and his commandments, the belief in Divine Justice, the rememberance of the liberation from Egypt, and the choosing of Israel. Together these form the foundation of Jewish faith.<br><i>ibid</i>., p. 116.<br>(4) The Amidah, <span dir="ltr">עמידה</span>, is the most central and important part of the service next to the <i>Shema</i>. It is also referred to as the <i>Tefillah</i>, <span dir="ltr">התפילה</span>, “The Prayer” and the <i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>. <span dir="ltr">שמונה עשרה</span>, or eighteen benedictions because it originally contained eighteen separate benedictions but which has come down to us as a prayer consisting of nineteen benedictions during the regular daily worship service. The Prayer is recited three times a day silently while standing, therefore the name <i>Amidah</i> which means “standing” became associated with it. The benedictions contain expressions of praise, thanksgiving, confession, and petition to God.<br>The <i>Amidah</i> contains three basic parts. The first part consists of three opening benedictions which are praises. They glorify God, His everlasting love and His infinite holiness. The second part of the weekday <i>Amidah</i> contains thirteen blessings (which were originally only twelve) which are petitions for the individual as well as for the nation. This middle section of the <i>Amidah</i> is different on the Sabbath and festivals. On the Sabbath there is only one benediction in the middle of the <i>Amidah</i> (therefore only a total of seven benedictions) and it concerns the special nature of the day. A <i>Kedusha</i> or a sanctification of the name of God, is included in this section of the Sabbath morning <i>Amidah</i>. On the festivals this is also the case with a special middle benediction which concerns the unique nature of the holiday. This is true of all festivals except Rosh HaShanah which contains three central blessings in its <i>Musaf Amidah</i> (see footnote 166), thus making a total of nine benedictions.<br>The third part of the <i>Amidah</i> consists of three closing benedictions whose theme is one of thanksgiving. The first three and last three benedictions never change regardless of which service the <i>Amidah</i> is found in or on what day it is recited. The prayer is first recited privately in silence and it is then repeated out loud by the reader (except for the Evening Service, see footnote 144) for the benefit of those who are unable to say it themselves (see also footnote 42).<br><i>ibid</i>., pp. 130-31.<br>(5) The <i>Taḥanun</i>, <span dir="ltr">תחנון</span>, prayers of confession; see footnote 10.<br>(6) The Torah reading on the mornings that it is required, namely on the Sabbath, festivals, Mondays, Thursdays, New Moons, the intermediate days of Passover and Succot, Purim and public fast days. Normally, that is on most Sabbaths, Mondays and Thursdays the Torah is read according to its regular weekly division of fifty-four (on a leap year and fifty on a non-leap year) portions. On special Sabbaths, festivals, and other occasions specially designated portions are read which have a relationship with that particular occasion.<br>(7) <i>Ashrei</i>. <span dir="ltr">אשרי</span>, “Happy are they” is basically Psalm 20 and a collection of Biblical quotations. It is in essence a prophetic lesson and a second sanctification.<br>(8) <i>Aleinu le-Shabbe’aḥ</i>, <span dir="ltr">עלינו לשבח</span>, “It is our duty to praise the Lord” is recited at the conclusion of the Morning Service. It is usually preceded by a full <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177) read by the reader and it is followed by a Mourner’s Kaddish. The <i>Aleinu</i> or adoration prayer since the fourteenth century has been a proclaimation of God as the Supreme King of the Universe and the God of a United Humanity. In the first part Israel aknowledges that it has been selected for service to God and the second half proclaims Israel’s faith and hope that all idolatry will disappear and that all activity will be turned to God. All will be united under the Kingship of God.<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit.</i>, p. 208.<br>The <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service remains fairly constant in the prayers recited every morning except for the <i>Amidah</i> which changes according to the occasion as described above. There are also additions to the <i>pesukei de-zimra</i> (2) on Sabbaths and festivals, and on festivals and New Moons the <i>Hallel</i> (special psalms of praise and thanksgiving which consist of Psalms 113-118 with various Psalms omitted on certain festivals) is added. Special <i>piyyutim</i> (see footnote 149) are also inserted on certain Sabbaths and festivals during the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service.<br>The Mishna and Talmud discuss when the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service should be recited. The <i>Shema</i> must be recited from the period of time which begins with daybreak and ends after a quarter of the day has passed (Ber. 1:2; Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 58:1). One must recite the <i>Amidah</i> during the hours encompassed by sunrise and a third of the day (Ber. 4:1; Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 89:1). If by chance the recitation of the daily prayers was delayed they could be recited until midday (Ber. 4:1; Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> 89:1). If the <i>Shaḥarit Amidah</i> is not recited, an extra Amidah is added during the <i>Minḥaḥ</i>, Afternoon Service.<br>During the daily weekday <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service the <i>tallit</i>, prayer shawl, and the <i>tefillin</i>, phylacteries, are worn. On the Sabbath and festivals only the <i>tallit</i> is worn. One wears neither <i>tallit</i> nor <i>tefillin</i> on the Ninth day of Av for the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Serivce but wears them instead for the <i>Minḥah</i> Service. One must not interupt one’s prayer by speaking from the prayer “<i>Barukh she-Amar</i>” which precedes the <i>pesukei de-zimra</i> until after the <i>Amidah</i>.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 1257-58.</i> (Morning Prayers) on the Eve of Yom Kippur.

Siman 605

<b>“The custom of “<i>kapprot</i></b><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kapparot,</i> <span dir="ltr">כפרות</span>, which is plural for <i>Kapparah</i>, meaning expiation. This is a custom where the sins of a person are symbolically transferred to a fowl. This practice is mostly done on the day before Yom Kippur and in some congregations it is also performed on the day before Rosh HaShanah or on Hoshana Rabba. During the ceremony Psalms 107;10,14,17-21 and Job 33:23-24 are recited. Following this a cock is taken for a male and a hen for a female, the fowl is swung around one’s head three times while the person says: “This is my substitute, my vicarious offering, my atonement; this cock (or hen) shall meet death, but I shall find a long and pleasent life of peace”. It is thought by some (erroneously) that the fowl assumes the punishment for sins that the person would normally receive. Often the fowl is donated to the poor minus the intestines which are given to the birds. Some people substitute the monetary value of the fowl and donate that to the poor.<br>The custom is not a talmudic one. It first appears in the writings of the <i>geonim</i> (see footnote 19) in the ninth century. The connection between a man and a cock is that both can be referred to as a <i>gever</i>, so a <i>gever</i> (man) can transfer his sins on to another <i>gever</i> (cock). Another reason for the use of a cock or a hen was due to the fact that after the destruction of the Temple, no animal used in the sacrificial rite could be used for a similar purpose outside the Temple. The cock and the hen had no Temple cultic connection. Caro, along with R. Solomon b. Abraham Adret and Naḥmanides opposed this custom but Isserles included it because of its practice in the Ashkenazi community where it had taken on mystic interpretations from the Kabbalists.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, pp. 756-57.<br>The basic Hebrew sources and comments on <i>Kapparot</i> are the following:<br><i>Tur</i>, <span dir="rtl">טור</span>, (see footnote 23) 605 - There are places where it is customary to slaughter a rooster as atonement (for <i>Kapparah</i>). And thus it is (related) in geonic (see footnote 19) responsa: “You asked; we customarily slaughter a rooster on the Eve of Yom Kippur, and we do not know the reason for this custom. If it is an “exchange” (substitute) for a sacrifice (if it symbolizes a sacrifice), then what is the difference between a rooster and cattle or a beast, but certainly there is a problem. However, there are two reasons: (1) a rooster is found more commonly in a household than any cattle, beast, or fowl; (2) There are places of wealthy people who substitute rams; and the main horned animal (for the Yom Kippur ceremony) is analogous to the ram of our father Isaac (which was substituted for him (Isaac) as a sacrifice), therefore the matter (of using a rooster) is not established (determined).”<br>In addition we have heard from the early scholars that even though the price of a cattle is higher than that of a rooster, nevertheless a rooster is chosen because its designation is <i>gever</i> (man, rooster) as is said in (<i>Yoma</i> 20a): What is the meaning of <i>Kara Gavra</i>, R. Sila says the meaning is that the rooster crows and since its designation is <i>gever</i> and the exchange is of one <i>gever</i> (rooster) for another <i>gever</i> (man), therefore it (using a rooster) is effective and superior (to any other animal). And this is the custom here, the congregational reader holds the rooster and lays his hand on its head (in the manner in which a sacrifice was performed in the Temple) and then he takes it (the rooster) and lays it upon the head of the one seeking atonement and says (the verses in the Prayer Book used in this service (<i>Oẓar ha-Tefillot</i>, volume 2, pp. 1090-91)). “This (<i>gever</i>, rooster) for this (<i>gever</i>, man), this substitutes this, this is in exchange for this,” and he (the reader) returns it upon him once (swings it around his (the one seeking atonement’s) head one time) and says (psalms 107:10,14,17,19-21) “Such as sit in darkness and in the shadow of death, being bound in affliction and iron…He brought them out of darkness and and the shadow of death, and brake their bands in sunder…Fools because of their transgression, and because of their iniquities are afflicted… Then they cry unto the Lord in their trouble and He saves them out of their distress. He sent his word, and healed them, and delivered them from their destructions. Oh, that men would praise the Lord for His goodness, and for His wonderful works to the children of men.” “Thou shalt give life for life”, (Exodus 21:23). And he (the reader) does this according to this order three times, and after this he lays his hand on the head of the rooster in the way of the <i>Semikhah</i>, putting the hands on it (the animal’s head before slaughtering) and slaughter it immediately after the <i>Semikhah</i>, and they customarily give it (the slaughtered rooster) to the poor so there would be atonement for his own soul (for the one who gives it).<br>That it was customary to throw the insides of it (the rooster) on the roof in order to give them to the birds, there is some proof (indication, justification) for this from the Talmud tractate <i>Ḥulin</i> (95a) 110a: “Rami b. Tamri… once happened to be in Sura on the Eve of Yom Kippur. When the townspeople took all the udders (<i>Tur</i>: Liver and Kidneys) (of the animals) and threw them away, he immediately went and collected them and ate them”.<br>In the Prayer Book <i>Oẓar ha-Tefillot</i>, <span dir="ltr">אוצר התפילות</span> Published by Sefer, New York, 1946, page 1089, there is an extensive, detailed note with Rashi’s (description of) the custom of the <i>Kapparot</i> ceremony on the Eve of Yom Kippur. Rashi already described this custom meaning it was a common practice during his time.<br>The <i>Kapparot</i> ceremony is not mentioned in the Talmud, only in Rashi. It is mentioned in <i>Maḥzor Vitry</i> by R. Shimḥa bar Samuel, a disciple of Rashi who quotes the ceremony from the <i>Pesikta</i>, <span dir="ltr">פסקתא</span>, but our text of the <i>Pesikta</i> does not have it. The first mention of the <i>Kapparot</i> ceremony is by the geonic Sheshna in <i>Sha’are Teshuvot</i>, Responsum 299, and by Natronai Gaon in <i>Bet Nekhot ha-Halakhot</i> 50a. paragraphs 15 and 16.</i>” <b>(atonement ceremony) on the Eve of Yom Kippur” - Containing one paragraph.</b><br>The custom regarding the “<i>kapparah</i>” (atonement ceremony) on the Eve of Yom Kippur by slaughtering a rooster for each male and to say biblical verses over it should be stopped.<br><i>Hagah</i>: There are some <i>geonim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Geonim</i>, (singular <i>gaon</i>) is the formal title for the heads of the academies in Sura and Pumbedita in Babylonia from around the end of the sixth century until the middle of the eleventh century. The <i>geonim</i> were the highest Jewish authorities. In the tenth and eleventh centuries heads of academies in Ereẓ Israel were also called <i>geonim</i>. The geonic period proper ended in 1040. The heads of the academies in Baghdad, Damascus, and Egypt were also called geonim and later it became a term applied as an honor to any rabbi who had great toraitic knowledge.<br>It cannot exactly be determined when the term <i>gaon</i> came into use. Prior to its use generally the term <i>rosh yeshivah shel golah</i>, the head of the academy of the Diaspora, was used. The heads of these academies were appointed by the exilarchs, the political leaders of the Jewish people in exile. People rose to the office of <i>gaon</i> often through an hierarchy of offices, thus not always did the most learned reach the position. Often the office was used for political purposes by the exilarch. An assistant to the <i>gaon</i> was referred to as the <i>av bet din</i>. The position of <i>gaon</i> usually fell upon an elderly man who could only serve for a rather short period of time, and therefore did not always make a great impression.<br>Babylonia was the center of world Jewry and the Jews looked to the <i>geonim</i> as a source of instruction for Jews and also as the deciders of Jewish law. The <i>geonim</i> formed many new halakhic decisions which evolved in the Diaspora. They formulated <i>takkanot</i> or ordinances which altered Jewish law according to the new situations. The <i>geonim</i> and their academies were supported by taxes levied against the people for this purpose.<br>The halakhic decision of a <i>gaon</i> generally had the effect of law and it was binding. Due to the new situation which the Diaspora provided many halakhic decisions of the <i>geonim</i> were based on <i>minhagim</i>, or customs, that took on the force of a law (the principle under which Isserles operated). Their responsa to halakhic questions were followed as law. The goal of the <i>gaon</i> in the Diaspora was mainly to interpret the Babylonian Talmud for the Babylonian Jews and to lessen their emotional attachment to Ereẓ Israel. This created much political animosity between the Jews of Babylonia and those left in Ereẓ Israel. Since the major scholars of the time where exiled to Babylonia, the center of Jewish leadership was in the hands of the <i>gaon</i> for a long period of time, more than four centuries.<br>The goanate, though, did lose its power even though some of the greatest geonim were among the later ones. From the late ninth century onward, most of the <i>geonim</i> did not live in the cities of the academies, Sura and Pumbedita, they lived in Baghdad along with the exilarch. Competition between the two academies and political disagreements over the appointment of <i>geonim</i> lessened their effectiveness as did the rise of new academies and their leaders. Scholars stopped sending them halakhic questions preferring their own ability to arrive at a decision. Jewish communities outside of Babylonia began taking on independence from the original center of the Diaspora. As the caliphate in Baghdad weakened, financial support from other Jewish communities ceased for the Babylonian academies. The gaonate ended as an institution around 1040.<br>The religious leaders of Baghdad and later Ereẓ Israel took on the title of <i>gaon</i> after the fall of the gaonate in Babylonia. The position of the <i>gaon</i> in Ereẓ Israel was one passed on by heredity. The geonim in Ereẓ Israel had to manage all Jewish affairs in addition to heading the academy. They ordained rabbis, appointed judges, and managed the economic affairs of the Jews. The title of <i>gaon</i> finally spread to Damascus and Egypt where it eventually died out in the twelfth and thirteenth centuries.<br>Simha Assaf and Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 7, pp. 315-24.</i> who listed this custom (as a proper custom) and likewise many of the <i>aḥronim</i> listed it thusly. And likewise it is the custom in all these lands,<sup class="footnote-marker">19a</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a comment to the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, found in the <i>Turei Zahav Magen David</i>, or for short the <i>Taz</i> by David ben Samuel ha-Levi who lived from 1586 until 1667. David ben Samuel was born in the Ukraine. He married the daughter of Joel Sirkes, the author of <i>Bayit Ḥadash</i> (see footnote 20b.) in whose yeshivah he studied. The commentary <i>Turei Zahav</i> is found to all four parts of the Shulḥan Arukh. It is not a running commentary, but includes discussions of various points found in the <i>Tur</i> of Jacob Asher (see footnote 23) and in the Talmud and its commentators. The <i>Turei Zahav</i> is found in the inside margin of the <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> section of the Shulḥan Arukh opposite the commentary of Abraham Abel Gumbiner called <i>Magen Avraham</i> (see footnote 33), which is a running commentary but which has a closer relationship to the material found in the <i>Tur</i> than it does to the Shulḥan Arukh;<br>Shmuel Ashkenazi, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 5, pp. 1354-55.<br>605:1 - “And so is the custom in all these lands”: In the <i>Tur</i>, <span dir="rtl">טור</span>, (see footnote 23 and the translation to this section of the <i>Tur</i> found in footnote 18) are written the verses that are recited and the following verse is mentioned there (in addition to the verses found in Psalms 107:10,14,17,-21), “Thou shalt give life for life” (Exodus 21:23).</i>. and it is not to be changed because it is a custom of the pious. It is customary to take a rooster for each male, and for each female (to) take a hen, (<span dir="ltr">בית יוסף בשם תשב״ץ</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Beit Yosef</i>, in the name of <i>Tashbaẓ</i>, <span dir="ltr">בית יוסף בשם תשב״ץ</span>.<br>The <i>Beit Yosef</i>, <span dir="ltr">בית יוסף</span>, the companion work written by Joseph Caro (1488-1575) to the Shulḥan Arukh. Caro began writing the <i>Beit Yosef</i> in 1522 and completed it in 1542 in Safed. It was first published in 1555. The <i>Beit Yosef</i> followed the format of the four <i>Turim</i> established by Jacob b. Asher in his book by that title. Caro included in the <i>Beit Yosef</i> all the halakhic material in use during his time which included the talmudic sources and also the post-talmudic scholars which he used to reach an halakhic decision. Caro linked himself to the <i>Turim</i> and did not repeat halakhic material already cited in the <i>Turim</i>. Caro employed the method of determining <i>halakhah</i> by following the majority decision of his “three pillars of halakhic decisions”, Alfasi, Maimonides, and Asher b. Jehiel. If there was no majority decision by these three he consulted and decided according to the majority of another five scholars, Naḥmanides, Solomon b. Abraham Adret, Nissim Gerondi, Mordecai b. Hillel, and Moses b. Jacob of Coucy. If none of these men dealt with a particular law he decided according to the opinions of the majority of “famous” scholars. Caro consulted thirty-two works in his research. In this extensive work Caro created a book of Jewish law. He wanted to create then a companion book that would truly be a code. Therefore he wrote the Shulḥan Arukh which basically listed only the decisions that Caro reached in the <i>Beit Yosef</i> and not all the arguments and sources. The Shulḥan Arukh merely stated what the <i>halakhah</i> was and how it was practiced. (For a more extensive explanation of the <i>Beit Yosef</i> and how it fits into the broad scope of code literature, see the introduction to this work.)<br><i>Tashbaẓ</i>, <span dir="rtl">תשב״ץ</span>, is an abbreviation for <i>Teshuvot Shimon ben Ẓemaḥ</i>, which is a collection of responsa in three parts by Simeon ben Ẓemaḥ Duran who was also known as the “<i>Rashbaẓ</i>”, an acronym for <i>R</i>abbi <i>S</i>himon <i>ben Ẓ</i>emaḥ, who lived from 1361 until 1444. The <i>Rashbaẓ</i> was born in Spain and later moved to North Africa and settled in Algiers where he became a <i>dayyan</i>, a rabbinic judge and the Chief Rabbi of Algiers in 1408. The <i>Rashbaẓ</i> was against formulating strict decisions, <i>ḥumrot</i>, which did not have talmudical basis. He argued that one could be stringent with oneself but had to be lenient with others.<br>In his decisions he would exhaust all existing sources and discuss all opinions. His decisions became the authoritative laws of North African Jewry. His <i>takkanot</i>, his changes in the law, were followed for many centuries. He was often quoted by later halakhic scholars and was well respected. His writings were extensive and they included philosophical and liturgical works as well as halakhic literature.<br>Hirsch Jacob Zimmels, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 6, pp. 302-06.</i> For a pregnant woman to take two roosters<sup class="footnote-marker">20a</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a comment found in the commentary to the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim Magen Avraham</i> by Abraham Abele Gumbiner (see footnote 33): 605:2 - “Two roosters”: That is to say a rooster and a hen. Even if the embryo is female, one hen is enough for a mother and for her daughter, because two persons (of the same sex) are allowed to take one <i>Kapparah</i> (see footnote 18), (<i>Levush</i>, <span dir="rtl">לבוש</span>; which is a code whose entire name is <i>Levush Malkhut</i>, The work presents the laws found in the <i>Beit Yosef</i> of Joseph Caro (see footnote 20) in an abbreviated form. The Shulḥan Arukh appeared which was basically a digest of the larger <i>Beit Yosef</i>, but the <i>Levush</i> was completed so as to include the laws observed by the Ashkenazi Jews of Behemia. Mordecai ben Abraham Jaffe (1535-1612) wrote the <i>Levush</i>. He was born in Prague and studied under Solomon Luria and Moses Isserles. While he was writing the <i>Levush</i> he learned that Isserles was attempting the same goal he was to include the Ashkenazi laws in the Shulḥan Arukh so he put aside his work. When Jaffe received the glosses of his teacher Isserles he thought it was too brief and therefore he set about completing his <i>Levush</i>. The (custom is to) chose white roosters<sup class="footnote-marker">20b</sup><i class="footnote">A commentary by <i>Magen Avraham</i> (see footnote 33): 605:3 - “White roosters”: Anyhow, one should not try to get only white (chickens), which is similar to the practice of the Amorites (meaning, idol worshippers). If there happens to be a white one available he should buy it, (<i>Bayit Hadash</i>, <span dir="ltr">בית חדש</span>; which is a critical and comprehensive commentary on the <i>Arba’ah Turim</i> of Jacob b. (Notice the difference between this comment and the one translated above by <i>Magen Avraham</i> on the same subject.)<br>The following is a comment by the Wilna Gaon, which supplies the sources for references made in the Shulḥan Arukh. It was customary to give the atonement chickens to the poor or to redeem them<sup class="footnote-marker">20c</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a comment by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33): 605:4 - “Or to redeem (replace) them (the chickens) (with charity money…”: This is better so as not to embarrass the poor (<i>Shenai Luḥot ha-Berit</i>, <span dir="rtl">של״ה</span>, see footnote 20a., and <i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>, see footnote 8), (with the money they can buy their own food which is less embarrassing than accepting a chicken).</i> (replace them) with charity money which is given to the poor (for sustenance), (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>, Jacob ben Moses Moellin; see footnote 8.</i> There are places where it is customary to visit the graves and to increase (the giving of) charity which is all a beautiful custom. It is necessary to slaughter the atonement chickens immediately after completing the ceremony and laying one’s hands<sup class="footnote-marker">21a</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a comment by <i>Turei Zahav</i>, (see footnote 19a.): 605:3 - “And one lays his hands (on it, the chicken)…”: Even though this thing (this practice) appears in the <i>Tur</i>, <span dir="rtl">טור</span>, (see the translation of this section in footnote 18 and see footnote 23) in the name of the <i>geonim</i> (see footnote 19), it is very perplexing in my eyes since this appears as sacrificing animals and slaughtering them outside of the Temple. And even though the rooster is not proper as a sacrifice, since we found that it is a forbidden practice in chapter 469 (of the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>) concerning the matter of such meat for the Passover for which the <i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>, (see footnote 8) forbid even a rooster, and how much the more so here, where one does it explicitly as a sacrificial matter, that this fear is present (this consideration that it might be prohibited is present). This being so it is better to prevent this matter, (following the dictum to sit and not do it is better. (This expression, <span dir="rtl">ושב ואל תעשה</span>, is found in <i>Erubim</i> 100a: if by performing a <i>mitzvah</i> you might transgress a law, you should not do it. In a case of doubt do not do such a thing.) And so it seems to me in my humble opinion.</i> on it like (it was done with) the Sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The “laying of the hands” of the priest onto the animal that was sacrificed was part of the rite which transformed the animal from a mere profane animal into a holy sacrifice to God. Sacrifice from the biblical through the temple Period in Jerusalem was the way in which man communicated with God. Extensive rituals and practices developed around the sacrifice which was performed by the special priestly class, the <i>cohanim</i>. The main thrust behind a sacrifice was the fact that man was surrendering to God a living thing of some value to man. This brought out vividly the fact that all things man has on earth are given by God and ultimately God has complete control over man and all He has given to man. Special concern was placed on the blood of an animal sacrifice for <i>dam</i>, <span dir="rtl">דם</span>, blood, was the symbol of life. “For the life of the flesh is in the blood; and I have given it for your souls; for it is the blood that makes atonement, by reason of the life (that is in it)”, (Leviticus 17:11). The people were therefore forbidden to eat the blood of an animal since it belonged to God. The offering to God of a sacrifice had to be an animal which was owned by the person offering it and the animal had to be domesticated and proper for food. In other words, it had to have some worth. Work animals were excluded from this. An animal had to be at least eight days old and totally without blemishes, (Leviticus 22:17-25).<br>A very large portion of the Bible, especially the Pentateuch, is concerned with the extensive ritual, ceremony, and material that went into a sacrifice. Also different types of sacrifices were outlined for different purposes. The following are separate types of sacrifices present in the Bible: Propitiatory, both Sin and Guilt Offerings, Dedicatory, Burnt, Meal, Libation, Fellowship, Peace and Thanksgiving, Wave, Votive, Freewill, and Ordination Offerings.<br>During the period of the First and Second Temple, elaborate sacrificial services took place twice daily, <i>Shaḥrit</i>, Morning and <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon, along with special sacrifices for Sabbaths, festivals, and special circumstances.<br>Yom Kippur, being the holiest day of the year had associated with it a special and unique sacrificial atonement ritual. The <i>Avodah</i>, <span dir="rtl">עבודה</span>, which means literally “service” was the name applied to the ritual, during the Temple period, which was the central part of the <i>Musaf</i>, <span dir="rtl">מוסף</span>, additional, sacrifice on the Day of Atonement. With the <i>Avodah</i>, which is a poetical recounting of the Temple ritual, became the central part of the <i>Musaf</i> liturgy (see footnote 166) for the Day of Atonement. The ritual itself was based on the sixteenth chapter of Leviticus where the special sacrifical ritual for atonement is described. After the detailing of the ritual is completed, the Bible established that the tenth day of the seventh month (the tenth of Tishrei which today is considered the first month) would be set aside as a special Sabbath for the purpose of atonement, (Leviticus 16:29-31). The extensive details associated with the sacrifice on the Day of Atonement are described in the Talmud in tractate <i>Yoma</i>.<br>It was on Yom Kippur, and only on Yom Kippur, that the high priest would enter the very center of the Temple, the Holy of Holies. He had to make special preparations for this ritual. One week prior to the Day of Atonement, the high priest would begin living in a special apartment in the Temple court where he studied with the scholarly elders all the special laws of Yom Kippur. Another priest would also stand-by and study in case something happened to the high priest. The day prior to Yom Kippur the high priest would enter the Temple and perform all the minute details involved in a sacrifice along with the other priests who were used to sacrificing. The high priest rarely performed the regular daily sacrifices, he only functioned on special occasions. On the Day of Atonement, the high priest himself would perform all the sacred and sacrificial duties.<br>After proper cleansing for the <i>Musaf</i>, or <i>Avodah</i> Service the high priest would first sacrifice a bull as his own personal sin offering after which he would confess and purify the sins of his own family, those of the priests (the tribe of Aaron), and finally those of the whole congregation of Israel, (Leviticus 16:6). The high priest, in the Holy of Holies, would carefully sprinkle and dispose of the animal’s blood as was prescribed. It was at this time, and only at this time, that he would utter the holy name of God, the Tetragrammaton, <span dir="rtl">יהוה</span>, and when he uttered this the people outside would prostrate themselves and respond, “Blessed be His Name whose glorious kingdom is forever ever and ever.” This was repeated ten times according to the Babylonian Talmud, (Yoma 2:2) and thirteen times according to the Jerusalem Talmud (Yoma 3:7).<br>The high priest then drew lots, one marked for “Azazel” and the other marked as a “sin offering for the Lord”. Depending upon the drawing of the lots, two he-goats had different parts to play in the remaining ritual. The goat marked “for Azazel” would be lead out of the Temple into the wilderness called Azazel. This he-goat symbolically carried the sins of Israel away and was lost over a cliff in the wilderness along with Israel’s sins. A red ribbon which had been tied to the goat was brought back to the people to display to them that the goat had been lost in Azazel. The he-goat marked as a “sin offering for the Lord” was offered as such. This was followed by a special incense-offering and a prayer for good weather, prosperity, and the sovereignty of Judah, whereupon the high priest would come out from the Holy of Holies marking the end to the <i>Avodah</i> ritual.<br>The <i>Avodah</i> liturgy expanded in its development from simply a description of the Temple service and the reading of Mishna <i>Yoma</i>, chapters 1-7 to an elaborate service rich with special liturgical poems, <i>piyyutim</i> (see footnote 149), most of them acrostics, their beginning word following the alphabet. Different Jewish communities developed separate rites. Most rites contain a brief synopsis of the history of Israel and the purity of its early generations culminating in a description of the Temple ritual on the Day of Atonement and the Holy of Holies. Some communities and rites even still call for a prostration on the floor of the synagogue during the <i>Avodah</i> Service as was done at the Temple upon the prononciation of the Tetragrammaton.<br><i>Piyyutim</i> also close the <i>Avodah</i> Service expressing the misfortune of Israel who, because of her sins, is deprived of the Temple and its sacrificial cult and must suffer persecution and exile. The <i>piyyutim</i> call for the reestablishment of the Temple, which is followed by the <i>seliḥot</i> (see footnote 14) prayers (penitential prayers of forgiveness) of the <i>Musaf</i> Service.<br>Anson Rainey, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 599-602; Hanoch Avenary, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 3, pp. 976-80.</i>; and they (it is customary) throw their intestines on the roofs or in a courtyard, a place from where fowls are able to take (the intestines of the slaughtered chickens), (<span dir="ltr">טור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <span dir="rtl">טור</span>, is the singular for the word <i>Turim</i> or the <i>Arba’ah Turim</i>, the four columns, the major halakhic work of Jacob ben Asher who lived from around 1270 until 1340. He was the son of a famous halakhic authority, Asher b. Jehiel, known as the “<i>Rosh</i>”. Jacob ben Asher studied under his father and moved with him from Germany to Toledo in 1303. His work on the <i>Turim</i> was the result of the fact that in his time there was no one halakhic work free from controversy. Different opinions were present and there were no clear and authoritative halakhic decisions. Jacob ben Asher wanted to compose a work which would include all the laws and customs which applied in his day. He divided his work into four sections or <i>turim</i>, “rows”. Part one was called <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>. It contains 697 chapters on the laws of blessings, prayers, Sabbaths, festivals, and fasts. The second part was called <i>Yoreh De’ah</i>. It contains 403 chapters on the laws of ritual, <i>Issur ve-Hetter</i> (that which was forbidden and that which was permitted), and laws of mourning, idolatry, and usury. Part three, <i>Even ha-Ezer</i>, has 178 chapters on the laws affecting women; marriage, divorce, wedding contracts (<i>Ketubbah</i>), and childless widowhood (<i>ḥaliẓah</i>). The fourth part, <i>Ḥoshen Mishpat</i>, contains 427 chapters on civil law and personal relations.<br>Jacob ben Asher used the Talmud and its commentaries as well as the opinions of other authorities before him. He usually decided according to the opinion of Maimonides and his father, Asher b. Jehiel. He did though differ with Maimonides on questions of faith and belief.<br>The <i>Turim</i> was first published in 1475 and it became a widely accepted halakhic code. Joseph Caro used it and its organization as the basis for the <i>Beit Yosef</i> and the Shulḥan Arukh. (For a treatment of the <i>Arba’ah Turim</i> in relation to other code literature, see the introduction to this thesis.)<br>Ephraim Kupfer, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 9, pp. 1214-16.</i>

Siman 606

<b>“A man should appease his friend (i.e., a person whom he wronged) on the Eve of Yom Kippur” - Containing four paragraphs.</b><br>Yom Kippur does not atone for sins between a man and his comrad (fellow-man) until he conciliates him. Even if he angered him only in words, he is required to appease him (his fellow-man). And if at first he is not pacified, he (must) return and go to him a second and third time. Each time he should take three men with him, and if on the third time he does not become reconciled he (no longer) is obligated to him, (nevertheless afterwards he should say before ten (people) that he did request forgiveness from him), (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי דיומא ומהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> in <i>Yoma</i> and <i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מרדכי דיומא ומהרי״ל</span>.<br>Mordecai ben Hillel ha-Kohen, the author of <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span dir="rtl">מרדכי</span>, lived from approximately 1240 until 1298. Not much is known personally about this German author and rabbinic authority. He was a descendant of Eliezer b. Joel ha-Levi, a relative of Asher b. Jehiel, a brother-in-law to Meir ha-Kohen who wrote <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> (see footnote 27) and he was a pupil of Meir b. Baurch of Rothenburg, Isaac b. Moses of Vienna (author of <i>Or Zaru’a</i>, see footnote 118), and Perez b. Elijah of Corbeil. He died as a martyr along with his wife and five children in the Rindfleisch massacres. He is mostly known for his great work, <i>Sefer Mordekhai</i>, which is referred to as the “<i>Mordekhai</i>”. It is a huge collection which expanded upon talmudic problems in the style of the <i>tosafot</i> but which followed the arrangement of the laws established by Isaac Alfasi. Even though Mordecai does not include all of Alfasi’s laws, he included over three hundred books and authors and employed much material from the <i>Or Zaru’a</i> and responsa of Meir of Rothenburg. The <i>Mordekahi</i> was probably completed before 1286 when Meir was imprisoned because his writings are included in the book. The book was compiled by students of Mordecai who refer to him as “my master, Rabbi Mordecai”.<br>The book was widely distributed in two versions. The first version, the “Rhenish” contained customs of French, English, and eastern German communities. The “Austrian” version reflected the customs of south-eastern Europe, including Austria, Hungary, Bohemia, Saxony, and Moravia. Commentaries grew up around the <i>Mordekhai</i> including the <i>Hagahot Mordekhai</i> by Samuel Schlettstadt in 1376 (see footnote 198).<br>The <i>Mordekhai</i> became widely accepted even in the Sephardi Jewish communities. In fact, <i>Mordekhai</i> was one of the few Ashkenazi works that Joseph Caro cited in the <i>Beit Yosef</i> and the Shulḥan Arukh. The Mordekhai was the final halakhic ruling for most Jews until Moses Isserles came along.<br>The section of <i>Mordekhai</i> that is cited in this section of the Shulḥan Arukh is part of the commentary found in the <i>Mordekhai</i> on the Talmud tractate <i>Yoma</i>.<br>Israel Moses Ta-Shma, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 311-14.<br><i>Yoma</i>, <span dir="rtl">יומא</span>, is the fifth tractate in the order of <i>Mo’ed</i> found in our Mishna and Tosefta. It concerns the laws of Yom Kippur. The word <i>Yoma</i> means “The Day” in Aramaic, referring to the Day of Atonement. The first seven chapters of the Mishna <i>Yoma</i> describe the elaborate Temple service performed by the high priest (see footnote 22), and the eighth and final chapter concerns primarily the laws of fasting.<br>Moshe David Herr, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 16, pp. 844-45.<br>For <i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> If he (i.e., the person who was wronged) was his teacher, he must go to him many times until he becomes appeased.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: The person to forgive should not be cruel by refusing forgiveness (to the one seeking forgiveness), (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>)<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i>, unless his intent is for the good of the one requesting forgiveness, (<span dir="ltr">גמרא דיומא</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gemara Yoma</i>, <span dir="rtl">גמרא דיומא</span>, is a reference to the Babylonian tractate of <i>Yoma</i> concerning the laws of Yom Kippur; see footnote 24.</i> But if one caused him (the wronged person) a bad name, there is no necessity in forgiving him, (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי וסמ״ק והגה״מ פ״ב מהלכות תשובה ומהרי״ו</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> and <i>Sefer Mitzvot Katan</i> and <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> chapter two from “The Laws of Repentance” and <i>Mahariv</i>, <span dir="rtl">מרדכי וסמ״ק והגה״מ פ״ב מהלכדת תשובה ומהרי״ו</span>.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span dir="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.<br>The acronym “SeMaK”, <i>Sefer Mitzvot Katan</i>, <span dir="rtl">סמ״ק</span>, was written by Isaac ben Joseph of Corbeil who died in 1280. He was one of the greatest French codifiers of the thirteenth century. His “Small Book of Commandments” reflected his famous piety. This is the book for which he is known. His SeMaK is a collection of all the <i>halakhot</i> of his time period, along with ethical homilies, parables and legends, <i>aggadot</i>. He divided his work into seven “Pillars” corresponding to the seven days of the week where laws normally associated with a particular day were included under that day’s section. Isaac b. Joseph or Corbeil used as a guide for his work, though not his particular divisions, the previous work, <i>Sefer Mitzvot Gadol</i> (SeMaG, <span dir="rtl">סמ״ג</span>, the “Big Book of Commandments”) by Moses of Coucy. The SeMaK, though, did not include the extensive halakhic arguments of the SeMaG.<br>The SeMaK became such a popular work in France and Germany that parts of it were inserted in the daily prayer-books to be read instead of the prayers of supplication and the psalms. It became an accepted source used by later codifiers. Commentaries on the SeMaK arose and were attached to it. The book was first published in 1510 in Constantinople. Isaac also wrote decisions to responsa and <i>tosafot</i> to several talmudic tractates. (For the place of the SeMaK in code literature, see the introduction to this thesis).<br>Israel Moses Ta-Shma, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 9, pp. 21-2.<br><i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i>, <span dir="rtl">הגהות מיימוני</span>, was written by Meir ha-Kohen of Rothenburg who lived at the at the end of the thirteenth century and was a pupil of Meir b. Baruch of Rothenburg. <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> was written as a supplement and as notes of explanation to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> by Maimonides (see the introduction to this paper for a description of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. See also footnote 59). The purpose of the glosses to this work was to add to the rulings of Maimonides the opinions and decisions of Germany and France in addition to the views and responsa the author brought from his teacher Meir b. Baruch of Rothenburg. With the addition of these <i>hagahot,</i> the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> became a halakhic work which could be an authoritative code in many of the different centers of Judaism of both Ashkenazi as well as Sephardi communities.<br>The <i>hagahot</i> were first published together with the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> in the edition that was printed in Constantinople in 1509 and they have been included ever since. There are fourteen books in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> but <i>hagahot</i> are not included with the following: <i>Hafla’ah Zera’im</i> (except for a small part at the end), <i>Avodah</i>, <i>Korbanot</i> and <i>Tohorah</i>.<br><i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> was divided into two parts. One was a glosses and notes attached to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> itself, and the second part was called <i>Teshuvot Maimuniyyot</i> (first published in Venice in 1524). It was added on to the end of each book of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and it contained the responsa of German and French scholars relevant to the topics dealt with in the book. Some brief responsa, though, were included directly in the glosses if they had to do with the specific <i>halakhah</i>. Also non-responsa material was included in the <i>Teshuvot</i> section.<br>There were many differences between the 1509 Constantinople version and that published in Venice in 1524. It is obvious that this work underwent significant editing and additions.<br>The note cited by Isserles where he gave credit to <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> was taken from the notes of Meir ha-Kohen on the section of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> entitled <i>Hilkhot Teshuvah</i>, “the Laws of Repentance”, chapter two.<br>Shlomoh Zalman Havlin, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 7, pp. 1104-12.<br><i>Mahariv</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>, is an acronym for <i>M</i>orenu <i>h</i>a-Rav <i>R</i>abenu Jacob (<i>Y</i>a’akov) <i>W</i>eil, who is also known as Jacob ben Judah Weil who died in 1456. He was a German rabbi and an halakhic authority in the first half of the fifteenth century. He lived in the town of Weil. Weil was a pupil of the <i>Maharil</i>, Jacob Moellin (see footnote 8). <i>Maharil</i> ordained him and made him the rabbi of Nuremberg after which he went to Augsburg, Bamberg, and finally Erfurt. Scholars addressed many halakhic questions to him and he published responsa to them. His decisions particularly on laws of slaughtering and examination of animals, <i>hilkhot sheḥitah u-vedikah</i>, were accepted Ashkenazi practice. His responsa were socially and historically valuable in that through the types of questions and answers, one can get a good picture of what German Jewish life was like at this time and how it functioned. He wrote a great deal on the office of the rabbi in Germany and included his suggestions to improve the responsibility and purify the practices of many rabbis.<br>Moses Isserles, especially, and others placed a great deal of importance on Weil’s decisions and relied on them as binding. His works, especially <i>Hilkhot Sheḥitah u-Vedikah</i> inspired many commentaries and additions by later authorities which became the basic work for <i>shoḥatim</i>. ritual slaughterers of animals for food.<br>Yehoshua Horowitz, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 16, pp. 395-96.</i></small>
If a man against whom he sinned died, (the man who sinned) brings ten people and let them stand on his (the dead man’s) grave and he (the sinner) says, “I have sinned against the God of Israel, and against this “person” who I sinned against him,” (and it was customary to seek forgiveness on the Eve of Yom Kippur), (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי דיומא</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> in <i>Yoma</i>, <span dir="rtl">מרדכי דיומא</span>; see footnote 24.</i>
Early rabbinic authorities decreed, coupled with “<span dir="ltr">חרם</span><sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥerem</i>, <span dir="rtl">חרם</span>, is the status of that which is separated from common use or contact, either because it is proscribed as an abomination to God or because it is consecrated to Him.<br>There are different categories of <i>ḥerem</i>. The Torah considers the following to be <i>ḥerem</i>: Israelites who worship other gods and idols. People who commit this idolatry were to be put to the sword and the objects burned. These people and objects contaminated those they came in contact with; The seven nations inhabiting the land promised to Israel - the Hittites, Girgashites, Amorites, Canaanites, Perizzites, Hivites, and Jebusites (Deuteronomy 7:1-2; cf. 20:17). These people were to be totally destroyed; Whatever one privately devoted to the Lord as <i>ḥerem</i> is the most sacred, <i>Kodesh Kodashim</i>, and these objects were never to be sold nor was their status revokable (Leviticus 27:28). Cases of <i>ḥerem</i> in the rest of the Bible follow from these Torah laws. The term later came to mean total destruction.<br>The term <i>ḥerem</i> began to take on different meanings from the Biblical ones as time passed. Even the term <i>ḥerem</i> in the Bible changed its meaning. In Deuteronomy declaring an enemy as <i>ḥerem</i> was done to gain God’s favor by totally devoting to Him, one’s own nation and the total nation of the enemy.<br>After Saul the rise of the enemy <i>ḥerem</i> seems to have disappeared. <i>Ḥerem</i> came to mean, when referred to a nation that the other nation’s religion practiced evil ways that would badly influence the Israelites and therefore these people were to be separated from Israel to preserve their true belief in God (Exodus 34:11ff; Numbers 33:51ff; Deuteronomy 7:1-5; 20:16ff).<br>In later Jewish law the concept of <i>ḥerem</i> changed radically. The <i>ḥerem</i> of Ezra is the first indication that meant banishment and persecution. The term <i>niddui</i> was used in tannaitic literature to mean the punishment of an offender by his isolation from and his being held in contempt by the community at large. The term <i>niddui</i> was used in the Bible (Numbers 12:14) as an isolation punishment. The Pharisees employed <i>niddui</i> if a person did not follow their prescribed standards. A scholar was isolated for his non-compliance with the majority and no one was permitted to contact him lest they too became defiled. <i>Niddui</i> was a Punishment employed by the courts on the heads of the academies.<br>Later in the talmudic laws the term <i>ḥerem</i> was used again as an aggravated form of <i>niddui</i>. A <i>niddui</i> could last for two 30 day periods. After 60 days passed and the person did not satisfy the courts or the academies by changing his ways, a <i>ḥerem</i> was declared which was a total banishment and isolation. Another type of punishment was also established. That was a <i>nezifah</i>, a “reprimand”, which lasted for seven days. It was intended for shame and remorse and automatically expired while a <i>niddui</i> and a <i>ḥerem</i> had to be lifted by the courts.<br>A <i>niddui</i> differed from a <i>ḥerem</i> in that one who was declared <i>niddui</i> could have social intercourse for purposes of study or business, but one placed in <i>ḥerem</i> had to study alone and earn money from the small shop he was permitted to maintain. One punished by <i>niddui</i> or <i>ḥerem</i> was considered in a state of mourning and was therefore not permitted to cut his hair, do laundry, or wear shoes, except for out-of-town walks. He was forbidden to wash himself except for his face, hands, and feet but he did not have to rend his clothes. He had to live in confinement with his family only, no outsider was permitted to come near him, eat or drink with him, greet him, or give him any enjoyment (Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Yoreh De’ah</i> 334:2). He could not be counted as part of the three for grace after the meals nor as one of the ten for a prayer <i>minyan</i>. After his death, his coffin would be stoned, (one stone could be symbolically placed on the coffin). The <i>niddui</i> was actually considered a rather light penalty for minor offenses since it could be so easily lifted. <i>Niddui</i> was during talmudic times put on people by laymen and individual scholars for various reasons, not only by the courts, for reasons such as debt. Only a court designated <i>niddui</i> would necessitate the whole community from disassociating with the isolated person. A non-judicial <i>niddui</i> did not require this. A person had to be warned about a possible <i>niddui</i> for a non-religious offense, but for a religious one no warning was necessary. The <i>niddui</i> had to be publically announced but no evidence was required. The court was considered fair. A <i>niddui</i> by a court could be lifted by any court. A personal <i>niddui</i> had to be lifted by the person who imposed it and, if he was unavailable, by the <i>nasi</i> or leader of the community. The courts tended not to pronounce a <i>niddui</i> against a judge or a scholar. Flogging (see footnote 31) was considered a more appropriate punishment.<br>In post-talmudic times the threat of a <i>niddui</i> or a <i>ḥerem</i> was considered a way of future law enforcement. As time went on the conditions put on one who was excommunicated in the Talmud became the minimum. They became more and more severe. One who was banned could not have his sons circumcised or their children married, their children and wives were expelled from the synagogue, and they were not to be buried with any honor. He was considered as a non-Jew; his <i>ẓiẓit</i> fringes, were cut off, his <i>mezuzah</i> removed from his door, his food proclaimed unfit for Jews, and his books considered trash. The <i>ḥerem</i> became actually, a civil death. The man was dead to the community.<br><i>Ḥerem</i> became more and more frequent as the punishment of excommunication by the Church increased. Jewish courts were influenced by civil courts of the land to impose <i>ḥerem</i> on Jews for monetary reasons, but the Jewish courts remained independent in religious matters. Rabbis became more and more reluctant to impose a <i>ḥerem</i> on an individual especially without the consent of the entire congregation due to the extreme hardship it imposed on the family of the isolated man. This caused the development of partial <i>ḥerem</i> which was less severe and only isolated the person in synagogue worship but not in daily life, for example. <i>Niddui</i> was then imposed for major offenses such as a man refusing to divorce his wife when it was felt that he should, a bridegroom refusing to marry his bride, or offenses such as theft or fraud.<br>Minor forms of <i>ḥerem</i>, <i>niddui</i> or <i>nezifah</i> were pronounced by the head of the rabbinical court, but a severe <i>ḥerem</i> was pronounced in the synagogue either before the open ark or while holding the Torah scroll.
