The proclaimation was made with the sounding of the <i>shofar</i>, the ram’s horn (see footnote 221). The people held candles and put them out symbolically when the excommunication was declared. Several Biblical verses were recited against the one excommunicated and people were warned against associating with the person put in <i>ḥerem</i>. The ceremony concluded with a prayer for the faithful of the congregation.<br>In later times the <i>niddui</i> and <i>ḥerem</i> became so frequent and common that it lost almost all of its significance and force. It became the standard rabbinic reaction to all forms of deviation from the norm of Orthodoxy. Although still pronounced they are no longer binding on the person involved or the community, nor do they carry the terror they once did.<br>Haim Hermann Cohn, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 8, pp. 344-55.</i>”, ban, that the living should not slander the dead.
One may immerse (inaritual bath<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mikveh</i>, <span dir="rtl">מקוה</span>, is a pool or a bath of clear water. When a person immerses in it, it renders ritual cleanliness to one who has become ritually unclean through contact with the dead (Numbers 19) or any other defiling object, or through an unclean flux from the body (Leviticus 15), especially for a menstruant. The <i>mikveh</i> is also used to purify vessels (Numbers 31:22-23). Today the <i>mikveh</i> is used for the menstruant since the laws of ritual purity no longer apply due to the destruction of the Temple. A woman must immerse in the <i>mikveh</i> and purify herself following her menstruation in order to again participate in marital relations. It is also obligatory for proselytes to immerse as part of the ceremony for conversion. Many people still use the <i>mikveh</i> for spiritual purification and thus immerse in it on the eve of the Sabbath, festivals, and especially on the eve of the Day of Atonement. The mikveh serves to purify the spirit, not the body, as described by Maimonides. One has to have a mental intention to purify oneself by immersing in the <i>mikveh</i>.<br>According to Biblical law any collection of water whether drawn or collected naturally is suitable for a <i>mikveh</i> as long as one person can immerse himself, but the rabbis later stated that only water which has not been drawn, that is not collected in a vessel or recepticle, could be used. The rabbis also established a minimum for the amount of water to be used, that is the amount of water needed to fill a square cubit to the height of three cubits. This is between 250-1,000 liters depending on various calculations. If it contains at least this much undrawn water, any amount of drawn water can be added to it. A whole talmudic tractate <i>Mikva’ot</i>. is devoted to <i>mikvehs</i> and how they are to be constructed.<br>A <i>mikveh</i> cannot be prefabricated and just installed on a site since this makes it a vessel and constitutes water that has been drawn or collected. It may be built anywhere and out of any material that is water-tight. No water may leak from it, and it must contain the minimun of forty <i>se’ah</i> (250-1,000 liters) of valid, undrawn, water. Originally its height had to be one-hundred and twenty centimeters so one could stand and be totally immersed (even if bending was required). Later it was established that any height was valid if a person could be immersed laying down provided the minimum quantity of water was there.<br>All natural spring water provided it was not discolored by any admixtures is valid. Rain water or melted snow or ice is ideal for the <i>mikveh</i> provided that it flows unstopped into the <i>mikveh</i>. Pipes may be used to carry this water provided they touch the ground and are thus not considered vessels. A <i>mikveh</i> must be emptied by any means, even a pump, from above. No drain in the bottom is permitted as it makes the <i>mikveh</i> a vessel and subject to leakage. As long as the <i>mikveh</i> has contained at least forty <i>se’ah</i> of valid water, all water added to it, even drawn water is valid.<br>David Kotlar and Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 11, pp. 1534-44.<br>A prayer is normally recited after the immersion called al <i>ha-tevilah</i>, “Blessed art Thou, O Lord, our God, King of the Universe, who hast sanctified us by His commandments, and commanded us concerning the immersion”.</i>) and accept lashes<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Lashes and <i>Malkkut Ar’ba’im</i>, <span dir="rtl">מלקות ארבעים</span>, forty lashes is also known as flogging which is a Biblical form of punishment. When no other form of punishment was specifically prescribed, flogging became the standard form of punishment (Deuteronomy 25:2). Flogging was the only punishment in the Bible used as a general rule and not in relation to any particular offence except for the slandering of a virgin where the lashes as well as a fine were prescribed (Deuteronomy 22:18).<br>The maximum number of stripes to be administered in any one case are forty (Deuteronomy 25:3) for any further flogging the Bible stated, would degrade your brother in your eyes (Deuteronomy 25:3). The intent of the Bible seems to be that forty is the maximum number of stripes allowed, but that each offense and its seriousness could determine the number of stripes from one to forty provided the maximum number was not exceeded.<br>Talmudical law detailed how the Biblical punishment of flogging was to be administered. All the laws are found in the Talmudic tractate <i>Makkot</i>, <span dir="rtl">מכות</span>. The rabbis altered the Biblical law of flogging reducing the number of the maximum number of stripes to ever be received from forty to thirty-nine (Mak. 22a) so as to avoid the danger that the flogger accidentally might exceed the number of forty lashes. If he were permitted to administer forty lashes, the flogger might have given an extra one before he could have been stopped thus administering forty-one lashes which exceeds the maximum number of lashes allowed by the Bible and disgracing the man in the eyes of his brothers and thus also would the flogger be made subject to flogging for his transgression. Therefore the rabbis ruled that the maximum number of stripes they would allow was thirty-nine, for even if the flogger made a mistake he could be stopped before he exceeded the maximum number of forty stripes even if he gave an extra one as he was being stopped. (This is the reason for the comment by Isserles to this law given by Caro, see footnote 55).<br>The rabbis carefully defined all the offenses for which flogging would serve as a punishment. The number thirty-nine became the maximum number of stripes for offenses for which flogging was administered. The rabbis though, were careful not to cause death by flogging which would have exceeded the Biblical law. Therefore all people to be flogged were first examined to see if they could physically withstand the punishment. The examiner would then determine the safe number of stripes to be inflicted (Mak. 3:11). Flogging would be stopped if it appeared during the stripes that the man could not take anymore (Mak. 17:5). Flogging could also be postponed a day until a person would be fit to under-go the punishment (Mak. 17:3).<br>Floggings were administered with a whip made of calfskin to the bare upper body of the offender. One-third of the lashes were given on the breast and the other two-thirds on the back. The one being flogged would stand in a bowed position and the flogger would stand on a stone above him. As the stripes were being given admonitory and consolatory verses from the Bible would be recited (Mak. 3:12-14). If death did result and the flogging had been conducted according to the law, the flogger was not liable. If though, he had not faithfully followed the law, he had to flee to a city of refuge which was the case in any accidental homocide.<br>Flogging for disciplinary reasons as well as for punishment for other than transgressing actively a prohibition of the Torah was also prescribed by the rabbis and this was usually done in a public place so as to be a deterrent to others to violate laws. Usually disciplinary stripes were given in lesser numbers (that is less than thirty-nine) and were not administered to the bare upper body nor were they given with a leather whip. As time passed, people were more often allowed to pay fines rather than be whipped and whipping all but replaced capital punishment in Israel.<br>On Yom Kippur a custom arose that after the <i>Minḥah</i> Afternoon Service, forty stripes (according to Caro, but only thirty-nine in Ashkenazi communities as pointed out by Isserles) were administered while the victim repeated the confession, <i>viddui</i> (see footnote 39). The one who administered the flogging was to say “And He (God) pities and will atone sins”, (Psalms 78:38). The purpose of this custom was to increase one’s awareness of his need for confession to atone for his sins. This was a visual and physical admission of sins and it was believed to help one receive complete atonement.<br>Haim Hermann Cohn, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 6, pp. 1348-51; Moshe David Herr, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 5, p. 1381.</i> (to effect atonement) whenever desired provided that it is before nightfall, but one does not bless over the immersion.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: One needs to immerse one time without a confession because of pollution (urinary emmission). The same holds true if one pours nine kavs<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>kav</i>, was a unit of measurement for a liquid. According to present day standards a <i>kav</i> is approximately equivalent to 1.2 liters.</i> of water (upon himself), (if the immersion pains him, (<span dir="ltr">מגן אברהם</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Magen Avraham</i>, <span dir="rtl">מגן אברהם</span>, is a seventeenth century commentary on the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> which was first printed in Dyhernfurth in 1692. It was highly accepted in Poland and Germany where it became the model for halakhic decisions by the scholars of that country who often differed from other codifiers. The <i>Magen</i> Avraham was written by Abraham Abele ben Ḥayyim ha-Levi Gombiner who lived from 1637 until 1683. He was of Polish birth but he moved to Lithuania after the death of his parents in Chmielnicki Massacres in 1648. After studying there with his relative Jacob Isaac Gombiner he moved to Kalisz where he was appointed head of the yeshivah and <i>dayyan</i>, judge, of the <i>bet din</i> rabbinical court.<br>Abraham’s commentary is evidence of his vast knowledge of halakhic material. The goal of his work, <i>Magen Avraham</i> was to provide a compromise between the decisions of Joseph Caro and the glosses of Moses Isserles. When no compromise could be arrived at Abraham usually sided with his fellow Ashkenazi, Isserles. Abraham felt that all Jewish customs were valid and sacred and he attempted to justify them even when there was a disagreement among the codifiers. Abraham highly regarded the Zohar and Kabbalists and he occasionally accepted their opinions over that of the codifiers.<br>Gombiner was also the author of a commentary on the <i>Yalkut Shimoni</i> called <i>Zayit Ra’anan</i> and a collection of homilies on Genesis called <i>Shemen Sason</i> in addition to a short commentary of the <i>Tosefta</i> of <i>Nezikim</i>.<br>Shmuel Ashkenazi, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 7, pp. 766-67.</i>), this is also effective, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ו וכל בו ותשב״צ</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahariv</i> and <i>Kol Bo</i> and <i>Tashbaẓ</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ו וכל בו ותשב״ץ</span>.<br><i>Mahariv</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>, see footnote 27.<br><i>Kol Bo</i>, <span dir="rtl">כל בו</span>, which when translated means “everything within” is an anonymous work which contained both halakhic decisions and explanations of <i>halakhot</i> arranged according to subject matter. The book, <i>Kol Bo</i>, was written either at the end of the thirteenth century or at the beginning of the fourteenth century. The work is very similar to a commentary on <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> called <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i> written by Aaron b. Jacob ha-Kohen of Lunel and published in Florence in 1750-51. The fact that they were so similar and covered the same material except that the <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i> contained more material than did the <i>Kol Bo</i> caused some scholars to believe that the <i>Kol Bo</i> was a later abridgment to the <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i>. But this may not be true due to the differences in their arrangement, the <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i> being more systematic. There is another view that the <i>Kol Bo</i> was, the first edition to the <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i> and probably by the same author, Aaron b. Jacob ha-Kohen; the material in the <i>Kol Bo</i> certainly preceded that of the <i>Orḥot Ḥayyim</i>.<br>There are one-hundred and forty-eight sections to the <i>Kol Bo</i> which cover many subjects of Jewish ceremonial, ritual, civil, personal, and community life. The anthology includes collections of laws from numerous and varied halakhic works. The <i>Kol Bo</i> was basically patterned after the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> of Maimonides together with the additions of the scholars of Germany, France, and Provence. There were in addition a few rulings by Spanish scholars included. Many of the laws included in the <i>Kol Bo</i> are from books no longer in existence today. It is possible that the <i>Kol Bo</i> never had much original material, but was mainly an anthology of rules from various sources. The <i>Kol Bo</i> was first printed in Naples in 1490-91.<br>Shlomoh Zalman Havlin, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, pp. 1159-60.<br><i>Tashbaẓ</i>, <span dir="rtl">תשב״ץ</span>, see footnote 20.</i> One who incurs a death between Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur, it is permissible to wash and to immerse on the Eve of Yom Kippur because Yom Kippur cancels the “<i>shiva</i><sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shiva</i> is a seven day mourning period which begins immediately after the funeral. The mourners traditionally gather in the house of the deceased where they sit on low stools or over-turned couches with their heads enrobed. This is obligatory for close relatives of the deceased, be it husband or wife, mother or father, son or daughter, or brother or sister. The mourners must perform <i>keri’ah</i>, a rending of their garments as a symbol of mourning and they are required to recite the blessing <i>dayyan ha-emet</i> proclaiming God as the true judge. During the <i>shiva</i> period, mourners are not permitted to work physically, conduct financial transactions, bathe, annoint the body, cut the hair, cohabit, wear leather shoes, wash clothes, greet acquaintances, and study the Torah. Study, though of sorrowful portions of the Bible and Talmud, such as Job, Lamentations, parts of Jeremiah and the laws of mourning is permitted.<br>The first meal of the mourners after the funeral is called <i>se’udat havra’ah</i>, the meal of consolation. This meal is provided by friends and neighbors for the mourners in accordance with the talmudic law that a mourner is forbidden to eat of his own bread on the first day of mourning (Mk. 27b). A mourner is also not permitted to put on <i>teffilin</i>, prayer phylacteries, on the first day of the <i>Shiva</i> period.<br>The first three days of this period are considered the most intense and are known as the three days for weeping the entire seven day period is known as a time of lamenting. The <i>shiva</i> period is suspended on the Sabbath and ends on a holiday even if the total period of seven days has not elapsed. (see also footnote 37).<br>Aaron Rothkoff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 488-89.</i>”, (the seven day mourning period), (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל, הלכות שמחות</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span dir="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>, “The Laws of Mourning”, <span dir="ltr">הלכות שמחות</span>, (“<span dir="rtl">שמחות</span>”, “Pleasures” is a euphemism). The Laws of Mourning as discussed by Moellin; see footnote 8.</i> Even though it is customary not to wash (bath) during the entire “sheloshim<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sheloshim</i>, means thirty and it refers to the thirty days of mourning after the death of a close relative; mother, father, wife, husband, son, daughter, brother, or sister, and it begins from the time of the burial. The mourner during the <i>sheloshim</i> is not to wear new or even festive clothes; not to shave or have a hair cut; not to participate in festivities including wedding, circumcision, or <i>pidyon ha-ben</i> (redemption of the first born male child) banquets unless it is one’s own child; not to marry; and to abstain from going to entertainment. It is also customary to change one’s usual seat in the synagogue during these thirty days. If the last day of <i>sheloshim</i> falls on the Sabbath then the mourning period ends prior to the Sabbath.<br>The three pilgrimage feativals and Rosh HaShanah may shorten the <i>shiva</i> or <i>sheloshim</i> period. If the mourner observes at least one hour of the <i>shiva</i> (see footnote 35) before Passover or Shavuot, the <i>shiva</i> is waived and the <i>sheloshim</i> is reduced to fifteen days after the holiday, but in the case of Succot the mourner has to observe only eight days of <i>sheloshim</i> after the festival. If a mourner observes at least one hour of <i>shiva</i> before Rosh HaShanah, the <i>shiva</i> is waived and the Day of Atonement ends the <i>sheloshim</i>. If a mourner observes at least one hour of <i>shiva</i> before Yom Kippur <i>shiva</i> is waived and Succot ends <i>sheloshim</i>. Minor festivals such as Ḥanukkah and Purim do not shorten the <i>shiva</i> or <i>sheloshim</i>. If a person only learns of a death within thirty days of the passing (<i>shemu’ah kerovah</i>) he must observe the complete rites of <i>shiva</i> and <i>sheloshim</i>. If the news reaches him more than thirty days after the death has occurred (<i>shemua’ah reḥokah</i>) then he must only observe the mourning rites of <i>shiva</i> and <i>sheloshim</i> for one hour. When one is mourning the death of one’s parents the prohibitions of the <i>sheloshim</i> period are observed for an entire twelve months along with the recitation of the mourner’s <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177) for eleven months, (see Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Yoreh De’ah</i>, 399).</i>”, (thirty day mourning period), a commanded immersion is permitted, (<span dir="ltr">דעת עצמו</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Da’at Aẓmo</i>, <span dir="rtl">דעת עצמו</span>; this is the way indicated in Isserles’ glosses that the comment he was making was Isserles’ own opinion and was not taken from another source.</i></small>

Siman 607

<b>“The order of confession (of sins)</b><sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Viddui</i>, <span dir="rtl">ודוי</span>, confession of sins, is a prerequisite for expiation and atonement in the Bible for sins committed individually or collectively. In the Bible there is usually a pardoning by God following the confession. Examples of this are found in the stories of Cain, (Genesis 4:13) David, (Psalms 32,41,51, and 69), Judah with Tamar (Genesis 36:26), Achan and the spoils of Jerico (Joshua 7:19-21), Saul and the Amalekite booty (I Samuel 15:24-25). There are also examples of Biblical confessions made for the nation; Moses and the golden calf worshipping (Exodus 32:31), the high priest’s confession on the Day of Atonement (Leviticus 16:6, 11, 21) and the confession of Ezra (9:6, 7, 15) and Nehemiah (1:6,7;9:2,33-35).<br>Prior to the destruction of the Temple confessions had to precede special sin and guilt sacrificial offerings. The person confessing had to place his hands upon the head of the animal sacrifice to transfer his sins to the animal (Leviticus 1:4). The Bible gives no wording for these confessions but there is in the Mishna the wording for the confession of the high priest on Yom Kippur: “O God, I have committed iniquity, transgressed, and sinned before Thee, I and my house. O God forgive the iniquities and transgressions and sins which I have committed and transgressed and sinned before Thee I and my house as it is written in the Law of Thy servant Moses, ‘For on this day shall atonement be made for you, to cleanse you; from all your sins shall ye be clean before the Lord’” (Leviticus 16:30; Yoma 3:8).<br>In rabbinic times it became an accepted custom to confess one's sins before seeking atonement and the confession of sins became an integral part of the synagogue ritual. On the Day of Atonement it became a focal point of the service. According to the Talmud (Yoma 87b) the simple statement "Truly, we have sinned" is sufficient for confession, but elaborate formulas of confession have evolved. The <i>Ashamnu</i> "We have incurred guilt" is the prayer on Yom Kippur that is inserted into the fourth benediction of the reader's repetition of the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). The prayer consists of two parts, each of which contains an alphabetical listing of sins probably committed by people during the year for which they are seeking atonement on Yom Kippur. The first alphabetical confession is known as the <i>Viddui Katan</i>, the "Small Confession". The second part of the <i>Ashamnu</i> is known as the <i>Viddui Gadol</i>, the "Great Confession". It is also known as the <i>Al Ḥet</i> "For the sin which I committed before Thee" which is the statement that precedes each specified sin. These confessionals are first mentioned in geonic liturgy (see footnote 19). Additions to the enumerated sins have evolved to include all possible transgressions since a person might have unintentionally forgotten about a sin during the year which must be confessed in order to receive atonement. The sins are all confessed in the first person plural, "we", communally, thus a person may even confess a sin he is sure he did not commit.<br>In addition to Yom Kippur, the <i>Ashamnu</i> is also recited during the <i>Seliḥot</i> Services prior to Yom Kippur (see footnote 14). It is also recited in the <i>Minḥah</i> Afternoon Service on the Eve of Yom Kippur and ten times during the Day itself.<br>The <i>Ashamnu</i> is also included in the daily service of the Ḥasidic rite, and on Monday and Thursday it is recited by the Sephardi, Italian, and Yemenite communities.<br>The <i>viddui</i>, confession of sins, can also be said by individuals silently at appropriate occasions especially when one is about to die. The <i>viddui</i> said on the Day of Atonement in the singular has become acceptable as a death confessional. A bridegroom also recites this <i>viddui</i> in the singular during the <i>Minḥah</i> Service before his wedding, the wedding day being considered a day of judgment for the bride and groom.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 5, pp. 878-80.</i> <b>during <i>Minḥah</i></b><sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minḥah</i>, <span class="rtl">מנחה</span>, is the Afternoon Service which is one of the three daily services, the Morning Service being called the <i>Shaḥarit</i> (see footnote 17) and the Evening Service being called the <i>Arvit</i> or <i>Ma'ariv</i> Service (see footnote 144). The <i>Minḥah</i> Service possibly derives its name from the <i>minḥah</i> sacrificial offering performed at the Temple in Jerusalem in the afternoon. A lamb was sacrificed at the Temple at dusk. The <i>Minḥah</i> Service consists of the following parts: the <i>Ashrei</i> (Psalm 145 preceded by Psalms 84:5 and 144:15 and closed by Psalm 115:18); the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 17); the <i>Taḥanun</i> (see footnote 10); and it is concluded with the <i>Aleinu</i> (see footnote 17).<br>On the Sabbath and on fast days a portion of the Torah is read before the <i>Amidah</i> and in some rites portions dealing with daily sacrifices are read before the <i>Ashrei</i>. On Sabbaths part of the portion from the Torah of the coming week is read.<br>The <i>Minḥah</i> Prayer can begin any time after the sixth and one-half hour of the day, which mean any time after 12:30 P. M. If <i>Minḥah</i> is prayed at this time of the day it is called <i>Minḥah Gedolah</i> or the "major" <i>Minḥah</i>. If <i>Minḥah</i> is prayed after the nine and one-half hour, which means after 3:30 P. M., it is called <i>Minḥah Ketannah</i> or the "minor" <i>Minḥah</i>. The <i>Minḥah</i> Service must though be completed before the twelfth hour, that is, before sunset, (Ber. 4:1; Ber 26b-27a).<br>The Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 324 states that one may pray both <i>Minḥah Gedolah</i> and <i>Minḥah Ketannah</i> provided that one is obligatory (<i>ḥovah</i>) and the other is a voluntary act (<i>reshut</i>). But this is only allowed for the extremely pious.<br>The third meal on the Sabbath, <i>Se'udah Shelishit</i> is usually eaten between the <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon Service, and the <i>Ma'ariv</i>, or Evening Service. It has become the custom during the daily service to wait and begin the <i>Minḥah</i> Service shortly before sunset, so that the congregation can wait a few moments and then not have to reassemble (for a third time, having also assembled in the morning for <i>Shaḥarit</i>) for the Evening, <i>Ma'ariv</i> Service which on Sabbaths and holidays can be recited immediately following the sunset. On weekdays, it can be recited even before sunset.<br>cf., Aaron Rothkoff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 31-32.</i> <b>(the Afternoon Prayer) on the Eve of Yom Kippur” - Containing six paragraphs.</b><br>One needs to confess during <i>Minḥah</i> (the Afternoon Prayer) before the (last) meal before fasting.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Se'udah ha-Mafseket</i>, <span class="rtl">סעודה המפסקת</span>, is the term given to the last meal which is eaten immediately prior to the fast of Yom Kippur and the fast of <i>Tishah be-Av</i>. It contains the last food which is eaten until the fast has been completed.</i><br><small><i>Hagah</i>: If one is alone he says it (the confessional) after he finished his Silent Prayer, but the public reader<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shelia'aḥ Ẓibbur</i>, <span class="rtl">שליח צבור</span>, is the public reader or the envoy or messenger of the community. It is the term given to an individual in public synagogue worship who officiates as the reader or the cantor, <i>ḥazzan</i>, the one who chants the liturgy. The main function of the <i>sheli'aḥ ẓibbur</i> is to lead the congregation in communal worship by chanting (or reading) aloud certain prayers or parts of them. He also recites the doxology of calling the congregation to worship (<i>Barekhu</i>) and he repeats the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 17 and 43). He also recites most <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177) prayers which is a prayer in praise of God, and he leads the congregation in responsive readings and hymns. The Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, 53:4-9 lists the qualifications of a <i>sheli'aḥ ẓibbur</i>. He must have humility, be acceptable to the congregation, know the rules of prayer, and the proper pronunciation of the Hebrew text, have an agreeable voice, be properly dressed, and have a beard. The beard however was later not required except on the High Holydays. Except for the recital of hymns and psalms (e.g., <i>pesukei de-zimra</i>, see footnote 17) the <i>sheli'aḥ ẓibbur</i> had to be a male past the age of bar mitzvah, thirteen years old.<br>c.f., Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 1355-56.</i> says his (confessional) on Yom Kippur during the Silent Prayer,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha-Tefillah</i>, <span class="rtl">התפילה</span>, "The Prayer" is a synonym for the <i>Amidah</i>, <span class="rtl">עמידה</span>, the Silent Prayer said standing which is recited individually during each of the daily services, the <i>Shaḥarit</i>, Morning Service (see footnote 17), <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon Service (see footnote 40), and the <i>Arvit</i> (or <i>Ma'ariv</i>), the Evening Service (see footnote 144). It is also recited for the <i>Musaf</i>, the Additional Service (see footnote 166) on the Sabbath and festivals, and on Yom Kippur for a fifth time during the <i>Ne'ilah</i>, the concluding prayer (see footnote 191). During a congregational prayer that is when there is a <i>minyan</i>, a quorum of at least ten adult males, the reader (see footnote 41) repeats the <i>Amidah</i> outloud and on festivals a number of additions are made. Originally the repetition was for the uneducated people who did not know the prayers. Upon hearing each blessing they could respond, "Amen" and thus fulfill their religious obligation of reciting the <i>Amidah</i>. The <i>Amidah</i> of <i>Arvit</i>, the Evening Service, was originally optional but it long ago became obligatory to recite it silently but it is not repeated outloud except on the Sabbath eve when an abbreviated version of it in one single benediction is recited.<br>The word Ha-Tefillah for this prayer originated in the Talmud where it was referred to as "The Prayer" <i>par excellence</i>. It is also known as the <i>Amidah</i> for it is said "standing" and as the <i>Shemoneh-Esreh</i> (18) for it originally had eighteen benedictions in the daily worship while today it contains ninteen.<br>The <i>Amidah</i> takes on various forms for different occasions. On weekdays there are ninteen benedictions, on fast days an additional benediction is added when the reader repeats the prayer, (in ancient times on some public fasts six prayers were added to the regular ones, Ta'an. 2:2-4). On Sabbaths and festivals there are only seven benedictions in the <i>Amidah</i> except for the <i>Musaf</i> Service (see footnote 166) on Rosh HaShanah where there are nine. All the various forms of the <i>Amidah</i> have six blessings in common, the first and last three, with the middle changing according to the occasion. The first three benedictions praise God and the last three basically express thanksgiving. On the weekdays the intermediate benedictions are petitions and the <i>Amidah</i> is therefore predominantly a prayer of supplication where praise, petition, and thanksgiving are included. In most of the benedictions the one praying addresses God as "Thou" for it is through the <i>Amidah</i> that one communicates with God. The pronoun, "we" is also used throughout the <i>Amidah</i> which indicates that it is to be a communal prayer. Even though at times it is said individually, the worshipper is considered a member of the congregation. On Sabbaths and on festivals the central prayer concerns the specialness of the day or one aspect of that part of the day (that is, morning, afternoon, or evening on the Sabbath), and there is no petition, only praise, the special blessing of the day, and thanksgiving.<br>On the Day of Atonement the central blessing called <i>Kedushat ha-Yom</i>, the sanctification of the day, is concluded specially as follows: "<i>Barukh</i>…<i>Melekh moḥel ve-sole'aḥ le-avonoteinu</i>…<i>mekaddesh Yisrael ve-Yom ha-Kippurim</i>," "Blessed…King who pardons and forgives our iniquities…who sanctifies Israel and the Day of Atonement". On Yom Kippur also the third blessing (of the first three standard blessings of praise) is elaborated to contain the prayer "<i>u-Vekhen Ten Paḥekha</i>", "Now therefore impose Thy awe", which is an ancient petition for the eschatological Kingdom of God. On the Day of Atonement the silent recital of the <i>Amidah</i> is followed by the <i>viddui</i>, a confession of sins (see footnote 39) which is not written as a benediction. When the reader repeats the <i>Amidah</i> the <i>viddui</i> is inserted into the fourth, the central, benediction. Two confessions are recited, one short and one long which are both arranged in alphabetical order. The sins which every person might have committed during the year are included and enumerated upon. Since this prayer is part of community worship, the pronoun "we" is used, "we have transgressed, etc." (see also footnote 17).<br>Joseph Heinemann, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 2, pp. 838-45.</i> (<span dir="ltr">טור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>, see footnote 23.</i></small>
There is no need to itemize (to detail) (the description of) the sin, but if one wants to detail it<sup class="footnote-marker">44a</sup><i class="footnote">The following comment is found in <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33): 607:2 - "And if one wants to detail (one's sins)…": This means that we speak (that he may confess his sins) even outloud, and it seems to me that this is so only if the sin is commonly known but a sin which is not common knowledge, in everyone's opinion it is forbidden to say it outloud, as it is written, "Happy is the one whose transgression is forgiven…" (Psalms 32:1), (and this is what is written in a <i>Hagah</i>, note). Certainly the <i>Amoraim</i> did not oppose the <i>Tannaim</i> as it is written in Sotah 32, that they ordained the prayer to a wisper so as not to embarrass the transgressors who are confessing about their sins, but rather R. Judah b. Baba decided that before God there was no need to detail (one's sins), and in this there is no difference whether the sin is publically known or not because all is revealed before Him (God), see in the <i>Gemara</i> and in <i>Bayit Ḥadash</i>, <span class="rtl">ב״ח</span>, (see footnote 20b.) that it is not according to the <i>Beit Yosef</i>, <span class="rtl">ב״י</span>, (see footnote 20.) There is a problem in what Rashi wrote in (the Talmud Tractate) <i>Ḥulin</i> at the end of chapter two, that if one sins unintentionally, it is well known, because if someone commits a sin unintentionally, he does not cover it up in order to be embarrassed (about it) and atone for himself, end quotes. It is written in scripture "Happy is the one whose transgression is forgiven whose sin is covered," (Psalms 32:1), and the sin was an unintentional one.</i> he has the permission to do so, but if one confesses silently (i.e., just moving one’s lips but not speaking audibly), it is appropriate to detail the sin.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: But when one prays outloud or the reader, when he repeats the prayer, they do not detail the sin. But saying the prayer “<span dir="ltr">על חטא</span><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Al Ḥet</i>, <span class="rtl">על חטא</span>, is the second part of the <i>Ashamnu</i>. The <i>Al Ḥet</i> is also known as the <i>Viddui Gadol</i>, the "Great Confession". It is an alphabetical listing of all possible sins which one may have committed during the year. Each sin is preceded by the statement <i>Al Ḥet</i>, "For the sin which we have committed before Thee" and then the sin is enumerated. The sins are all confessed in the first person plural "we" so that every person can confess all possible sins, even those he may have forgotten, or those he surely did not commit, together with the whole congregation so as not to overlook a sin which requires specific confession to achieve atonement. The <i>Al Ḥet</i> is found along with the <i>Viddui Katan</i>, the Small Confession, which is also an alphabetical listing of sins and precedes the <i>Al Ḥet</i> in the <i>Ashamnu</i>. The <i>Ashamnu</i> is recited by the reader in his repetition of the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43) during the <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon Service, on the Eve of Yom Kippur and in every repetition of the <i>Amidah</i> on the Day itself, except that of the <i>Ne'ilah</i>, Concluding Service, (see footnote 191). It is included in the fourth, the central, benediction; (see also footnote 39).<br>In the alphabetical listing of the sins in the <i>Al Ḥet</i>, two sins are included under each letter. Each line begins the same: "For the sin we have sinned before Thee…" There are a total of forty-four sins (two for each of the twenty-two Hebrew letters) in alphabetical order. Another nine lines are included to enumerate sins according to their prescribed punishments. The recitation is divided into four parts. After each part the following formula is recited: "And for all these, O God of forgiveness, forgive us, pardon us, grant us atonement." This is chanted during the reader's repetition of the <i>Amidah</i>. In the list of sins, there are specific as well as general sins stated to cover any unknown transgressions. Sins of a ritual nature are not included. The "we" represents collective responsibility that every member of a community should feel. The author of the <i>Al Ḥet</i> is unknown.<br>The Sephardi ritual has only one sin per letter and in some communities the order of the letters is reversed. The Yemenites use a shortened version. The prayer is usually said standing with the head bowed while beating one's breast at the mention of each sin.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 2, pp. 629-30.</i>” in alphabetical order, is not called itemizing (detailing) since everyone says it equally, therefore it is only like a text of a prayer, (<span dir="ltr">ד״ע</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Da'at Aẓmo</i>, <span class="rtl">ד״ע</span>, Isserles' own opinion; see footnote 38.</i></small>
One has to confess standing up and even if one (the confessor) hears it from the reader and he has already confessed, he still must stand.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: And one should again confess with the reader, (<span dir="ltr">ר״ן פ״ב דר״ה</span>);<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim, chapter two of (his commentary to) tractate Rosh HaShanah, <span class="rtl">ר״ן פ״נ דר״ה</span>.<br>Nissim ben Reuben Gerondi is also known by the acronym of his name RaN from <i>Ra</i>bbenu <i>N</i>issim. He lived from around 1310 until approximately 1375. He was one of the most important Spanish talmudists. Nissim was born in Gerona but settled in Barcelona. He never held any official rabbinical office but he functioned as a rabbi and a judge, a <i>dayyan</i>, in his community. Many of the <i>takkanot</i>, the ordinances supplementing the law, which were enacted in Spain originated with Rabbenu Nissim. He was considered an authoritative <i>posek</i>, halakhic decision maker, and he received questions on Jewish law from Ereẓ Israel and Syria as well as from his own community. His main teacher was Perez ha-Kohen. Rabbenu Nissim served mainly as the head of the yeshivah in Barcelona.<br>Nissim wrote many halakhic works. The method and system he followed was established basically by Naḥmanides, Solomon b. Abraham Adret, Aaron ha-Levi of Barcelona, and their contemporaries although in his decisions, Nissim did not name the scholars whose sayings he quoted. He also added much of his own thought to the material he received from others. One of Nissim's main works was his commentary on the <i>halakhot</i> of Isaac Alfasi, a code based on the Talmud. His work is included in the margins of Alfasi's commentary in all printed versions of this work. This commentary is found with the following tractates (the section quoted by Isserles in this footnote is included): <i>Shabbat</i>, <i>Pesaḥim</i>, <i>Beẓah</i>, <i>Rosh HaShanah</i>, <i>Yoma</i>, <i>Ta'anit</i>, <i>Megillah</i>, <i>Sukkah</i>, <i>Ketubbot</i>, <i>Gittin</i>, <i>Kiddushin</i>, <i>Shevu'ot</i>, <i>Avodah Zarah</i>, <i>Ḥullin</i>, and <i>Niddah</i>. Nissim also wrote novellae to parts of the Talmud. The commentary that Rabbenu Nissim wrote to the Talmud tractate <i>Nedarim</i> is his best known work and his commentary has become the standard one instead of that by Rashi. Some of the commentaries attributed to Nissim might not be his, as the acronym, RaN, was used by many. Only a few of Nissim's responsa are still in existence along with a collection of twelve sermons and a commentary to the Pentateuch where he wanted to prove the superiority of prophecy and the Bible over philosophy. This was to give people strength and faith during difficult periods of persecution. Nissim also wrote <i>piyyutim</i>, liturgical poems, (see footnote 149).<br>Leon A. Feldman, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 1185-86.</i> the essence of the confession is “but we sinned”, (<span dir="ltr">טור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>; see footnote 23.</i></small>
Sins that were confessed about on the past Yom Kippur and one did not repeat them, nonetheless he may again confess them.
During the <i>Minḥah</i> Prayer on the Eve of Yom Kippur one does not conclude the Prayer with a confession after it.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: The reader does not repeat the confession during the <i>Minḥah</i> Prayer, rather he prays the <i>Amidah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shemoneh Esreh</i>, <span class="rtl">שמונה צשרה</span>, the eighteen benedictions, which is also called the <i>Amidah</i>, the Silent Prayer said in a standing position, and <i>Ha-Tefillah</i>, "The Prayer" <i>par excellence</i>; see footnote 43.</i> like on the rest of the days of the year, (<span dir="ltr">טור, ומרדכי</span>)<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">טור ומרדכי</span>:<br>For <i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>; see footnote 23.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.</i>. and does not say the prayer “<span dir="ltr">אבינו מלכנו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avinu Malkhenu</i>, <span class="rtl">אבינו מלכנו</span>, "Our Father, our King"; see footnote 15.</i>”, (see the end of chapter  614), and all the moreso not the prayers of supplication (<i>taḥanun</i>).<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taḥanun</i>, <span class="rtl">תחנון</span>, prayer of supplication; see footnote 10.</i></small>
The entire congregation (every Jewish member of the community) receives forty lashes<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Malkut Arba'im</i>, <span class="rtl">מלקות ארבעים</span>, forty lashes, the maximum biblical punishment for a transgression; see footnote 31.<br>The following comment is offered by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 607:8 - "Lashes": Not exactly forty (lashes) but rather thirty-nine.</i> after the <i>Minḥah</i> Prayer, for because of it he will take to heart to turn away (and repent) from his transgressions.<br><i>Hagah:</i> It was customary that the one being flogged say the confessions, <i>vidduim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vidduim</i>, <span class="rtl">וידוים</span>, the plural of <i>viddui</i>, confession of sins; see footnote 39.</i>, at the time that he was flogged, and that the flogger say, “And He (God) pities and will atone sins…” (Psalms 78:38), three times which equals thirty-nine words corresponding to the thirty-nine lashes<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Thirty-nine lashes, a biblical means of punishment for certain sins or a transgressions; see footnote 31.</i>, (this is a custom). And it is customary to whip with a little strap because this (flogging) is merely a remembrance to the genuine flogging. One should take a strap of calfskin, corresponding to the Biblical verse, “the ox knows his owner and the ass his master’s crib,” (Isaiah 1:3), (<span dir="ltr">כל בו</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kol Bo</i>, <span class="rtl">כל בו</span>; see footnote 34.</i> The one who is flogged shall not stand nor shall he sit, but he should be in a slanting position, (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> with his face to the north and his rear should be to the south, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> Yom Kippur only atones for the repenters who believe in its (Yom Kippur’s) atonement. However, one who despises it and thinks to himself, “how can this Yom Kippur help me,” Yom Kippur does not atone for him, (<span dir="ltr">רמב״ם פ״ג מהלכות שגגות</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Rambam, chapter three from "<i>Hilkhot Shegagot</i>" (The Laws of Transgressions committed Unintentionally), <span class="rtl">מהלכות שגגות רמב״ם פ״ג</span>.<br>Rambam is an acronym for <i>Ra</i>bbi <i>M</i>osheh <i>b</i>en <i>M</i>aimon. He is also known as Maimonides. This twelfth century intellectual is one of the most famous Jewish philosophers, halakhists, and writers of all times in addition to being a famous physician. His most famous work is called the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which means "The Repetition of the Law" or a second Torah. It is a code of Jewish law. Maimonides wanted to collect and organize Jewish law so it could be easily found and understood by people not as well versed as he was in the law. Maimonides' goal was to concentrate all of Jewish law from the Written Law until his time in a scientific and systematic way. His knowledge of and commentaries on halakhic material was phenomenally extensive. He wanted to subdivide and classify all of Jewish law according to subject matter which had not been done since the Mishna of Judah ha-Nasi. He divided his work into fourteen books (the letters, <span class="rtl">י״ד</span>, equal fourteen and therefore the alternate name for his work is <i>ha-Yad ha-Hazakah</i>, "The Strong Hand"). Each book has eighty-three further divisions called <i>halakhot</i>, the construct form being <i>hilkhot</i> (the reference made by Isserles in this footnote is to one of these divisions called <i>Hilkhot Shegagot</i>), these parts were further divided into one thousand chapters, <i>perakim</i>, made up of some fifteen thousand paragraphs, each called a <i>halakhah</i>. Maimonides gave a single halakhic rule in his work in clear, legal Hebrew without stating different opinions or the sources for his decisions except when they originated in the Torah. For this he is criticized. (For a further explanation of Maimonides and the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> as part of the whole of codification literature, see the introduction to this thesis.)<br>Maimonides' work as a halakhist was not limited to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, although that is his most famous legal work. He wrote commentaries to some tractates of the Talmud, and mention is also made to his commentaries on the Palestinian Talmud as well as the Mishna. His <i>Mishneh Torah</i> contains the whole of Jewish law, both practical and theoretical. Maimonides also wrote responsa where we learn of the life of the Jewish community in Egypt and its neighboring countries during his lifetime. There are 464 responsa of Maimonides in Hebrew and Arabic. Many of his decisions became the accepted <i>halakhah</i>. Another famous work of Maimonides is his <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i>, the "Book of the Commandments". In this halakhic work Maimonides decided to arrange the traditional 613 commandments in a new way. He gave his own enumeration of the 248 positive and the 365 negative commandments. The book, originally written in Arabic and translated into Hebrew by Moses ibn Tibbon, received much criticism for the methodology employed, but it later became an accepted work. The <i>Sefer ha-Mitzvot</i> served as an introduction to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which he worked on for ten years. Although the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> is an halakhic work, and even though Maimonides' philosophic work, <i>The Guide of the Perplexed</i>, was written after the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, the Rambam still managed to include philosophic ideas in his code. Maimonides felt that philosophy and science are handmaidens to theology. Mainmonides in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> included a system of metaphysics (Book One), the astronomical calculations for the calendar (Book Three), and the doctrine of the Messiah and a refutation of Christianity, Islam and their founders (Book Fourteen).<br>Even though the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> received much criticism by the contemporary scholars of Maimonides because it was such a novel way or arranging <i>halakhah</i>, since it did not give all the sources, and because it was feared that students would use it and no longer study the original talmudical sources, the book became one of the most creative sources of <i>halakhah</i> in all Jewish literature.<br>Jacob I. Dienstag, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 11, pp. 764-68.</i>

Siman 608

<b>“The order of the meal prior to the beginning of the fast” - Containing four paragraphs.</b><br>One eats and stops eating before dusk<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Bein ha-Shemashot</i>, <span class="rtl">בין השמשות</span>, is a time which is called twilight or dusk. It is that short period of time from when the sun just begins to set on the horizon until the stars (at least three) begin to appear in the sky, which marks nightime and signifies a new day according to the Hebrew calendar. It is defined as "Not quite (doubtful) day and not quite night" in the Talmud tractate <i>Shabbat</i> 34b.</i> because one must add “from the profane to the holy<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">"From the profane to the holy." It is a talmudic principle which operates on the Sabbath, festivals, therefore on Yom Kippur, that one starts the holiday a bit early, (and prolongs it a bit longer the following day) making it more than exactly twenty-four hours, so that one can increase the holiness that the holiday brings to one's life. Therefore one begins one's fast before the twilight so that one can add to the holiness of the Day of Atonement.</i>”, (i.e., from the weekday to Yom Kippur). And this addition has no specific time limit. But before dusk, the length of time which is one thousand, five hundred “seconds” prior to nightfall, one has to add from the profane (i.e., from the weekday) to the holy (i.e., Yom Kippur) either a little time or more time.
Women who eat and drink until it is dark, and they do not know that it is a good deed (<i>mitzvah</i>) to add “from the profane to the holy” should not be objected to doing so, so that they should not do it (later) presumptuously (defiantly).<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: The same is the rule in every forbidden thing, we say that it is better that they (the people) do it (the wrong thing) inadvertently rather than doing it presumptuously. But this is so only when the law in question is not explicitly stated in the Torah, even though it is a toraitic law. But if it is explicitly stated in the Torah, than they (the people) object (try to prevent) the person who wants to transgress, (<span dir="ltr">ר״ן פדק ד‘ דביצה והרא״ש בשם העיטור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim, chapter four in (his commentary on Alfasi tractate) <i>Beẓah</i>, and the <i>Rosh</i> in the name of the Ittur, <span class="rtl">ר״ן פרק ד‘ דביצה והרא״ש בשם העיטור</span>.<br>For Rabbenu Nissim, <span class="rtl">ר״ן</span>; see footnote 47.<br>The Talmud tractate <i>Beẓah</i> means literally "egg" and it is named such because this is the opening word, in a discussion of what happens when an egg is laid on a festival. It is found in the order of <i>Mo'ed</i> in the Mishna, Tosefta, Babylonian Talmud, and Palestinian Talmud. The tractate deals with the laws of festivals, especially those laws common to all festivals in general. This tractate is therefore also called <i>Yom Tov</i>, "festival". The tractate contains five chapters in the Mishna and Talmud but only four in the Tosefta.<br>Zvi Kaplan, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 4, pp. 785-86.<br>The Rosh, (Ha-Rosh), is an acronym for <i>R</i>abbenu A<i>sh</i>er. He is also known as Asheri. His real name is Asher ben Jehiel and he lived from around 1250 until 1327. This famous talmudist studied under his father, Jehiel, one of the Ḥasidei Ashkenaz and his older brother. He lived for a short while in Troyes, France and then lived in Cologne and Coblenz. From there he moved to Worms and studied under Meir b. Baruch of Rothenburg who appointed Asher as a member of the local rabbinic court, <i>bet din</i>. When Meir was imprisoned, Asher became the leader of German Jewry. During the Rindfleisch massacres of 1298 Asher distinguished himself by giving respected halakhic decisions which were raised due to the disruption of family and communal life.<br>Asher left Germany in 1303 and went to Barcelona. He was a colleague of Solomon b. Abraham Adret and in 1305 became the rabbi of Toledo. Asher fought for a long time the study of philosophy which had become popular. His dislike of philosophy was the result of what he saw on his move through different countries to Toledo. He saw that people were abandoning the study of Torah for the study of philosophy. He enacted a ban against the study of philosophy and against those practices which resulted from Christian influences.<br>The responsa of the Rosh reflect both the modesty and humility of Spanish scholars. Asher had a great deal to do with meshing German <i>minhag</i>, custom, with Spanish <i>halakhah</i>, law. Asher, an outstanding halakhic authority, thereby gave authority to the French and German codifiers. Rabbenu Asher was not afraid to disagree with the <i>rishonim</i> (early authorities, see footnote 16) or the <i>geonim</i> (see footnote 19) when the talmudic source disagreed with them. Asher was known to be lenient in matters not specifically forbidden in the Torah if it was for the sake of peace, but he was strict against developments that harmed communal life.<br>Asher wrote the following halakhic works: <i>Piskei ha-Rosh</i> (known also as <i>Hilkhot ha-Rosh</i> and <i>Sefer ha-Ashrei</i>), a work modeled after Alfasi. He summed up decisions of earlier codifiers and it covered most of the Talmud tractates; a collection of responsa, which was published in Constantinople in 1517, contains over one thousand responsa in one hundred, eight chapters. These responsa give a picture of what German and Spanish life was like during his time; commentaries on the <i>Mishnayot</i> to the orders of <i>Zera'im</i> and <i>Tohorot</i>; <i>Tosafot</i> which is basically what he taught at the yeshivah and covered virtually all the tractes of the Babylonian Talmud. (See the introduction to this thesis for a placing of Asher b. Jehiel and his work among the other codifiers of Jewish law.)<br><i>Encyclopaedia Hebraica</i>, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 3, pp. 706-08.<br><i>Sefer ha-Ittur</i> was written by Isaac ben Abba Mari of Marseilles who lived from approximately 1120 to approximately 1190. He was a rabbinic scholar in Provence and Spain who studied under his father Abba Mari b. Isaac. He later went to Barcelona as an honored and respected halakhic authority. He wrote a number of halakhic commentaries but he is best known for his <i>Sefer ha-Ittur</i> which is an encyclopedic work of the main halakhic laws which are of practical application. The first part deals with financial and divorce bills. It is arranged according to subject matter but following a mnemonic acrostic <i>Tashkef be-Geza Ḥokhmah</i>, <span class="rtl">תשקף בגזע חכמה</span>, "Consider the Root of Wisdom". Each letter represents a certain concept. "<span class="rtl">ת</span>" stands for <i>tenai</i>, "condition", "<span class="rtl">ש</span>" for <i>shover</i>, "receipt", and "<span class="rtl">ק</span>" for <i>kiyyum</i>, "authentication", etc. The second part includes laws for the preparation of meat, <i>sheḥitah</i> (animal slaughter for food), circumcision, tefillin (prayer phylacteries), marriage benedictions, <i>ẓiẓit</i> (prayer shawl), and a separate section entitled "Ten Commandments" which contains ten positive commandments which must be performed at specific times. This arrangement is unique to halakhic literature. Isaac b. Abba Mari used a vast knowledge of <i>geonic</i> (see footnote 19) literature in his work as well as decisions of scholars from Spain, Germany, and northern France. He made great use of the Jerusalem Talmud. <i>Sefer ha-Ittur</i> became an authoritative halakhic work for rabbinic authorities of Spain and Germany. Naḥmanides often made use of it.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 9, pp. 12-13.</i> But if one knows that his words would not be listened to he should not say publically that they (the women) should be admonished except once. But he should not increase the admonishing since he knows that they (the women) will not listen to him. However, in private a man is obligated to admonish him (the transgressor) until this transgressor beats him (the admonisher) or curses him, (<span dir="ltr">ר״ן ס״פ הבע״י</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim, the end of (the talmudic) chapter <i>Haba al Yevamot</i>, "One who has intercourse with one's sonless deceased brother's wife who is to marry the levir", <span class="rtl">ר״ן ס״פ הבע״י</span>. <i>Yevamot</i>, <span class="rtl">יבמות</span>, "Levirate Marriages" is the first tractate in the order of <i>Nashim</i> (women) in the Mishna, Tosefta, the Babylonian and Jerusalem Talmuds. The Mishna contains sixteen chapters and although it primarily deals with levirate marriage, it also contains other laws pertaining to women. The Tosefta has fourteen chapters. The laws are basically the same as in the Mishna but the order is different.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>. v. 16, pp. 775-78.<br>For Rabbenu Nissim, <span class="rtl">ר״ז</span>; see footnote 47.</i></small>
If one stops eating while still much of the day is left, one is able to go back and to eat at any time as long as he has not accepted upon himself the fast.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: See above, chapter  653 if he accepted the fast in his own heart, this is not (a legally valid acceptance) (without pronouncing the words publically).<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, chapter 653:1 which is among the laws of <i>Tishah be-Av</i> (the Ninth day of the month of Av) which is a fast day, one is permitted to eat again even after he has eaten the last meal before the fast (<i>Se'udah ha-mafseket</i>, see footnote 41) as long as he has not publically declared that he would not eat any more food that day until the actual fast day had begun. If a person did publically declare that he had begun his fast, then it is legally binding on him and he is not permitted any more food until the fast day is over except for reasons of health (which will be discussed below in chapters 617-18 of the translation of the text). According to Isserles, the laws of <i>Tishah be-Av</i> in this respect are the same as on Yom Kippur.</i></small>
On the Eve of Yom Kippur one only is to eat light foods that are easily digestable, so that one will not be satiated and feel well satisfied during prayer.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: And likewise one must not eat things that heat (stimulate) the body so that one does not come to an accidental emission, and thus one should not eat dairy food because it increases the semen, but for breakfast it is customary to eat (dairy products,) (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i></small>

Siman 609

<b>“Keeping food warm</b><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Keeping food warm on Yom Kippur refers to putting food in an insulated container that was usually a box of some type surrounded by wool or old clothes. It should <i>not</i> be assumed that food could be kept warm on Yom Kippur by means of a continual fire on Yom Kippur, which is not permitted.</i> <b>on the Eve of Yom Kippur” - Containing one paragraph.</b><br>It is permitted to keep food warm from the Eve of Yom Kippur until the end of Yom Kippur.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: There are those who say that one is not to keep food warm on Yom Kippur (see in the <i>Tur</i>, <span dir="ltr">טור</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>; see footnote 23.</i> and so it is the custom in these lands, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i></small>

Siman 610

<b>“The kindling of lights (lamps) on Yom Kippur” - Containing four paragraphs.</b><br>In a place where it is customary to kindle a light on the night of Yom Kippur, one does so;<sup class="footnote-marker">68a</sup><i class="footnote">The following comment is given by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 610:1 - "We light": So that one will not have intercourse by the light of the candle. And for those whose custom it is not to light (a candle in the bedroom) their reasoning is so that one will not see her (one's wife) and desire her.</i> in a place<sup class="footnote-marker">68b</sup><i class="footnote">The following comment is given by <i>Turei Zahav</i>, (see footnote 19a.): 610:1 - "In a place (location or town) where it was customary to light": And the two places (locations, towns) had the same one intention, to prevent intercourse. The one who lights (a candle), his intention is (to observe the law) that it is forbidden to have intercourse by the light of a candle; and he who does not light (a candle), his intention is that he should not see her (his wife) and desire her, even though it is forbidden for him to have intercourse with her, nevertheless he would not avoid thinking about her and this will avoid (nullify, prevent) his clean thoughts on this day (Yom Kippur).</i> where it is not customary to kindle a light, one does not do so. (If there is a lamp in the house one is obligated to kindle a light also in the room where he sleeps so that he does not due to over-stimulation have relations with his wife, since he sees her in the light (i.e., illuminating) of the house, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> (Marital relations must take place in total darkness). But if it (Yom Kippur) falls on the Sabbath, all are obligated to kindle lights (for the Sabbath).<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: And one blesses (finishes the blessing over the candles with) “to light the candle of Shabbat and Yom Kippur.”</small>
There is one who says that one blesses over the kindling of the light of Yom Kippur.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: And this is the custom in these lands.</small>
In every place we kindle lights in the synagogues and in the houses of study, in dark alleys and for the sick.
It is customary in all places to increase the (number of) lights in the synagogues and to spread nice cloths (i.e., carpets, curtains) in the synagogue.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: It is customary to kindle a light for every man whether he is old or young, (a great (important) or a small (unimportant) man), (<span dir="rtl">מרדכי דמהרי״ו</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> and <i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי ומהרי״ו</span>.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.<br>For <i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>; see footnote 27.</i> and also a memorial light<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">It is customary for an immediate relative; a husband, wife, mother, father, son, daughter, brother, or sister to light a memorial light which is to burn for twenty-four hours in honor of each anniversary of the death of that relative. One is also to recite the Mourner's <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177) during all services of the day on which the anniversary falls.</i> for his deceased father and mother, (<span dir="ltr">כל בו</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kol Bo</i>, <span class="rtl">כל בו</span>; see footnote 34.</i> And this is proper and thusly wrote some rabbis. If these lights went out on Yom Kippur, one should not say to a Gentile that he should relight them again, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהדי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> One whose light goes out on Yom Kippur should rekindle it after Yom Kippur and he should not extinguish it again, but rather leave it lighted until its completion. And also he should accept upon himself that all (the rest) of his days he should not extinguish his light on Yom Kippur, neither he nor someone else (so it is found in an old collection of customs).<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> There are those who say that (it is proper) to set (cover) nicely the tables on Yom Kippur like (they are covered) on the Sabbath (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי ומנהגים</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> and <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי ומנהגים</span>.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.<br>For <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> and so is the custom. There are those who wrote that it is customary to dress in clean, white clothes on Yom Kippur, analogous to the ministering angels, and likewise it is customary to wear a <i>kittel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>Kittel</i>, the German word for gown, is a white garment worn in some Ashkenazi rites by worshippers during the services on Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur. It is also worn by the <i>ḥazzan</i>, the chanter (cantor) of the prayer service, during the <i>Musaf</i> Service (see footnote 166) on <i>Shemini Aẓeret</i>, which is the eighth day of Sukkot (see footnote 248) when the recitation of the prayer for rain is begun, and on the first day of Passover, when the recitation of the prayer for dew commences. There are some communities where the <i>kittel</i> is also worn by the person who conducts the Passover <i>seder</i> and by the bridegroom during the wedding ceremony. The color white has always been associated with purity, and here, with forgiveness of sins. There is solemn joy when the <i>kittel</i> is worn. The day of marriage is considered like a day of atonement and the <i>kittel</i> has been associated with the idea of atonement and penitence which is also associated with death. Therefore the dead are clothed in a white <i>kittel</i>.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, p. 1079.</i> which is white and clean, and it is also the clothing of the dead (the shroud), and therefore the heart of a man is humbled and broken, (<span dir="ltr">ד״ע מהגהות מיימוני פ״ז דשביתת עשור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Da'at Aẓmo</i>, <span class="rtl">ד״ע</span>, Isserles' own opinion (see footnote 38). Maimonides, chapter seven, of <i>Shvi'tat Asor</i>, "Resting of the Tenth Day (of Tishrei)" which is a synonym for the Laws of Yom Kippur, <span class="rtl">ד״צ מהגהות מיימוני פ״ז דשביתת עשור</span>.<br>Isserles has based this comment on the Notes on Maimonides, <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> (see footnote 27) from the seventh chapter of those notes which are included in the section of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which concerns the Laws of the Day of Atonement, the day falling on the Tenth Day of the month of Tishrei, which is the reason for the synonym given to this section.</i></small>

Siman 611

<b>“That the night of Yom Kippur has the same laws as the day” - Containing two paragraphs.</b><br>On Yom Kippur, its night is the same as its day in all matters; and what are its forbidden things on it: working, eating, drinking, washing, annointing, wearing sandles (leather), intercourse. But one is not guilty of the <i>karet</i> penalty<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Karet</i>, <span class="rtl">כרת</span>, means "extripation" which is a punishment at the hands of heaven mentioned in the Bible. It served as a penalty for numerous sins which were committed deliberately such as idolatry, desecration of the Sabbath, eating leaven on Passover, incest, adultery, and eating some forbidden foods. For such offences, the person need not be given a previous warning as the sin is so basic and severe. The <i>halakhah</i> defines <i>karet</i> as premature death (Sifra, <i>Emor</i> 14:14) and <i>baraita</i> (Mk. 28a; TJ, Bik. 2:1, 64b) state it as "death at the age of fifty". Some <i>amoriam</i> refer to it as death between the ages of fifty and sixty. The Mishna (Ker. 1:1) lists thirty-six transgressions mentioned in the Torah for which <i>karet</i> is punishment. The word <i>karet</i> has also become the standard for many severe violations of the <i>halakhah</i>. <i>Karet</i> has to be for a deliberate act. If the transgression was done inadvertently, only a sin-offering is required. The punishment of <i>karet</i> is divine, therefore God knows if it was deliberate or not and there need not be any witnesses.<br>There is discussion among the <i>tannaim</i> whether or not the penalty of <i>karet</i> exempts a person from flogging (see footnote 31) which is the penalty for most toraitic law violations where the person is warned. The decision is that one should be flogged and repentence can annul a <i>karet</i> (Mak. 23a-b). Even though a transgressor may be punished by <i>karet</i>, he is still liable for any civil claims that may have arisen from his actions (Ket. 30a).<br>It was believed that natural death took place after sixty and if one died before that or one's life span was in any way curtailed this was considered "death by the hand of heaven" even though it had no fixed time. There was also discussion regarding the speed of death. Death in one, two, or three days before the age of sixty, or for those committing a transgression after the age of sixty was considered a sign of <i>karet</i>.<br>The medieval scholars who were very concerned with reward and punishment decided that a death from <i>karet</i> meant the absolute end, the maximal punishment, while ordinary sinners after being punished in Gehinnom, lived again in the world to come, a world not shared by those punished with <i>karet</i>. This was the view of Maimonides while Naḥmanides felt that those who died by <i>karet</i> continued to suffer in the world to come since the soul can never be annihilated and perish. <i>Karet</i> was degradation of the soul and the negation of spiritual pleasures awaiting the souls of the righteous.<br>Israel Moses Ta-Shma, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, pp. 788-89.</i> except for working, eating, and drinking.
All (types of) work (for whose transgression) a person is guilty on the Sabbath is also guilty on Yom Kippur. And all types of work for whose transgression on the Sabbath a person is free from punishment but are still prohibited, are also prohibited on Yom Kippur in a like manner except that on the Sabbath a conscious sin is punishable by stoning,<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Stoning, <i>sekilah</i>, <span class="rtl">סקילה</span>, is a Biblical form of capital punishment which was the standard penalty for crime in all ancient civilizations. In the Torah there are two explicit methods of executing a criminal or a sinner; stoning and burning. According to the Talmud, the Torah has four methods of execution: stoning, burning, beheading, and strangling, (See B. Sanhedrin 49b., ff).<br>Stoning was an instinctive violent expression of popular wrath, (Exodus 17:4, 8:22; Numbers 14:10; I Samuel 30:6; I Kings 12:18; II Chronicles 10:18) and often in the Bible it is the prescribed mode of execution (Leviticus 20:2, 27, 24:16; Numbers 15:35; Deuteronomy 13:11, 17:5, 21:21, 22:21, et al). Originally, the whole community participated in the stoning and were required to throw stones at the guilty person. Stonings were probably the standard form of judicial execution in Biblical times, (Leviticus 24:23; Numbers 15:36; I Kings 21:13; II Chronicles 24:21).<br>The Mishna (Sanh.6:4) states that a "stoning place" was established where instead of a person being pelted by stones, the convicted person would be pushed down from a high place to his death provided it was not too high so as to mutilate the body which was a concern of the rabbis. It also was not to be too low so the death would be instantaneous. The reason for the stoning place was that the scriptural rule states "The hands of the witnesses shall be first upon him to put him to death," (Deuteronomy 17:7) and then afterwards the "hand of all the people (should be on him)", (Deuteronomy 17:7). To insure that the witnesses put him to death they were the ones to push him and thereby be first to put him to death. Thus this method of "stoning" became acceptable as opposed to the actual throwing of stones. This also seemed the more "humane" way of carrying out capital punishment as the convicted person died more quickly and the danger of mutilation was reduced. In Maimonides' comment to Sanhedrin 6:4 he stated that it really made no difference if stones were thrown at one or if one were thrown at stones.<br>cf., Haim Hermann Cohn, v. 5, pp. 142-43.</i> but on Yom Kippur it is punishable by <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Karet</i>, <span class="rtl">כרת</span>; see footnote 78.</i> Everything that is forbidden to be moved (or handled) on the Sabbath is also forbidden to be moved (or handled) on Yom Kippur, (but it is permitted to clean vegetables and to open (shell) nuts from the <i>Minḥah</i> time<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minḥah</i>, <span class="rtl">מנחה</span>, a time designating afternoon, meaning after the six and one half hour or after 12:30 P.M. according to our present day time system. (See footnote 40 for a more complete explanation).</i> onward when (Yom Kippur) falls on a weekday, but nowadays (presently) it is customary to forbid that.)<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: If a fire occurs on Yom Kippur, it is permitted to save one meal for the need of the night (following Yom Kippur) as one (may) save on the Sabbath the afternoon meal,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The afternoon meal on the Sabbath is also referred to as <i>Se'udah Shelishit</i>, the third meal which is eaten on the Sabbath between the <i>Minḥah</i>, Afternoon Service (see footnote 40) and the <i>Ma'ariv</i>, Evening Service (see footnote 144).</i> (<span dir="ltr">ר״ן פרק כל כתבי</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbenu Nissim on the chapter <i>Kol Khit-vey</i>, <span class="rtl"> כתבי ר״ן פרק כל</span>, which is a commentary on the talmudic tractate <i>Shabbat</i>.<br>For Rabbenu Nissim, <span class="rtl">ר״ן</span>; see footnote 47.</i> and it is already explained in section 334<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">See in the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, chapter 334 which contains twenty-seven paragraphs on the laws that apply when a fire breaks out on the Sabbath. These same laws apply regarding a fire, according to Isserles to Yom Kippur.</i> (as to) how to act at this time when a fire (occurs) on the Sabbath and the same law (applies) for Yom Kippur. It is customary that the children play with nuts (<span dir="ltr">אגודה ומהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Agudah</i> and <i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">אגודה ומהרי״ל</span>.<br>The <i>Agudah</i>, <span class="rtl">אגודה</span>, is a collection of halakhic decisions derived from talmudic discussions and arranged in the order of the talmudic tractates. It was written by Alexander Suslin ha-Kohen of Frankfort who died in 1349. The <i>Agudah</i> was published in Cracow in 1571 and it also included novellae of his own as well as those of his predecessors, and a commentary and collection of <i>halakhot</i> to the minor tractates and to the Mishnayot of the orders <i>Zera'in</i> and <i>Tohorot</i>. The language of the <i>Agudah</i> is very concise and it is evident that it was written quickly under the threat of the persecutions of the time since Suslin died a martyr's death in Erfurt. Suslin was the last of the early German halakhic authorities. This German talmudic scholar was born Erfurt where he taught, as well as in Worms, Cologne, and Frankfort.<br>The <i>Agudah</i>, Suslin's most famous work, gives halakhic rulings in concise form and it ignores differences of opinion. He used as sources Mordecai b. Hillel and Asher b. Jehiel. It is often necessary to consult the work of these two scholars to understand fully the <i>Agudah</i>. Jacob Weil (see footnote 27) wrote a digest to the work called <i>Ḥiddushei Agudah</i> which was published in Venice in 1523 and accompanies the <i>Agudah</i>. Later halakhic authorities such as Jacob ha-Levi Moellin (see footnote 8) and Moses Isserles considered his decisions authoritative and they quoted from him. Isserles mentioned the <i>Agudah</i> often in his glosses to the Shulḥan Arukh.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 2, p. 585.<br><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> One should not object to them (the children) even before the Afternoon Prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minḥah</i>, <span class="rtl">מנחה</span>; see footnote 40.</i>, and the custom is widespread with respect to the mentioned law of breaking nuts, (<span dir="ltr">ד״ע</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Da'at Aẓmo</i>, <span class="rtl">ד״ע</span>, Isserles' own opinion; see footnote 38.</i></small>

Siman 612

<b>“It is forbidden to eat on Yom Kippur, and the measure of its quantity” - Containing ten paragraphs.</b><br>One who eats on Yom Kippur as much as the size of a big date is guilty of the punishment of <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Karet</i>, <span class="rtl">כרת</span>; see footnote 78.</i> (The amount of a big date) is a little less than (the quantity of) an egg, and this quantity applies equally to everybody, whether a dwarf (midget) or whether (one is the size of) Og, King of Bashan.<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Og, King of Bashan was a very large, fat individual who lived in what is today the Golan Heights.</i>
All the food (that one eats) is combined to this measure (of less than a date), even salt that is on meat and juice that is on a vegetable, but eating and drinking are not being combined (they do not add together to get the quantity of a date, they are measured separately).
One who ate and ate again, if from the beginning of the first eating until the end of the last eating there was so much time as is needed to eat half a loaf of bread, then the food is being combined (to the above measure which was a date), but if not (if it took him longer than the time needed to eat half a loaf of bread) it is not combined (to the size of a date).
The (time) measure for eating a half a loaf of bread - some say it equals four eggs, and there are those who say it equals three eggs, (<span dir="ltr">״שוחקות״ רשב״א</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Soḥakot</i> (<i>Rashba</i>), (<span class="rtl">רשב״א</span>) <span class="rtl">שוחקות</span>, the word <i>Soḥakot</i> is a talmudic term of measure meaning large eggs.
