"Blessed art Thou, O Lord our God, King of the Universe, who hast kept us in life, and has preserved us, and enabled us to reach this season."</i>” without a cup (of wine).<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Normally when the <i>Sheheḥayanu</i> (see footnote 142) prayer is recited at a festival, it can be said with a cup of wine, but since Yom Kippur is a fast day, the wine is not permitted.</i><br><small><i>Hagah</i>: And afterwards they say the Evening Prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">The Evening Prayer <i>Arvit</i>, <span class="rtl">ערבית</span>, is also referred to as the <i>Ma'ariv</i> Service, which is the word at the beginning and end of the first blessing before the <i>Shema</i>, (see footnote 17 and 173). Originally the <i>Arvit</i> Service was an optional one since there was not an evening Temple sacrifice that corresponded to it as was the case with the <i>Shaḥarit</i> (see footnote 17), <i>Minḥah</i> (see footnote 40) and <i>Musaf</i> (see footnote 166) Service. Traditionally this Service was attributed to Jacob who prayed and Evening Service (Genesis 28:11).<br>The <i>Arvit</i> Service basically consists of a <i>Barekhu</i> which is a call to worship followed by the <i>Shema</i> and its benedictions and the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). After nightfall Psalm 134 begins the Service. On weekdays the Service begins with Psalms 78:38 and 20:10.<br>The blessings around the <i>Shema</i> are a bit different for the <i>Arvit</i> Service. The theme of the first blessing before the <i>Shema</i> is the change from evening to night and the second blessing is one of thanksgiving for the love shown by God for Israel in revealing his Torah. The blessing which follows the <i>Shema</i> in the <i>Arvit</i> Service is a <i>Ge'ullah</i> prayer which praises God as the Redeemer who redeemed Israel from Egyptian slavery. This is all followed by a special night prayer called the <i>Hashkivenu</i>, "Grant us to lie down in peace", which asks for God's help and protection from various mishaps and dangers that can happen in the mysterious night.<br>There were two versions of the final prayer, a Babylonian and a Palestinian. The Babylonian version is now used on weekdays and it speaks of God "who guards His people Israel forever." The Palestinian version is used on Sabbaths and festivals which is a prayer for peace and Zion; God "who spreads out the tabernacle of peace".<br>In the Ashkenazi rite several scriptural verses beginning with Psalm 89:53, "blessed by the Lord for evermore", are inserted between the <i>Hashkivenu</i> and the <i>Amidah</i>. The Sephardi rite does not have this.<br>The <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43) during the <i>Arvit</i> Service is only prayed silently. It is not repeated by the reader as it is in the other Services. The <i>Amidah</i> is preceded by a half reader's <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177) and it is followed by the full reader's <i>Kaddish</i>. The prayer, <i>Aleinu le-Shabbe'aḥ</i> (see footnote 182) concludes the Service.<br>On the evenings of Sabbaths and festivals there are a few changes in the <i>Arvit</i> Service. On the Sabbath it is preceded by a special set of prayers and Psalms which welcome the Sabbath, called <i>Kabbalat Shabbat</i>. The <i>Amidah</i> changes to the special Sabbath <i>Amidah</i> of only seven benedictions. Also a <i>Kiddush</i>, a blessing over wine, is also inserted into the Service. At the conclusion of the Sabbath a special <i>Havdalah</i> (see footnote 226) section is added to the fourth benediction of the <i>Amidah</i> and readings are added to the end of the Service. The <i>Arvit</i> Service usually follows the <i>Minḥah</i> Service immediately after sunset, but it can be recited up until dawn, and under special circumstances, even as late as after twilight.<br>Alexander Carlebach, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 3, pp. 664-66.</i> It is customary to recite “<i>Kol Nidrei</i><sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kol Nidrei</i>, <span class="rtl">כל נדרי</span>; see footnote 141.</i>” while it was still day and to lengthen it with melodies until nightfall, and (“<i>Kol Nidrei</i>”) is said three times, and each time (the cantor) raises his voice “higher” (says it louder) than before, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> And likewise the reader says the following prayer sentence three times; “And the entire congregation will be forgiven, (etc.)<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">This is a prayer from Numbers 15:26 and 14:19-20 which follows the chanting of the <i>Kol Nidrei</i> (see footnote 141).<br>Ben Zion Bokser. <i>The High Holyday Prayer Book</i>, New York, Hebrew Publishing Company, 1959, p.259.</i>” And the congregation says three times, “And God said I forgave you according to your word”, (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> A man must not deviate from the custom of his city even in the melodies or <i>piyyutim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Piyyutim</i>, <span class="rtl">פיוטים</span>, is a Hebrew word derived from Greek which means a lyrical composition which was intended to embellish an obligatory prayer in the liturgy, or any other religious service whether communal or private. The word refers to liturgical compositions in Hebrew from the first century of the Common Era until the beginning of the Enlightenment, the <i>Haskalah</i>. Originally <i>piyyutim</i> meant to replace the set versions of prayers, expressing the same basic ideas, mainly on the Sabbath and festivals. When prayers became fixed, <i>piyyutim</i> were inserted into or around a set prayer. Most <i>piyyutim</i> were used to adorn and make beautiful the holy days, but there are many which were written for the Sabbaths, fast days, and even weekdays. There are also <i>piyyutim</i> for weddings, curcumcisions and mourning.<br><i>Piyyutim</i> are characterized from regular prayers by their lofty style and rythm. We know the authors of many <i>piyyutim</i> while others remain anonymous. <i>Piyyutim</i> were produced at one time or another in every land where Jews lived, each area producing its own style. There are <i>Kerovah</i> types of <i>piyyutim</i> which usually are found in the <i>Amidah</i> prayer and <i>Yoẓer piyyutim</i> found in the benediction before and after the <i>Shema</i> in the Morning Service (see footnote 17 and 173). <i>Piyyutim</i> used in the <i>Amidah</i> of <i>Musaf</i> and <i>Arvit</i> for Sabbath and holy days are called <i>shivata</i> because of this <i>Amidah</i> having seven (<i>Shevah</i>) blessings. The Morning Service <i>Amidah</i> for Sabbaths and holy days contains a sanctification prayer, therefore the <i>piyyutim</i> associated there are called <i>Kedushata</i> (sanctification is <i>Kedushah</i> in Hebrew, see footnote 213). Different holy days have special <i>Kerovot piyyutim</i> associated with the characteristic of the holiday. On Yom Kippur the special section of the service describing the Temple Sacrificial Service has many <i>piyyutim</i> associated with the <i>Seder ha-Avodah</i> (see footnote 22). There are many <i>seliḥot piyyutim</i> for the fast days (see footnote 14).<br>Styles and vocabulary of the various <i>piyyutim</i> stretch Hebrew to its fullest in creativity and made the language rich. Many <i>piyyutim</i> are difficult to understand because of the freedom of style and vocabulary that was employed. The first <i>piyyutim</i> only used rhythm but later rhyme also played an important role in the <i>piyyutim</i> especially in Spain. Some have no specific poetical characteristics.<br>Ezra Fleischer, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 13. pp. 573-602.</i> that are said there, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i></small>
On the Night of Yom Kippur and on the next day we say (the blessing), “Blessed be His name whose glorious kingdom is forever and ever” in a loud voice (where normally it is said in a wisper).<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">This is the blessing that is said in response by the congregation to the <i>Barekhu</i>, which is the call to worship made by the reader. In all other services it is said in a wisper, but on Yom Kippur night and during the day it is said outloud by the congregation.<br>The following comment is given by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 619:8 - "Outloud": Since all year we say it in a wisper because Moses stole it (the response) from the angels, but on Yom Kippur, Israel also resembles the angels, (<i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>, see footnote 23).</i>
If (Yom Kippur) falls on the Sabbath we say “<span dir="ltr">ויכולו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The prayer which begins Va'yekhulu comes from Genesis 2:1-3. It is recited on the Sabbath because it tells the account of God's creation of the seventh day as a day of rest. It is normally recited just before the Sabbath <i>Kiddush</i>, (<i>Kiddush ha-Yom</i>, sanctification of the day; i.e., the Sabbath). There of course is no <i>Kiddush</i> on a fast day such as Yom Kippur, because it is always connected with drinking wine or eating bread.<br>Bokser, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., p. 275.</i>” after the Silent Prayer,<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">For an explanation of the Silent Prayer, the <i>Amidah</i> see footnote 43.</i> and the prayer “<span dir="ltr">תפילת מעין שבע</span><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tefilat Me'ein Sheva</i>, <span class="rtl">תפילת מעין שבע</span>, is a prayer recited on the Sabbath which is one prayer which summarizes the Sabbath <i>Amidah</i>, containing seven (<i>Sheva</i>, <span class="rtl">שבע</span>) blessings. The substance (<i>Me'ein</i>, <span class="rtl">מעין</span>) of the longer prayer is abbreviated in this short prayer. The ideas of the normal seven benedictions of the Sabbath evening <i>Amidah</i> are included in the prayer.</i>” and conclude (the prayer with the words), “<span dir="ltr">מקדש השבת</span><sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The prayer concludes with the words <i>Mekadesh ha-Shabbat</i>, <span class="rtl">מקדש השבת</span>.</i>”, (“who makes the Sabbath holy”), but do not mention Yom Kippur.<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">There is no special mention in the prayer which states the fact that it is the Day of Atonement, only the fact that it is the Sabbath.<br>There is mention of the fact that it is a high holyday in the <i>Birkhat ha-Mazon</i> if one must eat on Yom Kippur and thus one must say the Grace if he is able. See footnote 138.</i> (We do not say “<span dir="ltr">אבינו מלכנו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avinu Malkhenu</i>, <span class="rtl">אבינו מלכנו</span>, "Our Father, our King"; see footnote 15.</i>” on the Sabbath, but the rest of the prayers for forgiveness<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Seliḥot</i>, <span class="rtl">סליחות</span>, prayers for forgiveness; see footnote 14.</i> and the supplication prayers<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taḥanun</i>, <span class="rtl">תחנון</span>; supplication prayers; see footnote 10.</i> one says like on a weekday, (<span dir="ltr">ריב״ש סימן תקיב ומנהגים</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Isaac ben Sheshet Perfet, chapter 512, and <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">ריב״ש סימן תקי״ב דמנהגים</span>.<br><i>R</i>abbi <i>I</i>saac <i>b</i>en <i>Sh</i>eshet Perfet was also known by his acronym <i>Ribash</i>, <span class="rtl">ריב״ש</span>. He lived in Spain from 1326 until 1408. This halakhic authority was born in Barcelona and he studied under Perez ha-Kohen, Ḥasdai b. Judah Crescas and Nissim b. Reuben Gerondi. He moved to Saragossa after he was imprisoned in Barcelona, but his life in his new home was also full of controversy and family tragedy.<br>In Saragossa he tried to abolish certain local customs he did not agree with and he won the anger of many local scholars. He as a result finally moved to Valencia in 1385 where he served as the rabbi. In 1391 there were anti-Jewish riots which forced him to move to North Africa, and he settled in Algiers where he became the communal rabbi and was well respected even though at first his appointment was challenged by Simeon b. Ẓemaḥ Duran (<i>Tashbaẓ</i>, <span class="rtl">תשב״ץ</span>; see footnote 20).<br>Perfet's most important work was his responsa which was published in Constantinople in 1546. His responsa influenced the <i>halakhah</i> after him and Caro used Perfet's decisions extensively in the Shulḥan Arukh. In addition to the vast amount of halakhic material, the responsa also reveal much about the life and customs of the Jews of Spain and North Africa in the fourteenth century. Perfet was the first to discuss the status of the Marranos (Spanish Jews who converted to Christianity so as not to be expelled in 1492) from an halakhic point of view. This became a quite crucial and painful problem for the Jews of Spain and North Africa. Perfet also wrote commentaries on many talmudic tractates and the Pentateuch. He refused to be associated with Kabbalah and even though he knew philosophy, he opposed its study including the works of Maimonides.<br>Hirsch Jacob Zimmels, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 9, pp. 32-33.<br>For <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i>).
It is necessary to make one man stand to the right side of the reader and one to his left side (while he is saying these prayers).<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">While the reader is saying these prayers on the night of Yom Kippur he is to be flanked on both sides.</i>
There are people who stand<sup class="footnote-marker">161a</sup><i class="footnote">The following comment is given by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 619:10 - "Those who stand": This is at the hour of the <i>Arvit</i> Prayer, (see footnote 144), but one who stands throughout the whole night will not be able to pray with devotion during the day (<i>Beit Yosef</i>, <span class="rtl">בית יוסף</span>, see footnote 20). They should be careful not to hold back when they need to go to the bathroom; and if they become weak they are able to lean on anything, (<i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>, see footnote 8, and <i>Darkhei Moshe</i>, <span class="rtl">ד״מ</span>, see footnote 6). See the beginning of 585, (in the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, and the reason for standing is to be similar to the angels, and therefore women should not stand, and this is what is written at the end of chapter 610 (of the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>), (<i>Matteh Moshe</i>, <span class="rtl">מט״מ</span>; this book is a compendium of Jewish ritual law completed in 1584. It was written by Moses Mat who lived from 1551 until 1606. He was a Galician rabbi was born in Przemysl. He was a disciple of Solomon Luria. Mat headed the yeshivah in Pesemysl and later lived in Lyuboml and Opatow. He was one of the leading rabbis of Poland and also wrote <i>Taryag Mitzvot</i>, which is a versification of the 613 commandments. He wrote a commentary to the Pentateuch which was actually a supercommentary to the commentary by Rashi. He is quoted often by his contemporary Joel Sirkes, author of the <i>Bayit Ḥadash</i>, <span class="rtl">ב״ח</span>, see footnote 20b.); Tovia Preschel, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 11, pp. 1120-21).</i> on their feet the entire day and the whole night.
It is customary to spend the night in the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">161b</sup><i class="footnote">The following is a comment given by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 619:11 - "To sleep in the synagogue": <i>Levush</i>, <span class="rtl">לבוש</span>, (see footnote 20a.) wrote that it is better to go to sleep in one's house because he who stays awake at night will sleep during the day, and he will not say with conviction (his prayers). If (he stays awake all night) so as to guard the candles (so the synagogue does not burn down) he should hire a guard instead. It was written in the <i>Shenei Luḥot ha-Berit</i>, <span class="rtl">של״ה</span>, (see footnote 20a.), that before one goes to sleep one should say the first four Psalms which is to guard against (nocturnal) pollution.</i> and to recite poems and songs of praise all night.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: It is better to sleep far from the ark (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.</i> and whoever does not want to recite songs of praise and poems should not sleep there (in the synagogue), (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ו</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>; see footnote 27.</i> The chanters (cantors) who pray the entire day should not stay awake all night because they might ruin their voices if they do not sleep, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.<br>165. <i>Shaḥarit</i>, <span class="rtl">שחרית</span>, the Morning Service; see footnote 17.</i></small>

Siman 620

<b>“It is a good custom to shorten the Morning Prayer” - Containing one paragraph.</b><br>It is good to shorten the poems and the penitential prayers of the Morning Service so as to speed up the matter so that one can pray the Additional Service<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Musaf</i>, <span class="rtl">מוסף</span>, the Additional Service; is a special section of prayers that is added to the Morning Service on Sabbaths, festivals, and New Moons which corresponds to the additional sacrifice that was made on these days in the Temple. In the Bible (Numbers 28-29) additional offerings are prescribed to be brought on Sabbaths, the three pilgrimage festivals, Sukkot, Pesaḥ, and Shavuot, Rosh HaShanah, Yom Kippur, and Rosh Ḥodesh (New Moons). Talmud tractate <i>Yoma</i> 33a states that this Additional Sacrifice was to be made after the regular Morning Sacrifice. After the Temple was destroyed the <i>Musaf</i> Service found its place after the <i>Shaḥarit</i> (see footnote 17) Service in the prayers on these Sabbaths and festivals. The <i>Musaf</i> Service now comes immediately after the Torah Service, where the Torah and <i>Haftarah</i> (see footnote 170) are read, which follows the Morning Prayers on Sabbaths, festivals, and New Moons. <i>Musaf</i> can be recited anytime during the day, but one who recites it after the seventh hour (1:00 P.M. according to our time system except on Yom Kippur, see footnote 167) is considered a transgressor.<br>The <i>Musaf</i> Service begins with the reader's recitation of the half-<i>Kaddish</i>, or praise of God (see footnote 177), which is followed by the <i>Musaf Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). This <i>Amidah</i> normally consists of seven benedictions, the first and last three being the same as is always said, and the middle benediction known as <i>Kedushat ha-Yom</i>, "Sanctity of the Day". In the case of the <i>Musaf</i> Service the middle benediction has an introductory paragraph followed by a prayer for the restoration of the Temple Service, and it concludes with the section from the Torah which deals with the particular Additional Sacrifice made on that Sabbath or festival.<br>On Rosh HaShanah the <i>Musaf Amidah</i> takes on a unique configuration. It has three central benedictions, thus making a total of nine benedictions in the <i>Amidah</i>. The three benedictions cover the theme of the holy day, <i>malkhuyyot</i> (kingships), <i>zikhronot</i> (remembrances), and <i>shofarot</i> (Ram's Horns, see footnote 221).<br>The <i>Musaf Amidah</i> after being recited silently is repeated outloud by the reader (see footnote 42). On the Sabbath, the <i>Musaf Amidah</i> is made up of twenty-two verses following the Hebrew alphabet backwards, and a description of the <i>Musaf</i> Sacrifice found in Numbers 28:9-10. The <i>Musaf Amidah</i> takes on a slightly different form for each festival. On the Day of Atonement, the <i>Amidah</i> opens with the usual three benedictions. This is followed by the description of the Additional Sacrifices made on Yom Kippur found in Numbers 29:7-8. After this a prayer for the forgiveness of sins is found. The Confession, the <i>Al Ḥet</i> and <i>Ashamnu</i> Prayers (see footnote 45), form the most important part of the <i>Amidah</i> as they do in the other <i>Amidot</i> of Yam Kippur. Various <i>piyyutim</i> (see footnote 149) are added in the reader's repetition of the <i>Musaf Amidah</i> on Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur.<br>Aaron Rothkoff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 532-34.</i> before the seventh hour (1:00 P.M.).<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">In halakhic literature the daylight hours were divided up into twelve equal parts regardless of how short or long the day was at various times of the year. In rough terms we can think of daylight beginning at 6:00 A.M. and ending at 6:00 P.M. Therefore the first hour of the day, in halakhic terms begins at 6:00 A.M. and ends at 7:00 A.M. We need then only add six to the halakhic hour to get the approximate corresponding time in our system. Therefore the seventh hour for us would be seven plus six, which is thirteen o'clock or, in other words 1:00 P.M.</i>

Siman 621

<b>“The order of the reading of the Torah and of circumcision on Yom Kippur” - Containing six paragraphs.</b><br>We take out (from the ark) two Torah scrolls.<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Two Torah scrolls are taken out on the festivals because portions from two separate sections of the Torah are read. The Torahs can be set before hand so that they can be opened to the correct portion without the necessity of rolling the scroll from one portion to the next.</i> In the first Torah six men read from the portion “<span dir="ltr">אחרי מות</span>”, (Leviticus 16:1-18:30) until “and he did as the Lord commanded (Moses)”, (Leviticus 16:34). But if (Yom Kippur) falls on Shabbat, seven (men read from the first Torah), and the <i>Maftir</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maftir</i>, <span class="rtl">מפטיר</span>, means literally "one who concludes". It is the name given to the man who is the last to read in the Torah and he also usually reads the <i>haftarah</i> (see footnote 170), the section of the prophets that corresponds to the Torah reading. <i>Maftir</i> is also the name given to the three or more concluding verses of the regular weekly Torah portion as well as to the final verses read on festivals and public fast days.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 11, p. 685.</i> (the last reader) reads from the second (Torah scroll) from the portion, <i>Pinḥas</i>, (Numbers 25:10-30:1), the section “and you shall have on the tenth day of this seventh month”, (Numbers 29:7-11). The <i>Maftir</i> (the <i>Haftarah</i> section from the Prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Haftarah</i>, <span class="rtl">הפטרה</span>, is a portion from the Prophets section of the Bible read after the Torah is read on Sabbaths, festivals, and fast days. On Sabbaths and festivals the <i>haftarah</i> is read during the Morning, <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service (see footnote 17), but on fast days it is read only during the Afternoon, <i>Minḥah</i> Service (see footnote 40). The exception to this is Yom Kippur and <i>Tishah be-Av</i> (see footnote 102) where there is a <i>haftarah</i> after the Torah reading in both the Morning and the Afternoon Service.<br>The Torah in its regular portions is read straight through during the year but such is not the case on festivals and some special Sabbaths. The <i>haftarot</i> are selected in parts from both the Former and Latter Prophets. Only two prophetic books are read in their entirety as <i>haftarot</i>, the Book of Obadiah which has only twenty-one verses and is read after the Torah portion <i>Va-Yishlaḥ</i> (Genesis 32:4-36-43) according to the Sephardi rite, and the Book of Jonah which is the <i>haftarah</i> for the <i>Minḥah</i> Service on Yom Kippur (see the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> 622:2).<br><i>Haftarot</i> were usually selected so there would be some similarity in content between the Pentateuchal and the Prophetic portions, but often this did not happen and <i>haftarot</i> were chosen because of historical events or because of some special date. Special <i>haftarot</i> are read on special Sabbaths and the <i>haftarah</i> for each festival is based on the nature of the festival.<br>When the custom of reading the <i>haftarah</i> got started is not known for sure, but it is thought that it began during the persecutions of the Antiochus Epiphanes which preceded the Hasmonean revolt. The Torah was not permitted to be read by the Jews during the persecution for it was felt that the reading of it kept the Jews together and gave them a special strength. As a substitute for the Torah reading, sections form the Prophets were chosen that would remind the Jews of the corresponding Torah portion. Appearantly when the ban against reading the Torah was lifted, the practice of reading the <i>haftarah</i> continued. The first mention of the practice of the reading of the <i>haftarah</i> is found in the New Testament. Acts 13:15 states, "after the reading of the law and the prophets". <i>Haftarot</i> are also discussed in the Talmud as to which are to be read at specific times and festivals. In Mishnaic times different communities read different <i>haftarot</i>, and a set order was probably not established until talmudic times. Some <i>haftarot</i> today differ from those recorded in the Talmud, and there are differences in the Sephardi and Ashkenazi rites.<br>The <i>maftir</i>, the one who reads the <i>haftarah</i> also reads the last part of the weekly portion, (i.e., the Torah reader reads it for him). On the Sabbath, after the seventh reader from the Torah, the <i>maftir</i> usually rereads the last three verses of the weekly portion. On festivals and the four special Sabbaths, the <i>maftir</i> reads the special section from the second scroll which is usually a short description of of the festival found in the Torah. Before the <i>haftarah</i> is read (or chanted) the <i>maftir</i> precedes the <i>haftarah</i> with two blessings and after he ends the <i>haftarah</i> he recites three blessings to which a fourth one is added on Sabbaths and festivals. This fourth blessing changes with the nature of the day. The Sabbath <i>haftarah</i> usually has a minimum of twenty-one verses while the festival has at least fifteen verses. Lately it has become the custom for the Bar Mitzvah boy (a man upon reaching the age of thirteen) to chant the <i>haftarah</i> to display his ability with a Hebrew text.<br>Louis Isaac Rabinowitz, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 16, pp. 1342-44.</i>) comes from Isaiah, “and shall say, cast you up, cast you up, prepare the way” until “for the mouth of the Lord has spoken it”, (Isaiah 57:14-58:14).
If there is a circumcision on Yom Kippur;<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">A circumcision must take place on the eighth day after birth, if the baby is healthy, regardless of whether it is the Sabbath, Yom Kippur, or any day of the year. The circumcision takes precedence over anything else.</i> then they circumcize between the (Morning Prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Shaḥarit</i>, <span class="rtl">שחרית</span>; see footnote 17.</i> prior to the <i>Keri’at Shema</i> named) “<span dir="ltr">יוצר</span><sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Shema</i>, made up of Deuteronomy 6:4-9; 11:13-21; and Numbers 15:37-41, during the Morning Service is surrounded by two blessings before and one afterwards. (In the Evening Service there are two blessings before and two afterwards.) The Shema and its blessings are called <i>Keri'at Shema</i>, <span class="rtl">קריאת שמע</span>, the Reading of the <i>Shema</i>. It is the basis of a Jew's declaration of One God and his devotion to God. "Hear, O Israel, the Lord our God, the Lord is One."<br>The first blessing before the <i>Shema</i> in the mornings is called the "<i>Yoẓer Or</i>", <span class="rtl">יוצר אור</span>, "Who formest light and createst darkness" and it is a prayer of thankgiving for the creation of physical light, the light of day and the daily renewal of creation. (For a further explanation of the Reading of the <i>Shema</i>, see footnote 17).<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., p. 108.</i>”, “Creator,” and the Additional Service<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Musaf</i>, <span class="rtl">מוסף</span>, Additional Service; see footnote 166.</i> after the reading of the Torah. And after the circumcision we say the prayer, “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span><sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Ashrei, <span class="rtl">אשרי</span>, means literally "Happy are they". It is the first word and the name of a prayer in the liturgy composed from the Book of Psalms. The prayer is made up of the following: Psalms 84:5, 144:15, 145, and 115:18. The Talmud (Ber. 4b) states that anyone who recites the <i>Ashrei</i> three times a day will be assured of a life in the world to come. Therefore the prayer is read three times a day in the liturgy. It is read twice during the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service, once in the preliminary psalms, <i>Pesukei de-Zimra</i> (see footnote 17), and once toward the end, and it is read at the beginning of the Afternoon, <i>Minḥah</i> Service (see footnote 40). The <i>Ashrei</i> is also recited before the <i>Seliḥot</i> (see footnote 14), prayers of forgiveness, in the months of Elul and Tishrei. On Yom Kippur the Sephardim recite the <i>Ashrei</i> both at <i>Minḥah</i> and <i>Ne'ilah</i> (see footnote 191) whereas the Ashkenazim only recite it at the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service.<br>Raphael Posner, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 3, p. 736.</i>” But the custom is to circumcize after the “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span>”, (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> And if the circumcision takes place in a location where it is necessary to leave the synagogue, we do not circumcize him until after the Torah scroll is returned to the ark, and then they return (to the synagogue) and say the (reader’s) <i>Kaddish</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kaddish</i>, <span class="rtl">קדיש</span>, which literally means "holy" is a doxology, mostly in Aramaic which is recited by a reader with responses made throughout it by the congregation at the close of individual sections and at the conclusion of the whole prayer. There are four principal types of the <i>Kaddish</i>; the whole or complete <i>Kaddish</i>, the half <i>Kaddish</i>, the <i>Kaddish de-Rabbanan</i> (the scholar's <i>Kaddish</i>), and the Mourner's <i>Kaddish</i>.<br>The whole <i>Kaddish</i> basically glorifies God and prays for peace. It is recited by the reader after the <i>Amidah</i> except for the Morning Service when it comes after the prayer <i>U-Va le-Ẓiyyon</i> (see footnote 187). The half <i>Kaddish</i> omits the conclusion of the prayer which consists of the last three blessings. The half <i>Kaddish</i> is recited by the reader between different sections of the service. The <i>Kaddish de-Rabbanan</i> is the whole Kaddish except for a substitution which prays for those who study Torah and teach it. It is recited after communal study, after the reading of the lighting the Sabbath candles in the synagogue (<i>Be-Mah Madlikin</i>, <i>Shabbat</i> 2), after the early Morning Service, and after the song <i>Ein Kh'Elohenu</i> praising God. The Mourner's Kaddish is recited by the close relatives of a deceased person for eleven months following the death and every year on the day which marks the anniversary of that death. It is basically the whole <i>Kaddish</i> with the exception of one line concerning supplications. It is said at certain points of the service, e. g., after the <i>Aleinu</i> (see footnote 182) and may be repeated after the reading of additional psalms.<br>All versions of the <i>Kaddish</i> are said standing facing Jerusalem. The Sephardi form has an additional verse which is not found in the Ashkenazi concerning the coming of the Messiah. At first the <i>Kaddish</i> was not part of the daily synagogue worship but by the geonic period (see footnote 19) it was an established part of the Service and it required ten men (a <i>minyan</i> constituting a congregation) to be recited.<br>The <i>Kaddish</i> did not become a Mourner's prayer until around the thirteenth century as a result of the persecution of German Jews by the Crusades. The prayer is not for the soul of the departed, but rather an expression of the justification of judgment proclaimed by those who have suffered a loss. Man is required to give praise to God even when afflicted by sorrow.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, pp. 660-62.</i>
We bless the circumcision without a cup (of wine).<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">A cup of wine is used in the circumcision ceremony, but because it is Yom Kippur there was some question as to whether this was permitted or not.</i> (There are those who say that we bless (the circumcision) with a cup (of wine) and we give it to the infant who is 179 circumcized, and this is the custom.<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">Children, and therefore certainly infants, are not subject to the laws of fasting on Yom Kippur. The custom, therefore developed that a cup of wine was used in the circumcision ceremony on Yom Kippur. The infant is usually given a small piece of cloth or cotton to suck on which has been dipped in wine. This sanctifies the baby's circumcision according to the Jewish law.</i>)
In the Additional Service<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Musaf</i>, <span class="rtl">מוסף</span>, Additional Service; see footnote 166.</i> the reader recites the order of the <i>Avodah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avodah</i>, <span class="rtl">עבודה</span>, the Sacrificial Service on Yom Kippur; see footnote 22.</i><br><small><i>Hagah</i>: It is customary to fall on their faces, prostrate, as they say the section of the <i>Avodah</i>, “and the priests and the people…” and also to prostrate when saying the <i>Aleinu</i> Prayer.<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The prayer <i>Aleinu Le-Shabbea'aḥ</i>, <span class="rtl">עלינר לשבח</span>, means "It is our duty to praise the Lord of all things." This prayer now is recited at the conclusion to the Morning, Afternoon, and Evening Pryaers, daily and on the Sabbaths and festivals. Originally it introduced the <i>Malkhuyyot</i> (Kingships) section of the Rosh HaShanah <i>Musaf Amidah</i> (see footnote 166). It is also found in the <i>Musaf</i> section of the liturgy for Yom Kippur.<br>The form of the <i>Aleinu</i> is like an early liturgical poem, <i>piyyut</i> (see footnote 149). It has short lines, each having about four words with marked rhythm and parallelism. Some traditions say it was written by Joshua while others give authorship to the men of the Great Assembly during the period of the Second Temple. It probably dates back to the time of the Second Temple because of two reasons. First of all there is no mention of the restoration of the Temple in it, which is unusual for this type of prayer it was written after the Temple was destroyed. Secondly, the prayer demands prostration which was a Temple practice abandoned after the Temple was destroyed except during this prayer on Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur in the Ashkenazi rite. At all other times it is customary to simply bow when reciting the words "we bend the knee…"<br>The main theme of the prayer is the Kingdom of God. God is praised in it for singling out the people of Israel as a nation of God worshippers not idolaters. The second paragraph expresses a hope for the coming of the Kingdom of God when all mankind will recognize the one and only true God, this being a universal ideal.<br>The prayer was censored over the years by the Christians who felt, due to a misunderstanding of the words, that it insulted Christianity even though it pre-dated that religion. Throughout the centuries different parts of the prayer were left out due to outside pressure. The Sephardim for the most part retained the entire original text which has not been restored throughout most of the Ashkenazi rites.<br>Hanoch Avenary, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 2, pp. 556-59.</i> But the reader is forbidden to move from his place at the time of the prayer in order to prosstrate, and one should object to those who do this (prostrate).<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Prostration was part of the Temple Service. It was believed that the <i>Aleinu</i> prayer was part of the Temple service in view of the line in the prayer which states that "we bend the knee and bow down…" (see footnote 182). After the Temple was destroyed it became the custom to only bend one's knee at this above stated line in the <i>Aleinu</i>. In the Ashkenazi rite, the reader would actually prostrate himself when he read this line during the <i>Musaf</i> Service on Rosh HaShanah and Yom Kippur. This was not done among the Sephardi communities.</i></small>
The order of the confession is; I have sinned, I have transgressed, I have offended, etc.
It is customary to pledge (to vow) charity on Yom Kippur for the dead,<sup class="footnote-marker">183a</sup><i class="footnote">The following comment is given by <i>Turei Zahav</i>, (see footnote 19a): 621:4 - "For the dead": Beit Yosef, <span class="rtl">ב״י</span>, (see footnote 20) cited that there is in a Midrash (stated) that even the dead need atonement and they acquire merit by the (living) giving of charity on their behalf since God examines the hearts (of people) and knows that if they were alive they would have given charity.</i> (and they remember their souls because the dead also have atonement on Yom Kippur), (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.</i>

Siman 622

<b>“The order of the Afternoon Service</b><sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minḥah</i>, <span class="rtl">מנחה</span>, Afternoon Service; see footnote 40.</i> <b>on Yom Kippur” - Containing four paragraphs.</b><br>In the Afternoon Service one says “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span><sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ashrei</i>, <span class="rtl">אשרי</span>; see footnote 175.</i>”, and “<span dir="ltr">ובא לציון</span><sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote"><i>U-Va Le-Ẓiyyon</i>, <span class="rtl">ובא לציון</span>, is a prayer found near the beginning of the <i>Minḥah</i> Service on Shabbat as well as on festivals. It is of biblical origin coming from Isaiah 59:20-21. It usually follows the <i>Ashrei</i> (see footnote 175). It is also found in additional prayers often recited after the daily <i>Shaḥarit</i>, Morning Service. The prayer speaks of a Redeemer coming to Zion, Jerusalem, and helping Israel who eternally shares in God's covenant.<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., p. 571.</i>”, but one does not say “<span dir="ltr">ואני תפלתי</span><sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ve'ani Tefilati</i>, <span class="rtl">ואני תפלתי</span>, is a prayer recited on the Sabbath during the <i>Minḥah</i> Service. It closes the prayers immediately preceding the Torah reading done during the Afternoon Sabbath Service. It is only recited on the Sabbath. If a festival occurs on a weekday the prayer is omitted. On Yom Kippur the prayer is omitted even if Yom Kippur falls on the Sabbath because it is not in keeping with the spirit of the Day of Atonement. The prayer is from Psalms 69:14, "And as for me, may my prayer unto Thee, O Lord, be in an acceptable time: O God, in the abundance of Thy lovingkindness, answer me with thy sure salvation."<br><i>ibid</i>., p. 572.</i>” even if it (Yom Kippur) falls on the Sabbath.<br><small><i>Hagah</i> : We are not used to saying “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span><sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ashrei</i>, <span class="rtl">אשרי</span>; see footnote 175.</i>” and “<span dir="rtl">ובא לציון</span><sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote"><i>U-Va Le-Ẓiyyon</i>, <span class="rtl">ובא לציון</span>; see footnote 187.</i>” before the Afternoon Service, only before the Closing Service, <i>Ne’ilah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ne'ilah</i>, <span class="rtl">נעילה</span>, is a worship service which concludes the prayer service recited on Yom Kippur. The name <i>Ne'ilah</i> was derived from a ritual during the time of the Second Temple. Even though the service is now only recited at the conclusion of the Day of Atonement, it was originally recited on all public fast days including Yom Kippur.<br>The full name of the service is <i>Ne'ilat She'arim</i> which means the "Closing of the Gates". This referred to the daily closing of the Temple gates at night. On Yom Kippur this terminology is symbolically associated with the closing of the heavenly gates which are open until sunset to receive the atonement of a person who truly repents.<br>According to the Talmud, during Temple times <i>Ne'ilah</i> was recited daily one hour before sunset at which time the Temple Gates were closed as there was no evening sacrifice only an Afternoon <i>Minḥah</i> Sacrifice (see footnote 22). On Yom Kippur <i>Ne'ilah</i>, because of the length of the Temple Service and the special rituals involved, could not begin until close to sunset. After the destruction of the Temple and when <i>Ne'ilah</i> became only associated with Yom Kippur, it became the practice for the service to begin close to sunset, before twilight and to end at nightfall.<br>In the third century the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service consisted of an <i>Amidah</i> of seven benedictions which were parallel to the other <i>Amidot</i> of the day (see footnote 43) and a confession of sins, the <i>Al Ḥet</i> (see footnote 45). The confession of sins though was later replaced by two prayers that are unique to the confession made during the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service: <i>Attah noten yad le-foshe'im</i>, "Thou stretchest forth Thy hand (in forgiveness) to sinners," and <i>Attah hivdalta enosh</i>, "Thou has distinguished man (from the beast)." These prayers remind man that God eagerly forgives one who is genuinely penitent. There is a basic rabbinic idea that divine judgment is inscribed in the "Book of Life" on Rosh HaShanah, but it is not sealed until the end of the Day of Atonement. Therefore at the end of the <i>Amidah</i> in the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service the root for the word inscribe, <i>ktv</i>, <span class="rtl">כתב</span>, is replaced by the root for the word seal, <i>ḥtm</i>, <span class="rtl">חתם</span>, when reference is made to the "Book of Life". To help set off the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service and its uniqueness on Yom Kippur, the <i>Ashrei</i> (see footnote 175) and the <i>U-Va Le-Ẓiyyon Go-el</i> (see footnote 187) prayer which normally come at the beginning of the <i>Minḥah</i> Service on Sabbaths and festivals, come at this time before the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service.<br>Beautiful liturgical poetry has become associated with the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service especially prayers of forgiveness, <i>seliḥot</i> (see footnote 14). The central theme of the Service is to give one a final chance and effort to seek forgiveness before the heavenly gates symbolically close at sunset. The Service ends with a feeling of confidence.<br>The <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service contains <i>piyyutim</i> (see footnote 149), an <i>Amidah</i> and a reader's repetition of it, <i>seliḥot</i>, <i>Ashamnu</i> (see footnote 45), <i>Avinu Malkenu</i> (see footnote 15), and the <i>Kaddish</i> (see footnote 177). The entire ritual culminates with a responsive proclamation of the <i>Shema</i> once (see footnote 173, but only the one line "Hear, O Israel, the Lord our God, the Lord is One") followed by its response three times, <i>Barukh shem kavod malkhut</i>o <i>le'olam va'ed</i>, "Praised be His glorious Kingdom forever and ever", and <i>Adonai Hu ha-Elohim</i>", "The Lord He is God" (I Kings 18:39) seven times. Finally in the Ashkenazi rite a final single <i>shofar</i> blast (see footnote 221) marks the end of the Day of Atonement, the Sabbath of Sabbaths.<br>Herman Kieval, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 12, pp. 943-44.</i>, and so wrote a few of our rabbis, (<span dir="ltr">מרדכי והגהות מיימוני סוף ספר אהבה, וסוף הלכות יו״כ, כל בו ומנהגים</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> and <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i> at the end of the book <i>Ahavah</i>, Love, (which is the name of the second book of Maimonides' <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, see footnote 59) and the end (of the section) of the Laws of Yom Kippur, and <i>Kol Bo</i> and <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">ומנהגים מרדכי והגהות מיימוני סו ״ס״ אהבה וסוף הלכות יו״כ וכל בו</span>.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.<br>For <i>Hagahot</i> Maimuniyyot, הגהות מיימוני; see footnote 27.<br>For <i>Kol Bo</i>, <span class="rtl">כל בו</span>; see footnote 34.<br>For <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.</i> We do not say “<span dir="ltr">אין כאלהינו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote"><i>En Kh'Elohenu</i>, <span class="rtl">אין כאלהינו</span>, is one of the most popular chants connected with the Jewish liturgy. The Sephardim sing it everyday while the Ashkenazi rite has usually limited its singing to the end of the <i>Musaf</i> Service (see footnote 166) on Sabbaths and festivals shortly before the <i>Aleinu</i> Prayer (see footnote 182). During the geonic period (see footnote 19) it was also chanted during the Evening Services.<br>The prayer expresses God four ways: God, Lord, King, and Savior and that there is none like Him, who is like Him?, we will give thanks to Him, we will bless Him, and proclaim that He is God who our fathers worshipped. Each statement expresses the same thoughts repeated four times, once for each name of God. We take out the Torah scroll and three (men) read from the portion “<span dir="ltr">עריות</span>” (Incest), (Leviticus 18:1-21) until the end of the portion and the third (man reads) the <i>Maftir</i> (<i>Haftarah</i>) from the Book of Jonah<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maftir</i>, <span class="rtl">מפטיר</span>, and <i>Haftarah</i>, <span class="rtl">הפטרה</span>; He was the head of the yeshivah in his home town of Schlettstadt. Little is known of his life other than the fact that he apparently had to hide for a number of years due to the revenge caused by his sentencing to death two conspirators who were working for the knights of Andlau. One of the conspirators was killed and the other fled and apostatized. It seems as if a number of Jews were involved in the affair for financial gain and Samuel eventually made his way to Babylonia where he obtained deeds of excommunication (see footnote 29) from the <i>nesi'im</i>, the heads of the academies, against those who were involved in the affair.<br>Samuel's best known work, <i>Hagahot Mordekhai</i> is also referred to as <i>Ha-Mordekhai ha-Katan</i>, <i>Ha-Mordekhai ha-Kaẓer</i>, and <i>Kiẓẓur Mordekhai</i>. It is an abridgment of the <i>Mordekhai</i> by Mordecai b. Hillel (see footnote 24). It seems as if the work had an independent value apart from the major work upon which it was based. It was mentioned in numerous halakhic works by such scholars as Isserlein (see footnote 96), Weil (see footnote 27), Moellin (see footnote 8), and Landau (see footnote 223).<br>Samuel added notes containing rulings and additions from the work of various <i>posekim</i>, halakhic decision makers, to the <i>Mordekhai</i>, and the notes have appeared as an appendix to the major work since the edition published in 1559 of Riva di Trento.<br>Shlomoh Zalman Havlin, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 974-75.</i></small>
If (Yom Kippur) falls on the Sabbath we say the prayer, “…<span dir="ltr">צדקתך</span><sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ẓidkatkha</i>, <span class="rtl">צדקתך</span>, is a prayer found in the <i>Minḥah</i> (see footnote 40) Service <i>Amidah</i> on Sabbaths and it is omitted at such times when <i>Taḥanun</i> is omitted on weekdays (see footnote 10) in the Ashkenazi rite.
