Siman 623

<b>“The order of the Closing Prayer, <i>Ne’ilah</i>” - Containing six paragraphs.</b><br>For the Closing Service we say “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span><sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ashrei</i>, <span class="rtl">אשרי</span>; see footnote 175.</i>” and <i>Kaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kaddish</i>, <span class="rtl">קדיש</span>; see footnote 177.</i> but we do not say “<span dir="ltr">ובא לציון</span><sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><i>U-Va Le-Ẓiyyon</i>, <span class="rtl">ובא לציון</span>; see footnote 187.</i>”.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: I have already written that the custom in these lands is to say “<span dir="ltr">אשרי</span>” and “<span dir="rtl">ובא לציון</span>” before the Closing Service.</small>
The time for the Closing Service is when the sun is just on the tops of the trees so that it can be completed close to the setting of the sun, and the reader needs to shorten the prayers of forgiveness<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Prayers of forgiveness, <i>seliḥot</i>, <span class="rtl">סליחות</span>; see footnote 14.</i> and the verses in the middle of the <i>Tefillah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Tefilah</i>, <span class="rtl">התפילה</span>, or the <i>Amidah</i>, <span class="rtl">עמידה</span>; see footnote 43.</i> and he must not lengthen in the Closing Prayer every word as he lengthens them in the rest of the prayers,<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">It is customary on Yom Kippur to chant and sing each word in a long drawn-out manner often repeating words in the chant, because one is obligated to spend the entire day in the synagogue in prayer. But for the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service this practice is not followed, rather, the Service is read rather quickly.<br>209. For an explanation of this practice concerning the wording around the expression the "Book of Life", see footnote 191 on the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service.</i> so that he will finish before the sun sets, (and he says instead of “inscribe us (in the “Book of Life”)”, “seal us (in the “Book of Life”)”), (<span dir="ltr">טור</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>; see footnote 23.</i>
If (the <i>Ne’ilah</i> Prayer) falls on the Sabbath, we remember in it the Sabbath in the prayer (meaning, we mention it),<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">See footnote 196 concerning the added words "of the Sabbath".</i> but in the confession which is after the Silent Prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Amidah</i>, <span class="rtl">עמידה</span>; see footnote 43.</i> we do not mention the Sabbath. This refers to the individual worshipper, but the reader, since he said it in the midst of his Silent Prayer, he mentions the Sabbath in it (even when repeating the Prayer outloud). If he does not mention it (the Sabbath) they do not make him return and repeat it.
He says “<span dir="ltr">כתר</span><sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Keter</i>, <span class="rtl">כתר</span>, was an ancient beginning of the <i>Kedushah</i>, <span class="rtl">קדושה</span>, or sanctification prayer. It is no longer said and <i>Kedushah</i> now begins with the word <i>na'ariẓkha</i>, <span class="rtl">נעריצך</span>. It is part of the Sabbath and festival <i>Musaf</i> Service (see footnote 166).<br>The <i>Kedushah</i> said during the <i>Musaf Amidah</i> is different than the Sanctification recited in the <i>Shaḥarit Amidah</i> on the Sabbath (see footnote 17). The <i>Musaf</i> Sanctification dwells more sublimely on the majestic conception of the angels in heaven glorifying the Eternal King and it introduces Israel proclaiming in response to the angelic choir, the holiness, glory, unity, and sovereignity of God's name. The <i>Shema</i>, the congregation's confession of faith, is part of the <i>Musaf Kedushah</i> (see footnote 17, section (3)).<br>The <i>Shema</i> entered the <i>Musaf Kedushah</i> as the result of a sixth century persecution. The Byzantine Empire forbid Jews to recite the <i>Shema</i> in public worship. On Sabbaths and festivals government spies would enter the synagogues to make sure the <i>Shema</i> was not said in its regular place during the <i>Shaḥarit</i> Service. As a means of getting the <i>Shema</i> into public prayer, the reader would insert it into the <i>Musaf Kedushah</i>, a place where the spies would not expect it and thus not be aware of it. The <i>Shema</i> has remained a part of this Sanctification ever since.<br>The <i>Kedushah</i> is part of the third blessing of the <i>Amidah</i> and in the <i>Musaf</i> Service for the Sabbath it is made up of the following parts along with connecting sentences: Isaiah 6:3, Ezekiel 3:12, the <i>Shema</i>, Deuteronomy 6:5, and Psalms 146:10. On festivals there is an additional verse from Zechariah 14:9 following the <i>Shema</i>.<br>Hertz, <i>op</i>. <i>cit</i>., pp. 528-31; 816-19.</i>”, “the crown”, just as in the Additional Service.<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Musaf</i>, <span class="rtl">מוסף</span>; see footnote 166.</i>
They give the priestly benediction<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Priestly Benediction, <span class="rtl">ברכת כהנים</span>; see footnote 100.</i> during the Closing Service. (The custom in these lands is not to give the priestly benediction, but we say “<span dir="ltr">אבינו מלכנו</span><sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Avinu Malkhenu</i>, <span class="rtl">אבינו מלכנו</span>; see footnote 15.</i>”.)
At the end of the prayers of forgiveness<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Prayers of forgiveness, <i>seliḥot</i>, <span class="rtl">סליחות</span>; see footnote 14.</i> we say seven times, “He is the God,” (and one time the “<span dir="ltr">שמע ישראל</span>”), and three times the, “Blessed be His glorious kingdom forever and ever,”<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">See the end of the footnote on the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service, number 191. This is how the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service ends.</i> (see above section 61,<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">In the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, chapter 61, there are twenty-six paragraphs which contain the laws concerned with how one is to pronounce it and what is to be one's personal conviction when reciting the <i>Shema</i> prayer.</i> (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, מנהגים; see footnote 13.</i>) and we blow the <i>shofar</i>, (<span dir="ltr">תקיעה, שברים, תרועה, תקיעה</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>shofar</i>, <span class="rtl">שופר</span>, is a ram's horn which has been prepared to use as a musical instrument. The word is mentioned sixty-nine times in the Bible as well as numerous times in talmudic and post-talmudic literature.<br>The <i>shofar</i> was used to proclaim significant events in Judaism. It proclaimed the Jubilee Year (Leviticus 25:9-11) every fiftieth year proclaiming freedom throughout the land. Its most noted use is on Rosh HaShanah which is also called <i>yom teru'ah</i>, "a day of blowing", (Numbers 29:1). It was also used to declare war (Judges 3:27) and to induce fear (Amos 3:6).<br>In Temple times the <i>shofar</i> was not exclusively a ram's horn. Any curved animal's horn from a sheep, goat, mountain goat, antelope, or a gazelle was possible. The use of the ram's horn became popular because of its association with the <i>Akedah</i>, the sacrifice of Isaac read on Rosh HaShanah when the <i>shofar</i> is sounded. A ram was substituted for Isaac in the biblical account (Genesis 22) which is read on the second day of Rosh HaShanah. The <i>shofar</i> may have carved designs on it as long as the mouthpiece is natural. It may not be painted. It may not have a hole.<br>The Bible only refers to two trumpet sounds, teki'ah and <i>teru'ah</i> (Numbers 10: 5-8) the Mishna (RH. 4:9) describes <i>teki'ah</i> as one long blast and <i>teru'ah</i> as three wavering crying blasts. It prescribes three sets of <i>shofar</i> sounds since the word <i>teru'ah</i> is mentioned three times in the Bible, (Leviticus 23:24, 25:9, and Numbers 29:1). In talmudic times there was a disagreement about what exactly the sounds of the <i>shofar</i> should be and Rabbi Ahbahu decided the issue by compromise. The <i>shofar</i> was to be sounded three times which included different notes of <i>shevarim</i>, which are broken sounds and <i>teru'ah</i>, nine staccato notes. The sounding is one set of <i>teki'ah</i>, <i>shevarim</i> - <i>teru'ah</i>, t<i>eki'ah</i>, and two sets of the following: <i>teki'ah</i>, <i>shevarim</i>, <i>teki'ah</i>, and <i>teki'ah</i>, <i>teru'ah</i>, <i>teki'ah</i>. The <i>teki'ah</i> begins as a low note swelling to a higher one. The <i>teru'ah</i> is a series of staccato blasts and the <i>shevarim</i> alternates higher and lower notes. The concluding note of each of the two series is a <i>teki'ah gedolah</i>, a great, long blast, which derives its origin from Exodus 19:13, "When the ram's horn soundeth long, they shall come up to the mount."<br>The <i>shofar</i> used to be sounded on the second day of the month of Elul marking the beginning of the penitential season. Today it is sounded daily except for the last day of the month of Elul at the Morning Service until Rosh Ha-Shanah is over and it is sounded at the end of the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service at the conclusion of Yom Kippur.<br>On Rosh HaShanah, Psalm 47 is recited seven times before the <i>shofar</i> is sounded. This is a reminder of the walls of Jericho being circled seven times before they fell to the sound of the <i>shofar</i>.<br>The <i>shofar</i> may only be sounded during the day. It is not sounded when Rosh HaShanah falls on the Sabbath, lest one violate the laws of the Sabbath by carrying the <i>shofar</i> into the synagogue. When the Temple was in existence the <i>shofar</i> was blown there on the Sabbath.<br>Throughout Jewish history the <i>shofar</i> was blown to announce a death, on fasts, at excommunications (see footnote 29), and at funerals. On Friday afternoons it was sounded six times to announce various work stopping times and the times to light the candles and usher in the Sabbath. Today it is used to inaugurate a new president in Israel.<br>According to the Sephardi rite the <i>shofar</i> is sounded at the end of the <i>Ne'ilah</i> Service as follows: <i>teki'ah</i>, one rising blast; <i>shevarim</i>, three blasts alternating high and low; <i>teru'ah</i>, seven staccato blasts; and <i>teki'ah</i>, one rising note.<br>Albert L. Lewis, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 14, pp. 1442-47.<br>The following comment is given by <i>Magen Avraham</i>, (see footnote 33.): 623:4 - "And they blow (the shofar)": Even though they still have not "separated" (said <i>Havdalah</i>, see footnote 226) in the prayer (and it is still not actually night), nevertheless since (the blowing of the <i>shofar</i> is a matter of) wisdom (art and know-how) and not work, and the rabbis were not too strict about it, (did not prohibit it at this occasion), since the day has already passed (<i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>, see footnote 23). And even though he wrote that one should complete it (the <i>shofar</i> blowing) close to sunset, this should complete it (the <i>shofar</i> blowing) close to sunset, this means that the stars still have not come out (indicating nightfall), nevertheless this is twilight, and see the end of chapter 299, (in the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>).</i><br><small><i>Hagah</i>: There are those who say that we only blow the <i>shofar</i> once (<span dir="ltr">תקיעה</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">The Ashkenazi rite states that at the end of the Ne'ilah Service only one long <i>shofar</i> blast is sounded, <i>teki'ah gedolah</i>, <span class="rtl">תקיעה גדולה</span>.</i> (<span dir="ltr">מרדכ והגהות מיימוני סוף הלכות ואגור י״כ</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mordekhai</i> and <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i>, the end of the Laws of Yom Kippur, and Agur, <span class="rtl">מרדכי והגהות מיימוניי סוף הלכות י״כ ואגור</span>.<br>For <i>Mordekhai</i>, <span class="rtl">מרדכי</span>; see footnote 24.<br>For <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i>, <span class="rtl">הגהות מיימוני</span>; see footnote 27.<br>Agur, <span class="rtl">אגור</span>, is a halakhic work that deals basically with the subject matter found in <i>Tur Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> and <i>Yoreh De'ah</i>, (see footnote 23). It was written by Jacob b. Judah Landau, a fifteenth century German talmudist. He was educated in Germany by his famous father Judah who died in 1464. Judah was a favorite pupil of Jacob Moellin (see footnote 8) and a relative of Jacob Weil (see footnote 27) who was the head of a large yeshivah and a well respected <i>posek</i>, an halakhic decision maker.<br>Jacob emigrated at some time in his life to Italy along with the great wave of Jewish emigration from Germany and in Pavia in 1460 he met Joseph Colon from whom he drew many rulings and quotes. While in Pavia he wrote <i>Ḥazon</i> a work introduced by the words "How could it be?" In 1487 Jacob went to Naples. He worked there as a proofreader for the new Hebrew Press established there, which published his work, <i>Ha-Agur</i> along with <i>Ḥazon</i>.<br><i>Ha-Agur</i> is an anthology and a summation of German-Jewish scholarship on the laws contained in <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> and <i>Yoreh De'ah</i> down to Jacob's own time. He based himself on the <i>Tur</i> of Jacob b. Asher (see footnote 23). Jacob wanted to assemble all the data on a particular <i>halakhah</i>, (but omitted the arguments), lay down the <i>halakhah</i>, and then include any new rulings by people such as Israel Isserlein (see footnote 96), Jacob Weil (see footnote 27), Joseph Colon, and particularly his father Judah. The work is organized similar to the <i>Tur</i> and is distinguished by the interweaving of varied material from many different works. Landau, who was well versed in Kabbalah interlaced those theories together with the halakhic material as an aid to arriving at decisions. The work reveals a great deal of the teachings of the German scholars in the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries.<br>Israel Moses Ta-Shma, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 10, p. 1393.</i> and thus we do it in these lands, we sound the <i>shofar</i> after we say the <i>Kaddish</i><sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Kaddish</i>, <span class="rtl">קדיש</span>; see footnote 177.</i> after the Closing Service, but in a few places the custom is to blow the <i>shofar</i> before the <i>Kaddish</i>.</small>

Siman 624

<b>“The order at the close of (i.e., after) Yom Kippur” - Containing five paragraphs.</b><br>We pray the Evening Prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Arvit</i>, <span class="rtl">ערבית</span>, the Evening Service; see footnote 144.</i> and say the <i>Havdalah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Havdalah</i>, <span class="rtl">הבדלה</span>, literally means distinction. It is a blessing that is recited at the termination of Sabbaths and festivals in order to help distinguish the holy of the holiday from the profane or ordinary nature of the regular weekday. According to the Talmud (Ber. 33a) the men of the Great Synagogue instituted these prayers. At times the prayer was said in the <i>Amidah</i>, but when Israel got richer and could afford it, the prayer was said over a cup of wine and, therefore taken out of the <i>Amidah</i> and placed at the end of the Service. The location of the prayer varied through the tannaitic period. In different rites, different prayers begin the ceremony. Most Ashkenazim recite Isaiah 12:2-3, "Behold, God is my salvation". The introduction is followed by three blessings, over wine, spices, and light, the order varied before it was set down in the Mishna.<br>The blessing over light was to display that work (lighting fire) was now permitted as the Sabbath or festival was over. The blessing over wine comes from the early established duty to recite the prayer over wine. There is no agreement as to the origin of the blessing over the spices. The <i>rishonim</i> (see footnote 16) state that it is to compensate the Jew for the loss of his "additional soul" which traditionally accompanied him throughout the Sabbath and festivals, although other reasons have also been given.<br>The actual <i>Havdalah</i> blessing itself, the blessing over the separation of holy from profane, is the fourth blessing in the ceremony. It developed over the years into various versions. It was decided that the prayer must mention at least one distinction, that between holy and profane. When a festival is followed immediately by the Sabbath the distinction mentioned is between the holy and the holy. When a festival is followed by a weekday, <i>Havdalah</i> may be recited over wine alone without the candle and spices. Many local customs developed around this mystical ceremony as did many special songs and hymns.<br>Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 7, pp. 1481-82.</i> (the “separation”) in the benediction “<span dir="ltr">חונן הדעת</span><sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Sephardi ritual, the <i>Havdalah</i> ceremony was part of the <i>Arvit</i> Service (see footnote 144) and the recitation of the <i>Havdalah</i> was done during the <i>Amidah</i> (see footnote 43). It was inserted into the fourth benediction of the regular nineteen benedictions which make up the weekday <i>Amidah</i>, <i>Ḥonen ha-Da'at</i>, <span class="rtl">חונן הדעת</span>, a prayer of thanking God for knowledge and understanding.</i>”.
One has to add some time (i.e., lengthen the day of Yom Kippur)<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">"Add some time," means one must wait a little while and not rush for the conclusion of the Day of Atonement. One should prolong the day a bit by not rushing into the Havdalah prayer, (see footnote 226).</i> from the profane to the holy<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">From "the profane to the holy" means that by waiting a bit to recite the <i>Havdalah</i> prayer one takes away a bit of the profane, that is the weekday, and adds a bit to the holy, that is Yom Kippur, by prolonging the Day of Atonement a bit longer than is actually dictated by the fall of night.</i> also when (Yom Kippur is over, that is they should wait a little after the stars appear in the sky.<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">When three stars appear in the sky that is the definate distinction between light and dark, between day and night. The sight of the three stars definitely establishes the fact that it is night, that the holiday is over, that a new day has begun according to the Hebrew calendar, and that the <i>Havdalah</i> Prayer can be recited.</i>
One says <i>Havdalah</i> over the cup (of wine) but one does not say the benediction over the spices even if it (Yom Kippur) falls on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">For an explanation of this special case regarding the <i>Havdalah</i>, see the end of footnote 226.</i>
We (say a) blessing over the light, but we do not say a blessing after Yom Kippur on the light which they brought forth now from stones (i.e., sparks). Some say that we do say the benediction over the first fire which was ignited by this spark and onward.<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">To say the blessing over the lights in the <i>Havdalah</i> ceremony one may not use a fire that one sees from the first spark that results when two stones are struck together. The fire that can be used for the prayer has to be the result of at least the second fire that was ignited by those first sparks. The first sparks do not burn long enough to say a blessing over them. According to the commentator <i>Magen Avraham</i>, a commentary on the Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i> by Abraham Abele Gombiner (see footnote 33), the first sparks are not a real fire. Only the second fire ignited from the first sparks is considered a real fire that continues to burn long enough for a benediction to be recited over it.</i>
A Jew who lights the candle from the fire of a non-Jew does not (say a) blessing over it at the close of Yom Kippur even though at the end of the Sabbath we do bless it, because we do not say a blessing over the fire on the close of Yom Kippur, only over light which was not burning during the daytime of Yom Kippur, or over the light that was lighted from it, and (therefore) the custom is to light from the lamps of the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">These candles in the synagogue were kindled before Yom Kippur and therefore they are not the candles that were lighted and used on Yom Kippur by non-Jews. Candles lighted and used on Yom Kippur by Gentiles are not to be used to ignite candles to be blessed during the <i>Havdalah</i> ceremony, but candles in the synagogue that had been ignited prior to Yom Kippur and they burned all through Yom Kippur without being touched can be used to light candles which are to be blessed during the <i>Havdalah</i> ceremony following Yom Kippur.</i>; however if one lighted (his candle) on Yom Kippur, if it was lighted with permission (i.e., if he was allowed to light it), for example for a sick person, we are able to say a blessing over it.<br><small><i>Hagah</i>: There are those who say that one is to say the Prayer of <i>Havdalah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Havdalah</i>, <span class="rtl">הבדלה</span>; see footnote 226.</i> over the light of the synagogue, (<span dir="ltr">המגיד ואבודרהם וא״ז</span>),<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ha-Maggid</i> and <i>Abudarham</i> and <i>Or Zarua'a</i>, <span class="rtl">וא״ז המגיד ואבודרהם</span>.<br>Ha-Maggid, <span class="rtl">המגיד</span>, refers to the work <i>Maggid Mishneh</i>, a commentary to the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> by Maimonides, (see footnote 59) . It was written by a Spanish rabbi known as Vidal Yom Tov of Tolosa who lived in the second half of the fourteenth century. He came from Tolosa, Catalonia where he wrote his commentary. The commentary does not cover the entire <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, only Books 3; 4; 5, chapters 1-9; 11; 12, chapters 1-3; and 13 are in existence. Some of his commentaries may have been lost, but it is also possible that he only commented on laws that were practical during his time. His purpose was to clarify the difficult passages of Maimonides and to indicate the sources Maimonides used. Vidal also dealt with criticisms, <i>hassagot</i>, of Abraham b. David of Bosquieres and tried to answer them. He often found both men right attributing the differences to different versions of the text. He also explained the order of the <i>halakhot</i> of Maimonides in each book. He felt that a misunderstanding of Maimonides' order was the reason for the book being so severly criticized. Vidal was quite strict in his rulings where he quoted extensively from Naḥmanides and Solomon b. Abraham Adret and others. The <i>Maggid Mishneh</i> has become the standard commentary on the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.<br>Yehoshua Horowitz, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 16, pp. 119-20.<br><i>Sefer Abudarham</i>, <span class="rtl">ספר אבודרהם</span>, was written in Seville in 1340 by David ben Joseph Abudarham, a fourteenth century Spanish liturgical commentator. He was a communal leader in Toledo, and he was inspired to write his book because of the fact that customs connected with prayer varied from country to country where the Jews lived. Most of the Jews at this time did not understand the words of the prayers, nor the correct procedures and the reasons for saying them. Abudarham based his decisions on the Talmuds, the decisions of the <i>geonim</i> (see footnote 19), and early and late commentators of Spain, Provence, France and other Ashkenazi origins. Abudarham made much use of the prayer book of Saadiah Gaon. He also used several customs based on the <i>Manhig</i> of Abraham b. Nathan ha-Yarḥi of Lunel and the <i>Minhagot</i> of Asher b. Saul. He derived legal material from Asher b. Jehiel and the <i>Turim</i> of Jacob b. Asher (see footnote 23).<br>Abudarham traced the various customs in different countries of each prayer and commented on them. He included in his commentary an examination of the Passover <i>Haggadah</i>. He also discussed the weekly division of the Torah readings and their corresponding <i>Haftarot</i> (see footnote 170). He added to his book the rules of various benedictions, dividing them into nine categories and explaining and interpreting them. <i>Sefer Abudarham</i> was first published in Lisbon in 1490. Abudarham also wrote a book commenting on the liturgy of Yom Kippur which has been ascribed to Yose b. Yose, and other liturgical poems.<br>Zvi Avneri (Hans Lichtenstein) and Editorial Staff, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 2, pp. 181-82.<br>For <i>Or Zaru'a</i>, א״ז; see footnote 118.</i> and there are those who say that one should not use (the light of the synagogue) for <i>Havdalah</i>, rather one lights another light from the synagogue lamp.<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">In other words one lights the candle over which the <i>Havdalah</i> Prayer is recited from a candle that was lighted from the synagogue candles, thus making the lighting of the candle over which the blessing is said once removed from the synagogue light. In this way, the candle of the synagogue which burned throughout Yom Kippur is not used directly.</i> The correct practice is to say the <i>Havdalah</i> over both of them together, that is to kindle one light from the light of the synagogue, but one should not make the <i>Havdalah</i> over the light of the synagogue alone, (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.</i> For the rest of the laws concerning the light, see above in chapter  298.<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">The Shulḥan Arukh, <i>Oraḥ Ḥayyim</i>, chapter 238 contains the laws of the candle used for the <i>Havdalah</i> ceremony in fifteen chapters.</i> We eat and we rejoice at the close of Yom Kippur because it is somewhat of a holiday,<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">It is not actually according to official terminology a holiday on the night after Yom Kippur, but that night is celebrated as somewhat of a holiday to mark the end of fasting and the hope of true forgiveness by God for the genuine repenter.</i> (<span dir="ltr">טור ומהרי״ו, א״ז</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Tur</i> and <i>Mahariv</i> and <i>Or Zaru'a</i>, <span class="rtl">טור ומהרי״ו, א״ז</span>.<br>For <i>Tur</i>, <span class="rtl">טור</span>; see footnote 23.<br>For <i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>; see footnote 27.<br>For <i>Or Zaru'a</i>, א״ז; see footnote 118.</i> There are those who are strict and observe two days of Yom Kippur, and a person can be (halakhically) relieved of this practice,<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">If one started a second day of Yom Kippur he can be excused of it and stop his observation. The method that should be employed to stop the second day of fasting once it has been begun is the same as the process that is used to annul a vow, <i>hattarat nedarim</i>, declaring that the vow is null and void outloud by the <i>bet din</i>, the rabbinical court made up of three rabbis.</i> and one should not follow this strict practice since there is a fear that a person might be in danger (of his life or health), (<span dir="ltr">א״ז</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Or Zaru'a</i>, א״ז; see footnote 118.</i> He who fasts because he had a bad dream<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">A fast observed as the result of a bad dream or a nightmare, <i>ta'anit ḥalom</i>, <span class="rtl">תענית חלום</span>; see footnote 7.</i> on the day after Yom Kippur, there is no need for him to fast all his days<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">This means that if one has had a bad dream and observes a dream fast (see footnote 7) for it one year immediately after Yom Kippur, there is no need for him to fast every year after Yom Kippur just because he did it once. This one time is sufficient.</i> (after Yom Kippur every year just because he did it once), (<span dir="ltr">מנהגים</span>)<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>;see footnote 13.</i> One does not say prayers of supplication<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Taḥanun</i>, <span class="rtl">תחנון</span>, prayers of supplication; see footnote 10.</i> nor does he say “You are righteous, and you will be righteous, and you were righteous<sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ẓidkatkha</i>, <span class="rtl">צדקתך</span>; see footnote 199.</i>” from Yom Kippur until <i>Sukkot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sukkot</i>, <span class="rtl">סוכות</span>, the "Festival of Booths" is one of the three pilgrimage festivals. It begins on the fifteenth of Tishrei, five days after Yom Kippur. It commemorates the event that the Children of Israel dwelt in the wilderness after the Exodus from Egypt. The festival lasts for seven days. The first day (the first two days in the Diaspora) is a <i>yom-tov</i>, a festival on which no work is permitted. On the intermediate days, <i>ḥol ha-mo'ed</i>, work is permitted. Immediately after <i>Sukkot</i>, on the eigth day is the festival of <i>Shemini</i> Aẓeret, "The eighth day of solemn assembly" which is again a <i>yom-tov</i>. In the Diaspora a second day of <i>Shemini Aẓeret</i> is observed called <i>Simḥat Torah</i>, "Rejoicing over the Torah."<br>The holiday is called the feast of Tabernacles or Ingathering is mentioned often in the Bible, especially in Leviticus 23:39-43. It marks the Fall harvest.<br>The holiday is celebrated by living in temporarily constructed or completed booths, <i>Succot</i>, of which at least the roof is made especially for the holiday. These booths have a roof which enables those in them to see the sky but more of it has to be covered than not. Dwelling in these booths is to remind the Jews of the wandering of the Israelites through the desert. All meals are to be eaten (by the men) in the <i>Succah</i>, and one (i.e., the men) was supposed to live in the <i>Succah</i> throughout the holiday if the weather permitted this. During the time of the Temple Jews would make <i>Sukkot</i> one of their minimal of three pilgrimages to offer a special sacrifice in honor of the harvest and the festival.<br>The rabbis established four species of plant, <i>arba'ah minim</i>,to celebrate the festival with based on Leviticus 23:39-43; the citron, <i>etrog</i>; myrtle twigs, <i>hadasim</i>; palm branch, <i>lulav</i>; and willows, <i>aravot</i>.<br>Louis Jacobs, <i>E</i>. <i>J</i>., v. 15, pp. 495-502.</i> (the Festival of the Booths), and the meticulous ones begin immediately at the close of Yom Kippur to build the <i>Succah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">One is to build a <i>Succah</i> for the holiday of <i>Sukkot</i> (see footnote 248). <i>Succah</i> must be at least three sided and its roof must be made of leaves and branches. The roof should be such that it will not be able to withstand a heavy down-pour. One is to dwell in the <i>Succah</i> during the holiday of <i>Sukkot</i> to remind the Jew of the wandering of the Israelites in the desert following the Exodus from Egyptian slavery. If it rains or the weather is severe one is only required to eat meals in the temporary booth of this harvest festival. According to tradition preferably each household is to have its own <i>Succah</i> and the building of it should begin immediately following Yom Kippur.</i> so they can go from one commandment to another commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">It is a talmudic principle that one goes from <i>mitzvah</i> to <i>mitzvah</i>, from commandment to commandment. That means that when one is through completing his religious obligations in one area or a good deed, he must immediately begin fulfilling another commandment or doing another good deed. <i>Sukkot</i> is the next festival, following Yom Kippur by only five days. The concern with the festival of <i>Sukkot</i> must occupy the thoughts and the life of a pious person immediately upon the termination of Yom Kippur. Therefore the next day, if it is not the Sabbath, one should begin constructing his <i>Succah</i> (see footnote 249).</i> (<span dir="ltr">מהרי״ל ומנהגים, והגהות מיימוני פרק ב‘ דברכות, מהרי״ו</span>).<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maharil</i> and <i>Minhagim</i> and <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i>, chapter two of the section <i>Berakhot</i>, Blessings, and <i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">ומנהגים והגהות מיימוני פרק ב‘ דברכות, מהרי״ו מהרי״ל</span>.<br>For <i>Maharil</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ל</span>; see footnote 8.<br>For <i>Minhagim</i>, <span class="rtl">מנהגים</span>; see footnote 13.<br>For <i>Hagahot Maimuniyyot</i>, <span class="rtl">הגהות מיימוני</span>; see footnote 27.<br>For <i>Mahariv</i>, <span class="rtl">מהרי״ו</span>; see footnote 27.</i></small>
